# Likkutei Sichos


## ויקרא

## Sicha 2

## 7

### Sicha 2

A. The Rambam writes: "One who designates his chatas for a cheilev he ate may not bring it for a Shabbos he desecrated or for blood he consumed, for it is written, 'And he shall bring his offering, a female goat, etc., for his sin which he has sinned' — until his offering is for the sake of his sin, not that he should offer it 'from one sin for another'. And if he offered it, it is disqualified. Furthermore, they said: If he designated his chatas for a cheilev he ate yesterday, he may not bring it for a cheilev he ate today; but if he brought it, he has achieved atonement." This means that the law regarding a chatas designated for a cheilev eaten yesterday—that one should not bring it for a cheilev eaten today—is only a prohibition *lechatchilah* (by Rabbinic decree), but *bedieved*, if he brought it, he has achieved atonement.

However, there are those who have the text in the Rambam as "and if he brought it, he has *not* achieved atonement"—meaning there is no difference whether the chatas was designated for a different sin (such as cheilev for blood) or for this very sin itself (from yesterday); in any case, this is an offering "from one sin for another," and even if he brought a chatas designated for a cheilev eaten yesterday for a cheilev eaten today, he "has not achieved atonement" (even *bedieved*).

Now, on the face of it, this dispute (between the two versions) depends on the interpretation of the verse, "And he shall bring his offering, etc., for his sin which he has sinned"—for according to the version "if he brought it, he has achieved atonement," "we can well interpret it as 'his offering for his sin,' since it is for the same category [of sin]". Conversely, according to the version "if he brought it, he has not achieved atonement," the meaning of "for his sin" is that it must be "for the sake of his sin specifically"—this very sin that he committed.

But beyond the fact that this itself (the dispute over the interpretation of the verse) requires explanation, it is difficult to interpret that according to the Rambam, "we can well interpret it as 'his offering for his sin,' since it is for the same category," because the Rambam ruled in accordance with Rabbi Yochanan, who learned from this verse that when one eats two *zeisim* of cheilev in one period of unawareness, if he becomes aware of the first and then becomes aware of the second, he brings two [offerings], because "his sin which he has sinned" implies—"for each and every sin he shall bring". Thus, the phrase "his sin which he has sinned" does not mean the *category* of sin which he committed, but his sin literally—this specific sin that he committed.

Therefore, it seems to me that this distinction (between the versions) depends on the very definition of the atonement of a chatas, as follows.

## 9

[Teshuvah], which is "merely the abandonment of the sin"—the Alter Rebbe writes, "meaning that he resolves in his heart with a complete heart never to return to folly, to rebel against His kingdom, may He be blessed, and he will never again transgress the command of the King, G-d forbid, whether it be a positive commandment or a negative commandment." In other words: the resolution of the heart in teshuvah must be not only that he will not repeat this sin that he committed (as per the simple language of the Rambam, "that the sinner should abandon his sin, etc., and resolve in his heart not to do it again"), but that he will not transgress *any* commandment ("whether it be a positive commandment or a negative commandment"), and also "that he resolves with a complete heart never to return... to rebel against His kingdom, may He be blessed" (and from the simple language of the Alter Rebbe, it is implied that this is an essential requirement for the mitzvah of teshuvah as required by the Torah).

Regarding the reason for this, one can say:
In the commission of a sin (even a single sin), there are two matters: a) The evil within this specific act. b) Since every single commandment is "the command of the King," therefore, by committing a transgression, whatever it may be, the person is in the category of one who rebels against His kingdom, may He be blessed. And in order to rectify this rebellion, he must accept upon himself anew the yoke of the kingdom of Heaven (not to rebel against His kingdom, may He be blessed) and the yoke of the commandments ("he will never again transgress the command of the King, G-d forbid, whether it be a positive commandment or a negative commandment").

And with this, we can understand the reason why an *olah* offering atones for several positive commandments because it is a "gift":
The function of a "gift" is not the forgiveness of iniquity (and the nullification of the punishment), but rather that he may "see the face of the King." And this matter relates to the acceptance of the yoke of His kingdom, may He be blessed. For the forgiveness of iniquity (which exempts him from punishment) relates to the abandonment of the sin; but seeing the face of the King depends on the King agreeing to see him, for He will be appeased to accept him as a servant, etc., and this depends on the acceptance of the yoke of His kingdom, may He be blessed, which is expressed through the offering of the *olah*.

Therefore, one *olah* is effective for several positive commandments—because the appeasement of the *olah* offering is connected to the person's acceptance of the yoke of the kingdom and the yoke of the commandments, that which he accepts upon himself "never to return... to rebel against His kingdom, may He be blessed, and he will never again transgress the command of the King, G-d forbid, whether it be a positive commandment or a negative commandment." And since this acceptance includes all the commandments together as one—the appeasement for this acceptance is not related or tied to the particularity of a sin, or to each sin individually.

Conversely, the atonement (of a *chatas*) is the atonement of the iniquity, which (simply put) relates to the specific sin he committed, and therefore a separate *chatas* is required for each and every sin.

C. However, even the atonement and forgiveness for a sin contains two matters:
a) The abandonment of the act of this sin, meaning "that the sinner should abandon his sin... and resolve in his heart not to do it again"—for when the Holy One, blessed be He, sees that the person truly accepts upon himself "not to do it again" (and specifically in a manner where "the Knower of secrets will testify about him that he will never return to this sin forever"), He atones for his iniquity and forgives his punishment.
b) Regret over the past (confession of words), through which he effects the uprooting (nullification) of the evil that...

## Sicha 3

## 13

A. The Rambam writes in *Hilchos Pesulei HaMukdashin* (after explaining the various scenarios of *zevachim* that have become mixed and the details of their laws): "All *kodshim* can become mixed with their own kind, except for a *chatas* with an *asham*, for the *asham* is only from male sheep, and there is no *chatas* from sheep except for a female." His source is the Mishnah in *Zevachim*: "Everything can become mixed, except for the *chatas* and the *asham*."

The commentators raise a difficulty: What is the necessity of informing us that "everything can become mixed, except for the *chatas* and the *asham*"? After all, the fact that an *asham* and a *chatas* cannot become mixed is a matter of physical necessity, since one is a male and the other is a female. If so, why does the Tanna (and similarly, the Rambam) need to inform us of a physical reality?

Some have suggested that it is stated this way (in the Mishnah) so that one would not ask why the law of a mixed *chatas* and *asham* is not taught, just as the law of other mixtures is taught.

But on the face of it, this is forced. Since it is physically impossible for them to mix, there is no room from the outset for the question, "Why is a law not taught for something that is not possible?" (Are we dealing with fools?)

Furthermore, even if you were to say (with great difficulty) that this is the way of the Mishnah—to clarify even such a question (why a specific law is not taught, etc., even when that law has no place in reality)—it is understood that it is impossible to explain the words of the Rambam in this way, for his book is nothing but "laws, laws".

There is another explanation among the commentators: that this itself is what it comes to teach us—that the requirement of a female sheep written regarding a *chatas* is an essential requirement (*le'ikuva*).

But this explanation is also difficult to accept, because: (a) Why inform us that the female sheep written regarding a *chatas* is an essential requirement within the law of mixtures, rather than stating it plainly: that one who offers a male for his *chatas* has not fulfilled his obligation? (b) And most importantly: the fact that the female sheep written regarding a *chatas* is an essential requirement is a simple matter, as is evident from the discussion in the Gemara only regarding one who designates a female for his *olah*—whether *kedusha* takes effect upon it. (c) The Rambam has already ruled (in the preceding chapters) that a commoner who designates a male for a *chatas* does not sanctify it (neither with *kedushas haguf* nor *kedushas damim*).

B. Now, in the Gemara there it is stated: "What is different about a *chatas* and an *asham* [that they cannot mix]? One is a male and one is a female. But a *chatas* and an *olah* also [have this difference—one is a male and one is a female; there is the *se'ir* of the *nasi* (which is a male), a *chatas* and an *asham*...]"

## 15

...the inner dimension of Torah—to teach that the two parts of Torah are not separate parts, Heaven forbid, but rather both are parts of "one Torah," to the point that each one completes the other.

And if these things are true regarding all matters of Torah, then all the more so regarding *korbanos*. It is known that the content of the matter of *korbanos* is (not so much the act of offering, but rather) the thought and intent of the person before and during the offering [which is also one of the interpretations of the word *korban*—that the person draws himself close to Hashem; and as is hinted at also at the beginning of the *parsha* of *korbanos*: "A man who will offer from among you a *korban* to Hashem," that the offering must be "from you," from the person himself]—meaning, that together with the "laws of *korbanos*" regarding the act of offering, there are "laws of *korbanos*" in relation to the offering of the person.

Based on this, we can say regarding our subject: The novelty in the law (in the Mishnah and Rambam) that a *chatas* and an *asham* cannot become mixed is not (so much) regarding the act of offering (in the physical sense)—where it is physically impossible for the *chatas* to mix with the *asham*—but rather regarding the "laws of *chatas* and *asham*" in the duties of the heart and their spiritual content. In that realm, it is possible that there could be a mixture of the service of *chatas* (in spirituality) and the service of *asham* (in spirituality). The Tanna teaches us that since one is a male and one is a female, therefore, even in spirituality, it is impossible for them to mix.

[And even though the Rambam wrote (at the end of *Sefer Avodah*) that "all the *korbanos* are from the category of *chukim*," yet at the end of *Sefer Korbanos* he wrote, "Even though all the *chukim* of the Torah are decrees, it is appropriate to contemplate them, and whatever you can provide a reason for, provide a reason for". It is understood from this that even the fact that an *asham* and a *chatas* cannot mix because an *asham* comes from males and a *chatas* comes from females—even though it is in the category of *chukim*—it is appropriate to contemplate it and provide a reason for it, and in the words of the Rambam there—a study to "correct the opinions and straighten all the deeds."]

D. And it is possible to say the explanation for this:

The general difference between a *chatas* and an *asham* is that an *asham* is more severe than a *chatas*, to the point that many *ashamos* come even for intentional sins, whereas a *chatas* comes only for an unintentional sin, as the Ramban elaborated in his commentary on the Torah in explaining...


## ניסן

## 19

### Nissan

A. From Rosh Chodesh Nissan until the twelfth of the month, "the custom is to read the portion of the *Nasi* (Prince) who brought his offering on that day". For this reason, the custom is not to say *Tachanun* during these days, because "on the first of Nissan (the day the *Mishkan* was erected in the desert), the *Nesi'im* began to bring their offerings for the dedication of the *Mizbeach*, one *Nasi* per day until the thirteenth of Nissan, and for every *Nasi*, the day he brought his offering was his *Yom Tov*." (And since, together with the days of *Pesach* that follow, "the majority of the month is spent in holiness", *Tachanun* is not said during the entire month of Nissan).

This requires understanding:
Although all matters (and narratives) of the Torah are eternal, we do not find that a *zecher* (remembrance) for the generations was established in the order of *davening* (and the like) for every event in the Torah. It was only established for specific matters—those that are general and fundamental, such as "a remembrance of the Exodus from Egypt," "a remembrance of the *Mikdash*," and the like [and even regarding the "*Mikdash*," we do not create a *zecher* for every service performed in the *Mikdash* (even services that were performed many times)].

Why, then, do we create a *zecher* for the generations for the offerings of the *Nesi'im* for the dedication of the *Mizbeach*—which were (a) one-time events, and (b) in the *Mishkan* (meaning, they preceded in time not only this last exile, but also the Second *Mikdash*, the Babylonian exile, and the First *Mikdash*)!

And within this itself, there is a double *zecher*: (a) the recitation of the *Parshas HaNasi*, (b) the non-recitation of *Tachanun* (extending for an entire month)!

## 20

B. We also find a novelty in the manner of this *zecher*:
After reciting the "*Nasi*," the custom is to say the "*Yehi Ratzon*". Its text is: "May it be Your will, Hashem my G-d and G-d of my fathers, that if I, Your servant, am from the tribe of (so-and-so) whose *Parshah* of the *Nasi* I have read in Your Torah today, may all the *nitzotzin kadishin* (holy sparks) and all the holy lights included in the holiness of this tribe shine upon me, to understand and to comprehend Your Torah and Your *yirah*, to do Your will all the days of my life, I, my children, and my children's children, from now and forever, Amen."

In other words, every single person from *Klal Yisrael* (not only select individuals) prays that "all the *nitzotzin kadishin*... included in the holiness of this tribe shine upon me," and furthermore—that this illumination should not remain in concealment and hiding, but should influence his *avodah* in practice, "to understand and to comprehend Your Torah and Your *yirah*, to do Your will (and furthermore) all the days of my life (and furthermore—) I, my children, and my children's children (and in a manner of) from now and forever!"

Moreover: since we do not pray for miraculous occurrences, it must be that this prayer (of every person from *Klal Yisrael*) can be fulfilled through the natural order, in the double and redoubled darkness of the time of exile! Furthermore, in general, the prayer of *Yisrael* is fulfilled in practice, especially a prayer by the many, as it is written, "Behold, G-d is mighty and does not despise." And especially since we conclude the "*Yehi Ratzon*" by saying "Amen" (from the word *emes*—truth), which gives special strength to the blessing and effects the completion of the "*nitzachon*" (victory).

Thus, it turns out that the *zecher* for the generations for the offerings of the *Nesi'im* "for the dedication of the *Mizbeach*" (through the recitation of *Parshas HaNesi'im*) is not merely a *zecher* (for an event that occurred in the past), but is an eternal action, to the point that "all the *nitzotzin kadishin* and all the holy lights included in the holiness of this tribe" shine in practice (as above) "to understand and to comprehend Your Torah and Your *yirah*, to do Your will all the days of my life, I, my children, and my children's children, from now and forever!"

This is a novelty even regarding matters of "*zecher*" that are *midivrei sofrim*. For although all actions and prayers, etc., that come for the sake of a *zecher* for a specific thing ("a remembrance of the Exodus from Egypt" and the like, as above) are not merely a *zecher* in name only, but rather through this *zecher* something akin to the thing remembered is "created"—nevertheless, we do not find such an action in the prayers (and matters of "*zikaron*") of the entire year.

C. Furthermore—and to preface with the question:
How is it possible that every person from *Klal Yisrael* should recite the *Parshah* of each of the twelve *Nesi'im* with the "*Yehi Ratzon*" (that the lights, etc., of the holiness of that tribe should shine upon him)—when every person from

## 21

*Yisrael* is only from one tribe [as is understood from the "*Yehi Ratzon*" itself, which is in the language of doubt—"if I, Your servant, am from the tribe of (so-and-so), etc.", because it is possible he is not from that tribe], and it turns out that out of the twelve times the *Nesi'im* are recited, in eleven of them they are saying a prayer that does not pertain to the one praying!

It must be that every person from *Klal Yisrael* (is potentially) included in all the rest of *Yisrael* (the tribes), to the point that every person from *Klal Yisrael* can potentially receive the lights of all the tribes. In that case, the recitation of the *Nasi* and the "*Yehi Ratzon*" can be with certainty—that "all the *nitzotzin*, etc., and all the lights, etc., included in the holiness of this tribe, etc., should shine upon me," since it is possible that he has a connection to all the tribes.

This is similar to the instruction of the Rebbe (the Rashab), *n.e.*, that even a *Kohen* and a *Levi* should recite the *Nasi* and the "*Yehi Ratzon*" every day (even though it is certain that they are from the tribe of *Levi*)—"because this relates to *ibbur* (impregnation/inclusion)," and from the perspective of "*ibbur*", it is possible to have a connection to every tribe and to all the tribes.

Based on this, since every person from *Klal Yisrael* recites the *Parshiyos* of all the *Nesi'im* and the "*Yehi Ratzon*" of each one—and Hashem fulfills these requests—then upon the completion of the recitation of the *Nesi'im* (on the twelfth of Nissan), "all the *nitzotzin kadishin* and all the holy lights included" in every single tribe shine within every person from *Klal Yisrael*—in which are included all the sparks of holiness in the entire world (—which was created "for the sake of *Yisrael*"), and as is known that the twelve tribes include all possible modes of *avodah* (twelve general modes).

From this, the magnitude of the action of reciting the *Nesi'im* is understood, in "understanding and comprehending Your Torah and Your *yirah*, to do Your will, etc."—since it comes through all the "*nitzotzin kadishin*" of all the tribes.

## 22

D. One must explain the reason for this novelty regarding the offerings of the *Nesi'im* and the recitation in their *zecher*:
The dedication of the *Mizbeach* is the foundation and the beginning of the *avodah* of all *Yisrael* throughout all the generations. And as is known, the dedication of the *Mizbeach* is a matter like the matter of *chinuch* (education/inauguration); just as *chinuch* establishes the foundation for all the *avodah* of the one being educated throughout all the days of his life, so too, through the dedication of the *Mizbeach*, the foundation was established for the *avodah* of the *Mishkan*, which is the purpose of all human *avodah* (and throughout all the generations)—"And they shall make for Me a *Mikdash*, and I will dwell among them".

For this reason, (a) all the tribes participated in the dedication of the *Mizbeach*—because in them are included all the (particular) types of *avodas Hashem* (as above), (b) the offerings for the dedication of the *Mizbeach* were brought specifically by the *Nesi'im*—"for the *Nasi* is the whole" (his entire tribe, and through this—all the tribes (*Bnei Yisrael*) in their entirety).

Based on this, the reason we create a *zecher* for the generations for the offerings of the *Nesi'im* (through the recitation of *Parshas HaNesi'im* and the non-recitation of *Tachanun*) is understood—even though their offering was a one-time event, thousands of years ago, and in a specific place and time—because the offerings of the *Nesi'im* for the dedication of the *Mizbeach* were an eternal matter for all generations, a matter that continues and influences in practice the *avodah* of every person from *Klal Yisrael*, including in the double and redoubled darkness of the exile—that "all the *nitzotzin*, etc., to understand, etc., all the days of my life, etc., from now and forever" shine.

E. Based on the above (that the offerings of the *Nesi'im* are an eternal matter that continues in everyone in all generations), it is understood that among the unique matters of the month of Nissan is the emphasis on the matter of (a) the eternity of the *Nesi'im* of *Yisrael*, for every *Nasi* in his generation is like the first *Nasi* in his generation, (b) the *hiskashrus*, etc., of every person from

## 23

*Klal Yisrael* (in every generation) to the *Nesi'im* of *Yisrael*, which continues from the *hiskashrus*, etc., that existed at the time of the dedication of the *Mishkan*—an eternal *hiskashrus*, etc.

And it can be said that the connection (of eternity) of the *Nesi'im* of *Yisrael* to the month of Nissan is emphasized most strongly regarding the *Nasi* of our generation, the Rebbe, *k.m.h.a.*:
The second of Nissan is the *yom hilula* of the *Nasi*, the Rebbe (the Rashab), *n.e.*, and the day of the beginning of the *nesi'us* of (his son, his successor) the Rebbe, the *Nasi* of our generation.

And in both, we find the matter of eternity (also) in the physicality of the world, and the eternal *hiskashrus* of every one of them to all of *Yisrael* (and likewise—of all *Yisrael* to them):
The words of the Rebbe (the Rashab), *n.e.*, before his passing are known—"I am going to heaven, the *kesavim* (writings) I leave for you."

The meaning of these words: "The *tzaddikim* are like their Creator", and just as Hashem "gave (inserted) Himself" into His Torah, as the Sages say, "I wrote and gave My soul," so too with the *tzaddikim*, who "give" (and insert) themselves into their words of Torah.

This is the intent in the words of the Rebbe, *n.e.*, "I am going to heaven, the *kesavim* I leave for you"—that through (studying) his *kesavim* (his Torah), where the study leads to action in practice and even to spreading them outward, one connects, etc., with him himself (since he inserted himself into his *kesavim*), himself—as he is found in the state of ascending to heaven.

And since the "*kesavim*" are found below in the physicality of the world—then through them, the Rebbe, *n.e.*, is found in the physicality of the world; meaning, this is an eternal *hiskashrus* in physicality between the *Nasi*, the Rebbe, *n.e.*, and all of *Yisrael*.

And so it is regarding his son, his successor, the *Nasi* of our generation—that through his *kesavim*, his *sefarim*, and his possessions,

## 24

even the physical ones (as follows)—one connects with him himself and in physicality.

F. And it can be said that the Rebbe, the *Nasi* of our generation, emphasized this immediately in his first *maamar* (his Torah) (as *Nasi*)—from which it is understood that this is a beginning, a principle, and a foundation in his *nesi'us*—the *d.h.* "The beginning of nations is Amalek, and its end, etc.":

In that *maamar*, he explains that matters of holiness exist in a "self-sustaining (and eternal) existence," just as "the *mitzvos* that were clothed in physical matters, the parchment of the physical *tefillin* and the wool of the *tzitzis* and the like, and everything that is in His will, may He be blessed, are vessels suitable for the illumination of the supernal light, and they exist in a state of self-sustaining existence," and he continues, "and it can be said that this is the concept of 'holiness does not move from its place', and *tashmishei kedusha* stand in their holiness, etc."

In other words: since all matters of holiness are drawn from the source of holiness—Hashem Himself, therefore, just as He, may He be blessed, exists in a self-sustaining (and eternal) existence (for His existence is from His essence, etc.), so too His holiness and His light (which is attached to the *Ma'or*—the Luminary—and is akin to the *Ma'or*)—exists in a self-sustaining and eternal existence.

And in continuation of this, he writes there:
"And the place of the Torah and *avodah* of a *tzaddik* in his holiness remains even after his ascent from the life of the body to the true life, and the illumination of the light of his *avodah* remains in its place, for it can be said that the place where he studied and occupied himself with Torah, and all the vessels he used for the needs of the *avodah*, have his holiness upon them from the parts of his *birur* (refinement) in the world."

And he brings for this (an explanation—) a story from his father: "And I saw once that the Rebbe, the holy one, *k.m.*, entered his father's room, the Rebbe, *z.t.z.l.k.h.z.y.a.*, and the arrangements of the room were still as they were in his lifetime (this was approximately in the year [56]45 or [56]46) and he entered dressed in his *gartel* and stood by the table opposite the chair where he sat, and his lips moved as if speaking, and he wept much."

The explanation of this is understood most clearly according to what is discussed and explained in the *maamar* there—that the aforementioned matter (regarding the eternity of the place of his *avodah*, etc., of the *tzaddik*) comes in continuation of what is explained there "

## 25

regarding the vessels which, through their attachment and the unification of the light within them, are also in a state of self-sustaining existence" (like the light), from which it is understood that the eternal existence of the holiness of the physical place and all the vessels he used (the *tzaddik*)—for "the needs of the *avodah*"—is in a manner and akin to the self-sustaining (and eternal) existence of the vessels of the *Eser Sefiros* (due to their *bitul* and unification with the light).

And it can be said even more—that this is (not only akin to, but) the very same matter, meaning that the eternal existence that is "in the place of Torah and *avodah* of a *tzaddik*, etc., and all the vessels he used, etc." is at the same level of eternal existence of the vessels of the *Eser Sefiros* (of the world of *Atzilus*). And it can be said that this is also understood from what is written in *Tanya* in explanation of the Sages, "The Patriarchs are the *Merkavah* (Chariot)," that in all their matters they were a *Merkavah* of *Atzilus*, and so it is with *tzaddikim* and *Nesi'im* who are akin to them.

G. The conclusion from all the above—that the life of the *tzaddik* is eternal, and not only in the spirituality of matters but also—regarding the physical place and his physical vessels (his table, his chair, etc.)—and according to the precision of the language "holiness does not move from its place", the physical place in "his father's room" (in addition to the spiritual place—a level and degree)—because the holiness of the *tzaddik* permeates all his matters (also in the place and in the vessels he used for the needs of the *avodah*); and as the *maamar* says, "the place where he studied and occupied himself with Torah, and all the vessels he used for the needs of the *avodah*, have his holiness upon them."

And even more: the holiness of the place and the vessels, etc., of the *tzaddik* (even after his passing) is in the same manner as the time when the *tzaddik* used them in practice, soul in body; and as is understood from the aforementioned story—that "he entered his father's room... and the arrangements of the room were still as they were in his lifetime, etc., and he entered dressed in his *gartel* and stood by the table opposite the chair where he sat, and his lips moved as if speaking, and he wept much"—meaning "as it was in his lifetime" when he would enter for *yechidus* [which can be said to be its matter—the unification of the *yechidah* of the one entering for *yechidus* and the *yechidah* of the Rebbe]—in the lifetime of the Rebbe Maharash!

And according to the aforementioned saying of the Rebbe, *n.e.*, "I am going to heaven, the *kesavim* I leave for you"—it is understood that through *hiskashrus* to the *Nasi* of the generation—through "hiskashrus" with his *kesavim*, his *sefarim*, etc., in which he inserted himself as above—one draws from the self-sustaining and eternal existence of the holiness of the *tzaddik* and the *Nasi*, to all those who walk "in the way

## 26

of righteousness that he taught us from his ways, and we shall walk in his paths, forever and ever".

And especially through studying the *kesavim* and the *sefarim*, and bringing the study into action in practice, both within oneself and—through spreading the wellsprings (of the *kesavim* and the *sefarim*) outward.

And through this, "all the *nitzotzin kadishin* and all the holy lights included" in the *Nasi* of the generation shine within him—who includes "everything," for the *Nasi* is the whole, and this effects "to understand and to comprehend Your Torah [including and especially in this case—the *kesavim* and *sefarim* of the *Nasi*] and Your *yirah*, to do Your will all the days of my life, I, my children, and my children's children, from now and forever (eternal)."

H. The instruction regarding practice:
We must connect with the *Nesi'im* of *Yisrael* in every generation, and in our generation—with the *Nasi* of our generation, the Rebbe, *k.m.h.a.*, and through this we receive from the "eternity" of the *Nasi*.

And this effects "to understand and to comprehend Your Torah"—the revealed part of Torah and the inner part of Torah, including the Torah of our Rebbes, the *Nesi'im*, and "great is study that leads to action"—to do the will of Hashem all the days of my life—the fulfillment of *mitzvos* in practice,

And especially the fulfillment of the *mitzvos* and the instructions in matters that are timely: the "Pesach Campaign," to prepare himself, his brothers of *Bnei Yisrael*, and all of *Klal Yisrael*—for the festival of *Pesach*, to strive and to ensure that every person from *Klal Yisrael* has all the needs of the festival, both in physicality, and (and primarily—the action that one shall do)—his spiritual needs, the knowledge of the *halachos* of *Pesach* and the *mitzvos* of the festival, starting from the beginning (the *halachos* of *Erev Pesach*) and the *Seder* night.

I. All the above relates especially to the matter that was discussed recently regarding "And they shall make for Me a *Mikdash*, and I will dwell among them"—that the power for the *avodos* throughout all the days of the *Mishkan* came from the dedication of the *Mizbeach* through the offerings of the *Nesi'im* (as in section D)—and "any place where it is said 'for Me,' it does not move forever",

That all of *Yisrael*, men, women, and children, should make their home and their room, etc.—akin to a "*Mikdash Me'at*" (a small sanctuary), a house of Torah, prayer, and *gemilas chasadim* (charity), until the physical house and its physical vessels (the table, chair, and the like) become vessels for holiness (Torah, prayer, and charity), that it should be a "house full of *sefarim*", etc.,

And as is emphasized also on the festival of *Pesach*—"which passed over the houses of the children of *Yisrael*."

And special attention regarding the education of children, who are among the primary participants in the *Seder* of *Pesach* (to the point that we do several things for them even before *Pesach*, and we make changes so that the children will not sleep and so that the children will ask), including also—that every boy and girl should have (—their own) a *Siddur*, a *Chumash*, and (to distinguish) a *tzedakah* box, and also—a "*Haggadah Shel Pesach*" (with pictures),

## 27

through which he makes his room, his table, and his bed, etc.—a "*Mikdash Me'at*," a vessel for holiness, and (as above) an eternal existence is drawn into the physical room and the physical vessels and the entire house, because "holiness does not move from its place."

And one enters there "dressed in his *gartel*" and stands "by the table," in a manner of "know before Whom you are standing", and one speaks, with tears of joy, as a son speaking to his father, an only son to elderly parents, *Yisrael* ("You are children to Hashem your G-d") to our Father in heaven, which causes great *taanug* (pleasure) to the Father, and the *taanug* of the Creator becomes the *taanug* of the created.

Until this comes to the ultimate revelation and its perfection in the "Great *Mikdash*," the Third *Beis HaMikdash*, and there—in the Holy of Holies, the *yechidah* of the place (of the world), where the *yechidah* of every soul from *Yisrael* connects with the *yechidah* of Above, the Unique One of the world,

In the true and complete Redemption through *Mashiach Tzidkeinu*—the general *yechidah*, immediately and truly.

(From the *sicha* of *Shabbos Parshas Vayikra*, 5 Nissan, 5747)


## צו

## 28

### Tzav

1. In our parsha, regarding the verse "And he offered it upon the altar," Rashi writes: "Moshe served during all seven days of the inauguration in a white tunic."

Seemingly, Rashi’s intent is to explain the matter of Moshe performing all these services, clarifying that this was because he served in the kehunah throughout the seven days of the inauguration, as is stated in the commentaries. However, according to this, it requires explanation why Rashi interprets only the detail (the secondary aspect) of which garments he wore, and does not mention the essence and the general fact that he served in the kehunah throughout all seven days, as is stated in the Toras Kohanim : "During all seven days of the inauguration, Moshe served in the kehunah gedolah."

[This detail, that he served "in a white tunic," is brought in the Gemara to resolve a different question — "In what did Moshe serve during the seven days of the inauguration?". But on the face of it, Rashi’s primary intent is to explain the essence of the matter: that he performed all the services throughout all seven days of the inauguration.]

Seemingly, one could say that the reason Rashi deviated from the language of the Toras Kohanim—"During all seven days of the inauguration, Moshe served in the kehunah (gedolah)"—and wrote instead "he served in a white tunic," is because, in Rashi’s view in his commentary on the Torah, Moshe did not serve as a Kohen (even during the seven days of the inauguration), and his entire service was merely an *hora'as sha'ah* (a temporary ruling) by Divine command.

For this service of Moshe during the seven days of the inauguration can be interpreted in two ways: a) That during the seven days of the inauguration, Moshe was a (Gadol) Kohen, but by Divine command, he did not serve in the garments of the kehunah gedolah, but rather in a special (white) garment. b) That there was no status of (gedolah) kehunah here at all, and it was nothing more than an *hora'as sha'ah*.

Regarding Rashi’s opinion in his commentary on the Torah—whether Moshe served in the kehunah at all—in his commentary on the Torah (in Parshas Shemos, on the verse "And His anger flared"), he brings: "Rabbi Yehuda ben Korcha says: Every instance of 'flaring anger' in the Torah is marked with a lasting impression, but this one is not marked with a lasting impression... Rabbi Yosi said to him: Even here it is marked with a lasting impression: 'Is not Aharon your brother the Levite?'—who was destined to be a Levite and not a Kohen, and I would have said the kehunah would come from you; from now on it shall not be so, but he shall be the Kohen and you the Levite, as it is said : 'And Moshe the man of Elokus, his sons are named after the tribe of Levi.'"

Based on this, according to both opinions, Moshe was not a Kohen—according to Rabbi Yehuda ben Korcha, he was not destined to be a Kohen from the outset (and therefore one cannot say that this "flaring anger" left a lasting impression), and according to Rabbi Yosi, even if the kehunah was supposed to come from Moshe, it was taken from him.

Therefore, Rashi did not write here that during all seven days of the inauguration Moshe served in the (gedolah) kehunah, because in Rashi’s view, his service did not fall under the category of (gedolah) kehunah, but was merely an *hora'as sha'ah* as an emissary of Hashem to train Aharon and his sons for the service of the Mishkan.

## 29

2. Based on this, we can easily understand what Rashi explains in Parshas Emor regarding the verse "He shall eat the bread of his Elokus, of the most holy and of the holy": "And of the holy he shall eat"—"These are the *kodshim kalim*. And if the *kodshei kodashim* were mentioned, why were the *kodshim kalim* mentioned? If it were not mentioned, I would say that he may eat *kodshei kodashim* even if he has a blemish, for we find that they were permitted to a *zar* (non-Kohen), as Moshe ate the meat of the inauguration, etc. (and he concludes) so it is explained in Zevachim".

The commentators asked: Why does Rashi state that the permission for a *zar* to eat *kodshei kodashim* was through Moshe’s eating during the seven days of the inauguration? After all, the fact that "so it is explained in Zevachim" is only according to the initial assumption (*hava amina*) there in the Gemara, which interprets the *beraisa* that way and challenges the opinion of Rav, who holds that "Moshe Rabbeinu was a Kohen Gadol and partook of the *kodshei shamayim*, as it is said : 'From the ram of the inauguration, it was Moshe’s portion'"; but Rav Sheshes answered that the meaning of "*kodshei kodashim*... were permitted to a *zar*" refers to the fact that they were permitted "on a *bamah* to a *zar*."

Rather, Rashi’s opinion is that Moshe’s service during the seven days of the inauguration was not due to the law of kehunah, but was merely a matter of "service," and therefore his eating from the meat of the ram of the inauguration is considered as a *zar* eating.

It can be said that he follows his own reasoning, for even in his commentary on the Shas, Rashi holds that Moshe’s work during the seven days of the inauguration is not considered service "in the kehunah":

In the Gemara, Zevachim there, it concludes: "It is a dispute (whether Moshe was a Kohen)... 'And Hashem’s anger flared against Moshe.' Rabbi Yehuda ben Korcha says: Every instance of 'flaring anger' in the Torah is marked with a lasting impression, but this one is not marked with a lasting impression. Rabbi Shimon ben Yochai says: Even this is marked with a lasting impression, as it is said: 'Is not Aharon your brother the Levite?' And is he not a Kohen? This is what it means: I said you would be the Kohen and he the Levite; now he is the Kohen and you are the Levite. And the Sages say: Moshe was only 'invested' as a Kohen during the seven days of the inauguration alone. And others say: The kehunah was only withheld from the descendants of Moshe, as it is said : 'And Moshe the man of Elokus, his sons are named after the tribe of Levi,' and it says : 'Moshe and Aharon among His Kohanim, and Shmuel among those who call His name.'"

And Rashi explains in the entry "And others say": "The kehunah was only withheld from his descendants, but he himself was a Kohen all his days, and Rav holds like the 'others say'."

This requires explanation: After all, Rav’s proof that "Moshe Rabbeinu was a Kohen Gadol and partook of the *kodshei shamayim*" is from the verse "From the ram of the inauguration, it was Moshe’s portion," which was said during the seven days of the inauguration. If so, why did Rashi need to say "Rav holds like the 'others say'" (who hold that Moshe was a Kohen all his days)? He could have posited that he also holds like the Sages, that "he was only 'invested' as a Kohen... during the seven days of the inauguration," meaning that during those seven days he was a Kohen Gadol, and therefore he partook of the *kodshei shamayim* from the ram of the inauguration (and this is Rav’s intent when he says Moshe Rabbeinu was a Kohen).

However, based on the above, one can say that in Rashi’s view, if you say that Moshe was only "invested" as a Kohen for the seven days of the inauguration alone, like the Sages, then this is not the status of kehunah, but merely a matter of service as an *hora'as sha'ah* (and the term "invested" is a borrowed term. And this is somewhat forced). Therefore, one cannot posit that Rav holds like the Sages, because Rav clearly states that "Moshe Rabbeinu was a Kohen Gadol," not that he merely served in the kehunah (and the like). Thus, it must be that he holds like the "others say" that "the kehunah was only withheld from his descendants (of Moshe), but he himself was a Kohen all his days."

And one should also note the precision in Rashi’s language in Maseches Avodah Zarah : Regarding the Shas’s question there, "In what did Moshe serve during all seven days of the inauguration?", Rashi wrote: "He did not serve in the garments of the kehunah, for it is written : 'And you shall make holy garments for Aharon your brother,' and he was a *zar* regarding them, and by Divine command he served, and the garments were not mentioned." From Rashi’s language ("and the garments were not mentioned," rather than "without garments" and the like), it is implied that the fact that he served by Divine command is not (only) to explain that for this reason he could serve without the garments of the kehunah, but to emphasize that the service itself was by Divine command, and that there was no status of kehunah in it.

## 30

3. But it is forced to interpret Rashi’s position in his commentary on the Torah this way, because:
a) In Parshas Tetzaveh, regarding the command concerning the waving of the ram of the inauguration, Rashi explains : "Both are occupied with the waving, the owners and the Kohen. How so? The Kohen places his hand under the hand of the owner and waves, and in this, Aharon and his sons were the owners and Moshe was the Kohen". Thus, according to Rashi, Moshe was the Kohen.

b) In Parshas Pekudei, Rashi wrote: "On the eighth day of the inauguration, all were made equal in the kehunah, and the Targum is: 'And they were sanctified from it on that day, Moshe sanctified with them'." This implies that Moshe’s kehunah was equal to Aharon’s kehunah (at least on the eighth day of the inauguration).

c) In the aforementioned Rashi to Parshas Shemos regarding the dispute between Rabbi Yehuda ben Korcha and Rabbi Yosi as to whether that flaring anger left a lasting impression—from the fact that Rashi brings in the words of Rabbi Yosi the proof from the verse "his sons are named after the tribe of Levi," it implies that his intent is that Moshe himself was a Kohen all the days of his life, and only from Moshe’s descendants did the kehunah cease, and this is the lasting impression of the flaring anger.

[And perhaps even in the opinion of Rabbi Yehuda ben Korcha, it can be explained that from the outset the kehunah was given only to him alone and not to his descendants (but this is a great novelty—and he should have explained it), and therefore it is not considered a lasting impression of the flaring anger. Furthermore, it is explicitly stated in many places in the Midrashim in the name of Rabbi Yehuda ben Korcha that Moshe served in the kehunah gedolah for all forty years, and there is no contradiction to the simple meaning of the Torah.]

Therefore, it seems more likely that according to Rashi’s view in the simple meaning of the Torah, he was a "Kohen", and what Rashi wrote, "Moshe served during all seven days of the inauguration in a white tunic" (and likewise he did not write "in the kehunah gedolah"), is because in his opinion, for all thirty-seven years Moshe was a Kohen Gadol, but during the seven days of the inauguration, he served in a white tunic, and not like Aharon and his sons who served in the garments of the kehunah.

## 31

4. But according to this, one must understand from the other side: After all, the garments of the kehunah are not an additional thing in a Kohen, but as Rashi explains on the verse "To sanctify him to serve Me as a Kohen"—"To sanctify him, to bring him into the kehunah through the garments, that he shall be a Kohen to Me", and according to the Halacha : "When their garments are upon them, their kehunah is upon them; when their garments are not upon them, their kehunah is not upon them, and they are like *zarim*."

And during the seven days of the inauguration, when there was not yet a fully established Mishkan, as its primary erection and the service in it were from the eighth day of the inauguration (Rosh Chodesh Nissan) and onwards, one could perhaps force the point that then even a white tunic could be a garment suitable for the Mishkan of that time. But how is it possible to say that for all thirty-seven years, that Moshe was a Kohen Gadol, it was without the garments of the kehunah?!

And indeed, in Tosafos they wrote: "According to the one who says... Moshe Rabbeinu, peace be upon him, was a Kohen Gadol and served in the kehunah gedolah for forty years... the term 'kehunah gedolah' implies eight garments, like a Kohen Gadol."

5. This can be explained by prefacing the words of the Toras Kohanim in Parshas Acharei on the verse "Speak to Aharon your brother, and he shall not come at any time into the holy"—"Your brother shall not come, but Moshe may come." The Raavad explains: "And Moshe is not included in 'he shall not come at any time,' for Moshe was always entering before and within to hear the speech from between the two cherubim". And we do not find that Moshe needed to wear the garments of the kehunah in order to enter before and within "to hear the speech".

The explanation for this is:
In the Mishkan, we find two matters: a) As a Sanctuary—"A house for Hashem, prepared for the sacrifices to be offered in it", the place of the service of the Kohanim. b) "And I will meet with you there, and I will speak with you from above the cover, from between the two cherubim that are upon the Ark of the Testimony, of all that I will command you concerning the children of Israel", and this is the "Ohel Moed," a place designated for speech with Moshe.

Now, the first matter was innovated in the Mishkan, for then they were commanded "And they shall make Me a sanctuary," to make a Mishkan and a sanctuary—a house for Hashem, not like it was before this, when they would sacrifice on a *bamah* in every place; whereas the second matter, that "I will meet with you there" (a place designated for Hashem’s speech), already existed before the erection of the Mishkan, in Moshe’s tent, as it is written : "And Moshe would take the tent and pitch it outside the camp, far from the camp, and he called it Ohel Moed, and it would be that everyone who sought Hashem would go out to the Ohel Moed," "And it would be, when Moshe entered the tent, the pillar of cloud would descend and stand at the entrance of the tent, and He would speak with Moshe," "And Hashem would speak to Moshe face to face, etc., and he would return to the camp." This means that the "Ohel Moed" existed before the erection of the Mishkan, in "Moshe’s tent," in which the Holy One, blessed be He, would speak with Moshe and teach him Torah. However, "this matter was practiced from Yom Kippur until the Mishkan was erected and no more... and once it (the Mishkan) was erected, He did not speak with him again except from the Ohel Moed".

Now, Moshe’s essence is that through him the word of Hashem (the Torah) is transmitted to Israel, and he is always prepared for the speech of Hashem. If so, the primary essence of the Mishkan in relation to Moshe is that it is the "Ohel Moed," "And I will meet with you there." Therefore, it can be said that just as it was in Moshe’s tent, where he did not need special garments to hear "all that I will command you," so it is in the Ohel Moed in the Mishkan: the entry for the purpose of hearing the word of Hashem does not require special garments, the garments of a Kohen (Kohen Gadol), because this is his essence.

## 34

Whether Moshe was a Kohen Gadol for all forty years is only if this was a separate matter, or if it was part of his essence as the receiver of the Torah from the Holy One, blessed be He:

According to the "others" who say that the kehunah was only withheld from Moshe’s descendants, he possessed the holiness of a Kohen Gadol as a separate matter. Therefore, Rav, who says "Moshe Rabbeinu was a Kohen Gadol and partook of the *kodshei shamayim*," must hold like the "others" that he possessed the holiness of a Kohen Gadol as a separate matter. Consequently, if Moshe wanted to enter to serve and perform the work (of a Kohen Gadol), he had to wear the garments of the kehunah, as stated in the aforementioned Tosafos, that according to this opinion in Zevachim, that Moshe Rabbeinu, peace be upon him, was a Kohen Gadol and served in the kehunah gedolah for forty years, it was in the eight garments of the Kohen Gadol, as stated above.

But according to the opinion that holds that Moshe was only "invested" as a Kohen during the seven days of the inauguration alone, the meaning is that only then did he serve in the kehunah in practice. But for all forty years, the kehunah within him was part of his essence as the receiver of the Torah from the mouth of Hashem. Consequently, even if he entered for the purpose of service, he did not need to wear the garments of the kehunah, because he was always prepared to hear the word of Hashem, and thus his service as a Kohen is not a separate matter, but part of the "I will meet with you" in the Mishkan.

Based on this, it can be said that according to Rashi’s view in his commentary on the Torah, Moshe never separated from his status as the receiver of the Torah from the time of the Giving of the Torah at Mount Sinai. Even during the seven days of the inauguration when Moshe served, it was a detail within his general essence: receiving the word of Hashem from the Holy One, blessed be He. Therefore, Rashi wrote that "Moshe served during all seven days of the inauguration in a white tunic," and not like the Toras Kohanim that he served in the kehunah gedolah (even though he already mentioned before this that regarding the service during the seven days of the inauguration, Aharon and his sons were the owners and Moshe was the Kohen), because for Moshe this was only in the category of service, and not a separate matter—a Kohen Gadol.

Based on this, one can explain why Rashi wrote in Parshas Emor that Moshe’s eating of *kodshei kodashim* is considered the eating of a *zar*, because since his work even during the seven days of the inauguration was from the aspect of his receiving the Torah, which is distinct from (and higher than) the matter of the (gedolah) kehunah, therefore his eating is considered as a *zar*.

## 35

7. The lesson from this in the service of everyone:
All matters of the Torah are eternal—as a lesson. From this it is understood that also the above regarding Moshe, that his primary status is in the matter of Torah and hearing the word of Hashem, is relevant to the Children of Israel [“Even though who am I and what am I that my heart would dare to approach to attain even one part in a thousand of the level of... the Faithful Shepherd, nevertheless, a small extremity and a trace of his great goodness and light shines to the entirety of Israel in every generation”], that there must be a situation in every one of Israel—through the Moshe within him —where he elevates himself even above the matter of service and ministry in the manner of kehunah, but rather he is entirely connected and attached to the word of Hashem, Torah, in the manner of "his Torah is his occupation," to the point that all other matters, even matters of holiness, will be (in the manner of the service of the Levites in their platform and the Israelites in their station) in the category of "strangeness" to him.

And through this, we hasten the coming of our righteous Mashiach, and the Kohanim in their service, and the Levites in their platform, and the Israelites in their station, and "a new Torah shall go forth from Me", and Mashiach will teach Torah to all the people, and "the entire world will have no occupation other than to know Hashem alone. Therefore, Israel will be great sages and know the hidden things, and they will attain the knowledge of their Creator according to the power of man, as it is said : 'For the earth shall be full of the knowledge of Hashem as the waters cover the sea'."


## ערב פסח

## 36

### Erev Pesach

1. In the "Order of the Korban Pesach" which the Alter Rebbe included in his Siddur, and which he wrote should be recited on Erev Pesach after Mincha (which follows the text found in the sefer *Seder HaYom*, and was copied in the *Shelah* and the *Siddur HaAriZal*), he concludes: "And if the Pesach is found to be *treifah*, it does not count for him until he brings another."

One must understand: In the aforementioned "Order of the Korban Pesach," not all the details regarding the order of its offering are brought, especially those things that are not common and do not occur every year—so why was this specific detail, "And if the Pesach is found to be *treifah*, it does not count for him," included?

Furthermore, it is difficult: This law ("if the Pesach is found to be *treifah*, etc.") applies immediately after the slaughter (as the Rambam states: "One who slaughters the Pesach and it is found to be *ba'al mum* [blemished] or *treifah*, he must slaughter another, etc."), and it is not a "part" so significant that it should be arranged here at the end of all the proceedings—especially since, on the face of it, it is not a positive conclusion—after the entire order of its offering and after the details of the washing of the *Azarah* (Temple courtyard)?

2. We may explain this by first noting the precision of the language in the Alter Rebbe’s Siddur there, before the "Order of the Korban Pesach": "And may our lips compensate for the bulls; and the Mincha prayer is in place of the *Tamid* of the afternoon, and at the time when the Beis HaMikdash existed, the Pesach was slaughtered after the *Tamid* of the afternoon; thus it is appropriate to engage in the order of the Korban Pesach after the Mincha prayer and recite this."

Now, in the *Shelah* (and similarly copied in the *Siddur HaAriZal*), the language is: "And may our lips compensate for the bulls; therefore the Men of the Great Assembly established the prayers corresponding to the *Tamidim*, and the Mincha prayer is against the *Tamid* of the afternoon. And since at the time of the Beis HaMikdash the Pesach was slaughtered after the *Tamid* of the afternoon, in order that our lips may compensate for the bulls, it is appropriate to engage in the order of the Korban Pesach after the Mincha prayer."

However, the Alter Rebbe omitted the words, "Therefore the Men of the Great Assembly established the prayers corresponding to the *Tamidim*... and in order that our lips may compensate for the bulls." It can be said that this is not merely for the sake of brevity, but rather a change in content:

## 37

In those words in the *Shelah*, it is emphasized that the reason for reciting the order of the Korban Pesach is along the lines of the concept that "the prayers were established corresponding to the *Tamidim*," "in order that our lips may compensate for the bulls."

But seemingly, the fact that prayers were established in place of sacrifices, and the recitation of the order of the sacrifice in place of offering it, are two different laws: That which the prayers were established in place of sacrifices (by the decree of the Men of the Great Assembly) means that the Sages established the act of (reciting) the prayer in place of offering the *Tamid* (and the reading of the *Parshas HaTamid* and the like before the prayer is a custom that "all Israel has adopted"); whereas the reading of the section of the sacrifices in place of offering them is learned from the verse, "This is the law of the *Chatas*," "And this is the law of the *Asham*," etc., as our Sages say at the end of *Menachos*: "Whoever occupies himself with the law of the *Olah* is as if he offered an *Olah*; whoever occupies himself with the law of the *Chatas* is as if he offered a *Chatas*; whoever occupies himself with the law of the *Asham* is as if he offered an *Asham*."

Based on this, it can be said that this is the reason for the Alter Rebbe’s change from the language of the *Shelah* (omitting the words "therefore the Men of the Great Assembly established... the prayers corresponding to the *Tamidim*... and in order that our lips may compensate for the bulls"), because the fact that the matter of "Mincha is in place of the *Tamid* of the afternoon" is brought here is not to equate the content of the Mincha prayer with the content of reciting the order of the Korban Pesach (that both are in place of a sacrifice), but only as a reason for the timing of the recitation of the order of the Korban Pesach—since the Pesach was slaughtered after the *Tamid*, therefore "it is appropriate to engage in the order of the Korban Pesach after the Mincha prayer" (which "is in place of the *Tamid* of the afternoon").

However, based on this, one must understand the reason of the *Shelah*, whose language (as above) implies that the recitation of the order of the Korban Pesach is the same concept as "the prayers were established in place of sacrifices" ("in order that our lips may compensate for the bulls").

3. The explanation for this may be:
Regarding the matter of "may our lips compensate for the bulls," where the

## 38

recitation is in place of the sacrifice, we find several modes and definitions:

a) The reading of the sections of the sacrifices in the Written Torah atones in place of the sacrifice—as our Sages say in *Maseches Taanis* and *Megillah*: "'How shall I know that I will inherit it?' Avraham said, 'Ribono shel Olam, perhaps Israel will sin before You, and You will do to them [as You did to the nations]?' He said to him, 'No... Take for Me a three-year-old heifer.' He said before Him, 'Ribono shel Olam, this is fine when the Beis HaMikdash exists, but when the Beis HaMikdash does not exist, what will be with them?' He said to him, 'I have already established for them the order of the sacrifices; when they read them before Me, I consider it for them as if they offered them before Me, and I forgive them for all their sins.'"

b) Occupying oneself with Torah (and especially the laws of the sacrifices) is like offering the sacrifice—as our Sages say (at the end of *Menachos*) mentioned above: "Torah scholars who occupy themselves with Torah... I consider it for them as if they were burning and offering [incense] before My Name... whoever occupies himself with the law of the *Chatas* is as if he offered [a *Chatas*]..."

c) That which the prayers were established in place of sacrifices.

It can be said that these are three different definitions:
1) That the reading of the order of the sacrifices in the Written Torah is in place of the sacrifice—this refers to the result (the "effect"), that through the reading of the section of the sacrifices in the Written Torah, Hashem atones for Israel just as He atones for them through the sacrifices.

2) That the occupation with Torah (in the Oral Torah), and especially in the laws of the *Avodah* and sacrifices, is considered like the offering and burning—this means (not only regarding the result, but) also regarding the act of offering and burning, that it is as if there is an act of sacrifice here.
However, even so, the fact that the occupation is as if there is an act of sacrifice here only means that it is considered as if the person (*gavra*) performed the act of offering and burning, but this does not relate to the object (*cheftza*), for there is no *cheftza* of a sacrifice here.

3) But that which the prayers were established in place of sacrifices—it can be said that it relates to the *cheftza* of the offering,

## 39

that not only does the person receive the reward or atonement that comes through offering a sacrifice (the result of the sacrifice), or the elevation achieved in the person through the act of sacrifice, but the act of prayer is considered literally as the act of sacrifice, such that there is a *cheftza* of a "sacrifice" here. Therefore, there are several conditions on the one praying, similar to one who offers a sacrifice: "One must be careful that it be like the sacrifice in intention, and that he not mix into it any other thought, like a thought that invalidates holy offerings; and it should be while standing, like the *Avodah*; and with a fixed place, like the sacrifices, for each one has its place fixed for its slaughter and the application of its blood; and that nothing should intervene between him and the wall, like the sacrifice, for an intervention invalidates... and it is appropriate that he have fine garments special for prayer, like the priestly vestments, etc."

4. Now, in the recitation of the "Order of the Korban Pesach" on Erev Pesach, we find a novelty that we do not find in other matters of Torah regarding which it is said that "whoever occupies himself, etc., is as if he offered," for we do not recite the section of the Korban Pesach in the Written Torah (along the lines of reciting the *Parshas HaTamid* or the section of the sacrifices and the like (like that of *Taanis* and *Megillah*)), nor do we occupy ourselves with its laws in the Oral Torah (along the lines of the Mishnah *Eizehu Mekoman* (and that of *Menachos*) and the like)—rather, we recount the order of its actual offering.

It can be said that this is because the occupation with the order of the Korban Pesach is literally like the act of offering the Korban Pesach, along the lines of how it is in prayer through the act of prayer.

And with this, the language in the sefer *Seder HaYom* after the text of the "Order of the Korban Pesach" (which was copied in the *Shelah*, *Siddur HaAriZal*, and the Alter Rebbe’s Siddur) is resolved: "This is the matter in great brevity. And the person who is G-d-fearing and trembles at the word of Hashem must read it at its proper time, so that his reading may ascend in place of its offering, and he should worry about the destruction of the Beis HaMikdash and plead before Hashem, Creator of the world, that He rebuild it speedily in our days, Amen."

From the precision of the language, "the person who is G-d-fearing and trembles at the word of Hashem (must) read it at its proper time, so that his reading may ascend in place of its offering," it is implied that the fact that we recite the order of the Korban Pesach after the Mincha prayer is not only because that is the time of the offering of the Pesach (as the language before the order of the Korban Pesach implies), but because when the reading is at its proper time—"his reading ascends in place of its offering."

And seemingly, this requires explanation: After all, one of the differences between the two laws—that prayers were established in place of sacrifices, and the reading of the section (and learning the laws) of the sacrifices in place of offering them—is that prayer must be at the time of the offering of the *Tamidim* specifically, whereas learning the section (and laws) of the sacrifice in place of offering it is not connected with the time of the obligation of the offering.

[As is understood from the continuation of the discussion at the end of *Menachos* regarding "whoever occupies himself with the law of..., is as if he offered...," and as is stated explicitly before that there regarding the verse: "A song of ascents, behold, bless Hashem, all you servants of Hashem, who stand in the house of Hashem in the nights—what is 'in the nights'? Rabbi Yochanan said: These are the Torah scholars who occupy themselves with Torah at night; the verse considers it for them as if they were occupied with the *Avodah*."

And regarding what the *Tur* says, that the section of the sacrifices "is better to recite during the day, when they are in place of offering the sacrifice, whose time is during the day" (and according to many opinions one must recite it while standing), (a) the Tzemach Tzedek has already written that "this is specifically regarding the matter of reciting these sections which belong to the order of prayer, but regarding the matter of learning the laws of the sacrifices, there is no distinction between day and night"; (b) even in this, it is not similar to the stringency of the obligation of prayer at its time (which must be exactly at the time of the offering of the *Tamidim* specifically)].

If so, it is puzzling what is meant by "the person who is G-d-fearing and trembles at the word of Hashem must read it at its proper time, so that his reading may ascend in place of its offering"—that the fact that "his reading ascends in place of its offering" depends on the reading being at its proper time?

This proves that the recitation of the "Order of the Korban Pesach" is considered like the actual act of offering the Korban Pesach, and therefore "the person who is G-d-fearing and trembles at the word of Hashem must read it at its proper time, so that his reading may ascend in place of its offering."

And this is also the precision of the *Shelah’s* language in his words before the order of the Korban Pesach, where he brings that prayers are in place of sacrifices because "may our lips compensate for the bulls," and continues, "and in order that our lips may compensate for the bulls, it is appropriate to engage in the order of the Korban Pesach after the Mincha prayer"—in which it is emphasized that (a) the content of reciting the order of the Korban Pesach is like the matter of prayer, (b) the "may our lips compensate for the bulls" through the recitation of the order of the Korban Pesach depends on reciting it at its proper time, "after the Mincha prayer."

5. However, based on this, we must examine the language of the Alter Rebbe in his Siddur, who changed from the language of the *Shelah* mentioned above (as in section 2), even though he did copy the language (after the text of the order of the Korban Pesach): "the person who is G-d-fearing and trembles at the word of Hashem must read it at its proper time, so that his reading may ascend in place of its offering."

Simply put, it could be said that this is for the sake of brevity, especially since the lengthiness of the *Shelah* is also not in the sefer *Seder HaYom*.

Seemingly, this can be explained more deeply: The Alter Rebbe’s intention is that there is an advantage in the recitation of the "Order of the Korban Pesach" even over that which "prayers were established in place of sacrifices."

For in prayer, the revelation of its connection to the act of offering a sacrifice is not evident; rather, the Sages interpreted and revealed that the act (speech) of prayer is considered as the act of offering a sacrifice. That is to say, openly, the person is not occupied with the matter of offering a sacrifice (which is not even like one who is occupied with the law of the *Chatas*, etc., where the person is openly occupied with the law of the sacrifices);

Whereas in our case, the recitation of the order of the Korban Pesach, we are reciting the details of the order of the sacrifice as it was done in practice in the Beis HaMikdash (which is even more than learning the laws of the sacrifice in the Oral Torah, as above), meaning that the person is actually occupied with the matter of offering a sacrifice, only it is not in action but in his speech. From this, it is understood that when this speech is considered as an act of sacrifice, it is even more than the act of prayer. That is, it is considered as if he is standing in the Beis HaMikdash and actually performing all the details of the things he says in the order of the Korban Pesach, an act of sacrifice in reality.

Therefore, the Alter Rebbe did not copy the language of the *Shelah* mentioned above, which implies that the "may our lips compensate for the bulls" through the recitation of the order of the Korban Pesach is along the lines of that which "prayers were established in place of sacrifices," because in the recitation of the order of the Korban Pesach, it is in a more elevated manner.

6. Based on the above, we can also explain why there is a special obligation here that "he should worry about the destruction of the Beis HaMikdash and plead before Hashem, Creator of the world, that He rebuild it speedily in our days, Amen." Since the perfection of the act of the Korban Pesach is in its offering in the Beis HaMikdash, therefore, in order that his reading may ascend in place of its offering in reality, he must know that in the elevation where his reading ascends in place of its offering, "may our lips compensate for the bulls," there is still a lack in the perfection of its offering. He must worry about the destruction of the Beis HaMikdash and plead, etc., that He rebuild it, for then there will be the perfection of the offering of the Pesach, and in combination with the worry and the pleas ("and he should plead"), this effects that his reading will be in place of the sacrifice in reality.

And based on this foundation, the conclusion "And if the Pesach is found to be *treifah*, it does not count for him until he brings another" is well understood. For at the end of the "Order of the Korban Pesach," they emphasize the intention of this entire recitation (as the saying goes, "everything follows the conclusion"), and therefore they say this law, "if the Pesach is found to be *treifah*, it does not count for him, etc.," in which it is emphasized that the act of the person is not enough, but rather, the essential thing is the *cheftza* of the sacrifice in reality. Therefore, if the sacrifice is invalidated, it does not count for him and he must bring another (fitting the aforementioned novelty, that the occupation with the order of the Korban Pesach must be in a manner that it matters to him that it be its offering in reality, not just the result of the sacrifice or the elevation in the act of the person that is considered like a sacrifice).

7. We must explain the reason why the Korban Pesach differs from other sacrifices regarding "may our lips compensate for the bulls," in that specifically for it, we recite the order of the Korban Pesach in a manner that it is in place of the actual act of offering:
The Korban Pesach is the sacrifice of the redemption of the people of Israel, both redemption in the simple sense, as it is said regarding Pesach

## 43

they are found outside their boundaries—in exile. Even when their service is in the ultimate level of elevation and perfection, the fact that they are found outside their boundaries, in exile—that they are found "in the field"—is in the category of "*treifah*," for they lack true perfection, vitality, and freedom.

And this is the hint in this law: that at the time of reciting the order of the Korban Pesach, a person must feel that even though he elevates himself to a state of redemption and spiritual freedom—and even to the point that he has merited an elevation in the manner of "Pesach," a term of skipping and jumping, meaning an elevation that is not in proportion at all—nevertheless, since the "Pesach" is "in the field," it is in the category of "*treifah*," since he is still in exile.

And consequently—"it does not count for him," for with all the elevations that exist in this reading, in comparison to the level of Israel at the time of the redemption, they come to the realization that "it does not count for him," for at his level, this is not an elevation for him; it is not in proportion at all to his true elevation at the time of the redemption.

It can be said that this is why they conclude "until he brings another"—"another" for the sake of elevation, as in the Haggadah—"another" that is even above the *seraphim* (the world of *Beriah*), a completely different service (*Atzilus*), which is not in proportion at all to the previous service, and it is the elevation that will be in the offering of the Korban Pesach in the Third Beis HaMikdash, "that He rebuild... speedily in our days, Amen."

(From a sicha of Erev Pesach, after Mincha, 5748)


## חג הפסח

## Sicha 1

## 44

A. The Rambam writes (in *Hilchos Chametz U’Matzah*): "In the present time... after reciting the blessing *HaMotzi Lechem*, one recites the blessing over the eating of *matzah*, dips the *matzah* in *charoses*, and eats." This is also the opinion of Rav Amram Gaon, the Ritz Gias, and others (as cited in the *Maggid Mishneh* there) that one dips the *matzah* in *charoses*.

However, the Ra’avad challenged him, writing, "This is nonsense." The Tur also wrote regarding this practice, "I do not know why this dipping in *charoses* [is done], etc." Furthermore, the author of the *Manhig* raised a difficulty against their view: "Matzah is a remembrance of freedom, and *charoses* is a remembrance of the clay; how can they be combined?"

The *Bach* resolves this: "There is some basis for their words from the *Mishnah* : 'They brought before him *matzah*, *chazeres*, *charoses*, and two cooked dishes. Even though *charoses* is not a *mitzvah*, Rabbi Elazar bar Tzadok says it is a *mitzvah*.' The difficulty is: what are they arguing about? Since everyone agrees that *charoses* must be brought, what difference does it make if it is a *mitzvah* or not? Surely, even according to Rabbi Elazar bar Tzadok, one does not recite a blessing over the *charoses* even though it is a *mitzvah*, as our Rabbi writes shortly thereafter. Rather, it must be that for the *Tanna Kamma*, since it is not a *mitzvah* and is brought only for the sake of *kapa*, it is required only for the *maror* and vegetables. But for Rabbi Elazar bar Tzadok, since it is a *mitzvah* as a remembrance of the clay, it is therefore required for the *matzah* as well. And since the Rambam in Chapter 7 rules like Rabbi Elazar bar Tzadok that it is a *mitzvah*, he therefore also rules that one dips the *matzah* in *charoses*. As for the difficulty raised by the *Manhig*—that *matzah* is a remembrance of freedom and *charoses* a remembrance of the clay—it is possible to say that our intention with this dipping is to indicate that we were brought out of slavery, which is the clay (of which *charoses* is a remembrance), to freedom, of which the *matzah* is a remembrance."

According to this, it would seemingly follow that the Ra’avad rules like the *Tanna Kamma*—that *charoses* is not a *mitzvah* and is brought only for the sake of *kapa*, and is not required for anything other than *maror*—and therefore there is no place to dip *matzah* in *charoses*.

However, it is difficult to explain that the dispute between the Rambam and the Ra’avad is whether the *halachah* follows Rabbi Elazar bar Tzadok (that *charoses* is a *mitzvah* as a remembrance of the clay) or 

## 45

whether the *halachah* follows the Sages (that it is not a *mitzvah* and is brought only for *kapa*)—for if so, why did the Ra’avad not challenge the Rambam (a) earlier (in Chapter 7), where he wrote (b) explicitly that "the *charoses* is a *mitzvah*... a remembrance of the clay"? He should have said there that this is not so, and that the *halachah* is that it is only for the sake of *kapa*.

B. Seemingly, this can be explained by prefacing what our Alter Rebbe writes in his *Shulchan Aruch* : "They ordained that *charoses* should be before him during the recitation of the *Haggadah*, for the *charoses* is a remembrance of the clay in which our ancestors were enslaved in Egypt, etc.; therefore, it must be on the table at the time one recounts the slavery of Egypt." Later, in a separate chapter, he writes, "One does not dip in *charoses* except as a *mitzvah*, for it is a remembrance of the clay."

It is implied by his words that there are two separate laws regarding *charoses*: 1) Bringing it to the table so that it is before him during the recitation of the *Haggadah*, and in addition to this, 2) the *mitzvah* of dipping in *charoses*. The first law—bringing it to the table and having it present during the recitation of the *Haggadah*—is unrelated to the dipping, and therefore the Alter Rebbe did not mention in the law of bringing it that it is for the sake of the dipping.

This is also proven by what he writes later (in that same chapter) regarding bringing the vegetables with all the items, stating: "It is good to arrange them before him on the *ke’arah* in a way that he will not need to pass over the *mitzvos*; that is, the vegetables should be closer to him than the *matzah*, and the *matzah* closer than the *maror* and *charoses*, and the *maror* and *charoses* closer than the two cooked dishes. There are those who are not particular about the *charoses* and the two cooked dishes being close to him, for since they are not brought to the table except as a mere remembrance, the rule of 'do not pass over the *mitzvos*' does not apply to them". Here, he equates the *charoses* to the two cooked dishes, in that their presence on the table is (not for eating, but) only "as a mere remembrance," so that they are on the table at the time of the *Haggadah* recitation.

Seemingly, this is also the case according to the Rambam, for in Chapter 7 he wrote, "The *charoses* is a *mitzvah* of the Sages, a remembrance of the clay in which they labored in Egypt, etc., and they bring it to the table on the nights of Pesach." He did not mention there the matter of dipping in it or eating it, etc., which implies that the *mitzvah* is only in bringing it to the table, while here in Chapter 8 he speaks of the matter of dipping in it.

Based on this, one could say that according to the *Bach*, the dispute between the Sages and Rabbi Elazar bar Tzadok as to whether *charoses* is a *mitzvah* or for the sake of *kapa* concerns only the law of dipping in *charoses*—but for all, there is a *mitzvah* in bringing the *charoses* to the table during the recitation of the *Haggadah* as a remembrance of the clay. Therefore, the Ra’avad did not challenge him earlier in Chapter 7 (regarding the law of the *mitzvah* of bringing *charoses* to the table), but only in Chapter 8—regarding the law of dipping *matzah* in *charoses*.

## 46
