However, aside from the fact that it is a great strain to say that according to the Sages there is a *mitzvah* in bringing *charoses* to the table (since the plain language of their statement, "They brought before him *matzah*, *chazeres*, *charoses*, and two cooked dishes, even though *charoses* is not a *mitzvah*," implies that they hold there is no *mitzvah* in *charoses* at all)—the very interpretation above (that the dispute between the Rambam and the Ra’avad is whether the *halachah* follows the Sages or Rabbi Elazar bar Tzadok) is questionable. If that were the case, would the Ra’avad have written "This is nonsense" regarding the Rambam’s words? Is it "nonsense" simply because he ruled like Rabbi Elazar bar Tzadok?

Therefore, it seems to me that even the Ra’avad holds that the dipping in *charoses* is a *mitzvah* of the Sages as a remembrance of the clay, like the words of Rabbi Elazar bar Tzadok (and therefore he did not challenge the Rambam’s words earlier in Chapter 7, as mentioned above), and nevertheless, the Ra’avad holds that there is no place to dip *matzah* in *charoses*.

C. In *Tzafnas Paneach*, the dispute between the Rambam and the Ra’avad is explained differently. He writes: "See the *Hassagos* and the *Kesef Mishneh*. Regarding *matzah*, there are two reasons for the *mitzvah* of eating it: one as a remembrance of the poverty, and one as a remembrance of the freedom... Regarding *maror*, there is only one reason: as a remembrance of the slavery, and *charoses* is also for the sake of the slavery. Now, during the time of the *Beis HaMikdash*, they certainly dipped the *matzah* in *charoses* as well. But in the present time, since we see that the *mitzvah* of the remembrance of the slavery is nullified—for the *maror* in the present time is only rabbinic, and they only ordained it as a remembrance—but for *matzah*, there is no need to ordain it, since the reason of freedom remains, which is from the Torah. If so, there is no place for dipping in *charoses*. On this, our Rabbi and the Ra’avad differ: whether a rabbinic enactment is applicable where, without it, an obligation from the Torah already exists."

The intention of the *Tzafnas Paneach* is (also) to explain that the Ra’avad’s challenge is only regarding the law of dipping *matzah* in *charoses* in *Halachah* 8, and not regarding the law of dipping *matzah* in *charoses* mentioned earlier in the Rambam, when he speaks (in *Halachah* 6) of the law of eating *matzah* and *maror* during the time of the *Beis HaMikdash*. He writes, "And afterwards he wraps *matzah* and *maror* together and dips in *charoses*, etc.," and the Ra’avad challenges there only what the Rambam wrote, "wraps *matzah* and *maror* together" (that "this is like Hillel, but this order is not precise"), but not the dipping in *charoses*.

## 47

[One cannot say that what the Rambam wrote there, "and dips in *charoses*," refers only to the *maror* (which is in the wrap) and not to the *matzah*, for the plain language of the Rambam, "and dips in *charoses*" (and not "and dips the *maror* in *charoses*"), indicates that he dips everything in *charoses*. Furthermore: the Rambam continues later, "And if he ate *matzah* by itself and *maror* by itself, he recites the blessing over this by itself and over this by itself"—and he does not distinguish between them regarding the dipping in *charoses*, for when he eats them by themselves (and not in a wrap), he dips only the *maror* in *charoses, not the *matzah*].

This is what troubles the *Tzafnas Paneach* regarding the words of the Ra’avad—why did he not challenge the dipping of *matzah* in *charoses* in *Halachah* 6?

From this, the *Tzafnas Paneach* infers that during the time of the *Beis HaMikdash*, everyone agrees that they used to dip the *matzah* in *charoses*, because during the time of the *Beis HaMikdash*, there is also a remembrance of the poverty in the *matzah* (and consequently, it is appropriate to dip the *matzah* in *charoses*, which is a remembrance of the clay). The dispute between the Rambam and the Ra’avad is only regarding *matzah* in the present time, where the remembrance of the slavery in the *matzah* is nullified by Torah law (since the *maror* in the present time is not from the Torah), and only the remembrance of freedom remains in it. On this they differ: is there room to say that there was a special rabbinic enactment that the *matzah* in the present time should also be a remembrance of the slavery (just as they ordained *maror* in the present time)?

The Rambam holds that there is room for this enactment, and therefore he holds that even in the present time, there must be a dipping of *matzah* in *charoses*. In contrast, for the Ra’avad, there is no room for a special rabbinic enactment regarding an act that already has (in any case) an obligation from the Torah. Since even without the enactment there is an obligation to eat *matzah* from the Torah (as a remembrance of freedom), there is no room for the Sages to ordain an additional matter for this eating, and therefore there is no dipping of *matzah* in *charoses* in the present time.

However, his explanation of the Rambam’s position requires clarification: what matter is there in this enactment that the *matzah* should also be a remembrance of the slavery? For they are not introducing (through this enactment) any new act in the essence of eating *matzah* (other than the dipping in *charoses*, which is what is being discussed), because in any case one is obligated to eat *matzah* (unlike *maror*, where they ordained the eating of *maror* as a remembrance of the slavery), and nothing was added to the *matzah* except another reason for eating it.

[Seemingly, this is not similar to the examples brought in the *Tzafnas Paneach* there, such as "in that passage in *Berachos*... regarding a woman who served and then saw *niddah* blood, and in the *Yerushalmi* there, if there is a place for a light impurity alongside a severe impurity, etc."—for there, through the rabbinic enactment, a new status of impurity is created (the light impurity which is rabbinic). This is similar to the other examples in the *Tzafnas Paneach*, but in the case at hand, seemingly, no act or legal status was added to the *matzah* by this enactment.]

D. It seems possible to explain this by prefacing the explanation of the aforementioned *Tzafnas Paneach* that in the present time, "the remembrance of the slavery is nullified, for the *maror* in the present time is rabbinic." Seemingly, the fact that the *mitzvah* of *maror* does not exist by Torah law in the present time is because the eating of *maror* is secondary to the *Pesach* offering and is not a *mitzvah* in its own right, but is considered like the preparations for the *Pesach*. Therefore, in the present time, when there is no *Pesach* offering, there is no *mitzvah* of *maror* by Torah law (as the Rambam writes). But what is the

## 48

proof from this that in the present time the entire matter of the remembrance of the slavery is nullified? And in terms of the content of the matter, it is difficult to understand: why would it be that specifically in the present time (when the *Beis HaMikdash* does not exist) the remembrance of the slavery should be nullified, while only the remembrance of freedom remains?

The explanation for this is as follows:
In the *mitzvos* of this night, there are two general obligations as a remembrance of the Exodus from Egypt: the *mitzvah* of speech—recounting the Exodus from Egypt—and the *mitzvos* of action: *Pesach*, *matzah*, and *maror*.

The content of both is to make known our redemption and our freedom, both through recounting the Exodus from Egypt in speech, and through the actions and obligations of this night—the eating of *Pesach*, *matzah*, and *maror*.

Just as it is with the recounting of the Exodus from Egypt in speech, where one must begin with the disgrace, "He begins and makes known that we were slaves to Pharaoh in Egypt, and all the evil that he did to us, and concludes with the miracles and wonders that were done for us and our freedom" —so too is it with the remembrance of freedom in the actions of this night. One must emphasize in it the magnitude of the slavery and the servitude from which the redemption came. Therefore, there must also be the eating of *maror*, "because the Egyptians embittered the lives of our ancestors in Egypt", for only through this is the fulfillment of the *mitzvos* of freedom (of *Pesach* and *matzah*) complete.

[This is also the meaning of the statement that *maror* is part of the *mitzvah* of eating the *Pesach* offering and is considered like the preparations for the *Pesach*, because the fulfillment of the *mitzvah* of eating the *Pesach* offering—"because Hashem passed over the houses of our ancestors in Egypt" —is complete only together with the eating of *maror*, because they embittered, etc.]

Based on this, we can sweeten the words of the *Bach* (cited above) in resolving the difficulty of the *Manhig* (that *matzah* is a remembrance of freedom and *charoses* is a remembrance of the clay; how can they be combined?): "Our intention with this dipping is to indicate that we were brought out of slavery, which is the clay (of which *charoses* is a remembrance), to freedom, of which the *matzah* is a remembrance." That is to say, the matter of freedom and the matter of servitude are connected to one another, for through the remembrance of the servitude (the clay), the remembrance of the freedom is enhanced, in that Hashem brought us out from servitude to redemption.

E. The definition of recounting the Exodus from Egypt in speech and the conduct of freedom in action is not merely as a remembrance that our ancestors were slaves and were redeemed, but rather that this slavery and redemption belong to the person himself. Just as in the recounting of the Exodus from Egypt, "He begins and makes known that we were slaves to Pharaoh... with miracles... that were done for us and our freedom," so too is it with the freedom in action, as the Rambam writes : "A person is obligated to show himself as if he himself had now gone out from the slavery of Egypt, etc. And regarding this matter, Hashem commanded in the Torah : 'And you shall remember that you were a slave'—meaning, as if you yourself were a slave and went out to freedom and were redeemed."

The obligation of performing all the actions of this night in a manner of freedom is not merely as an external sign, but the obligation is that through these actions, the person should feel himself to be a free man, that "you went out to freedom and were redeemed."

According to this, it is understood that there is a difference between the time of the *Beis HaMikdash* and the time of the exile: In the time of the *Beis HaMikdash*, when the Jewish people are in a state of freedom, they can fulfill in completeness the obligation to show as if he himself went out from the slavery of Egypt, even when there is the remembrance of the servitude in action through *maror*.

But in a time when the Jews are not on their land and are in exile, a specific action that emphasizes the servitude (the eating of *maror*) could potentially cause a weakening in the remembrance of freedom and the feeling of freedom as it should be, since, after all, he is in a state of exile and servitude. Therefore, "the remembrance of the slavery is nullified" so that it will not confuse the fact that all the actions of this night must be in a manner of freedom.

F. The reason we recount the Exodus from Egypt in every generation during the time of the exile, even though we are in exile and servitude—as we say at the beginning of the *Haggadah*, "Now we are slaves"—is because the slavery of Egypt was a unique slavery, and any state of slavery and servitude of the Jewish people is not in the category and type of the slaves of Pharaoh, the servitude of the exile of Egypt, from which the Jewish people remained and became liberated forever.

It is possible to say that this is the difference between *maror* and *charoses*: both are a remembrance of the slavery, in that *maror* is for the sake of slavery, and *charoses* is from the words of the Sages "also for the sake of slavery." However, the remembrance of the slavery in *maror* belongs to slavery in general (for every slavery has bitterness in it, etc.), while *charoses* is "a remembrance of the clay in which they labored in Egypt," meaning it indicates the unique slavery of Egypt.

Based on this, we can explain the dispute between the Rambam and the Ra’avad regarding the dipping of *matzah* in *charoses*, and the difference in this regard between the time of the *Beis HaMikdash* and the present time:

In the time of the *Beis HaMikdash*, since they are in a state of freedom, it is possible to fulfill the *mitzvah* of remembering the servitude and redemption of Egypt in its completeness: "And you shall remember that you were a slave—meaning, as if you yourself were a slave and went out to freedom and were redeemed." Therefore, for all, they dip *matzah* in *charoses*, which indicates that "He brought us out from slavery to freedom" (as mentioned above from the *Bach*).

But in the present time, when they are in a state of "slaves," and it is impossible to fulfill the obligation of "And you shall remember that you were a slave... and went out to freedom" in total completeness, the question is whether they can fulfill it at least in part—the fulfillment of the "remembrance" of the departure from the unique servitude of Egypt (which exists even when they are in exile, as mentioned above).

The Rambam holds that it is possible to fulfill it, and therefore he dips *matzah* in *charoses*, "to indicate... that He brought us out from slavery, which is the clay (of which *charoses* is a remembrance), to freedom, of which the *matzah* is a remembrance," meaning the intention is the departure from the slavery of Egypt (which is hinted at in the clay). For the Ra’avad, it is not divisible, and there is no partial redemption. Consequently, even though he fulfills the recounting of the Exodus from Egypt in all its details, etc., and the *mitzvah* of *matzah* even in the present time is from the Torah—because one must fulfill not only the recounting of the Exodus from Egypt but also the act of freedom—

## 50

nevertheless, it is impossible to fulfill "And you shall remember that you were a slave... and went out to freedom" in action (in its completeness) when he is in a state of "slaves".

[It is possible to say that this is the precision of the Ra’avad’s challenge, "This is nonsense," for he may be hinting that this instruction—to dip *matzah* in *charoses* because "He brought us out from slavery, which is the clay (of which *charoses* is a remembrance), to freedom, of which the *matzah* is a remembrance"—in the present time, when he is not in freedom, is not a remembrance, but is in the category of "nonsense."]

G. It is possible to explain all of this in the inner dimension of the matters.
The fact that the servitude and exile of Egypt is more severe than other exiles is not only from the perspective of the slavery and physical servitude—the harshness of the servitude literally—but also (and on the contrary, primarily) from the perspective of the "harshness of the servitude" in spirituality. The Jewish people in Egypt were submissive and enslaved to the "evil" of Egypt, as is known regarding the interpretation of "And the Egyptians embittered us" : the Egyptians made us evil, in that their evil entered into us (to the point that the Jewish people were submerged in the forty-nine gates of impurity, and our Sages said that at the time of the splitting of the sea, the attribute of justice argued, "These are idolaters and these are, etc.").

The reason that the spiritual exile of Egypt is harder than all other exiles is because the evil of the exile of Egypt is more severe than all other exiles (as our Sages said that the Egyptians are more corrupt than all other nations, and this is hinted at in that Egypt is called "the nakedness of the land"). This is not just a difference in quantity, but rather it is a completely different type, because the evil of Egypt contains the general point of evil, from which all the details of the matters of evil branch out (including all the evil in the other exiles).

This is one of the explanations of our Sages that all the kingdoms are named after Egypt because they "distress" (*metzirin*) the Jews. This is because the evil in Egypt is the root and source for the evil in all the other kingdoms (and exiles), for Egypt is the point of evil from which the details of evil branch out, as mentioned above, and therefore they are all named after it.

## 51

It is possible to say that this matter—that the exile of Egypt contains the general point of evil—is hinted at in the matter of *charoses*, which is "a remembrance of the clay" (and as mentioned above, this emphasizes that it is a remembrance of the harshness of the servitude unique to the exile of Egypt specifically).

This is the (inner) reason that in *charoses* there are two laws (as mentioned above in section B in the words of the Rambam and the Alter Rebbe): the primary enactment, which is bringing it to the table so that it is before him at the time of the recitation of the *Haggadah*, and in addition to this, the *mitzvah* of dipping in it. It is possible to say that these correspond to the two matters in the exile and servitude of Egypt: that it contains a unique evil, the generality of evil and its expansion, and that it is the root of all the exiles, which causes the expansion of evil.

Based on this, we can explain the difference between *charoses* and *maror*: for *maror*, a measure of eating (*kezayis*) is stated, whereas for *charoses*, a measure of *kezayis* is not required, and in general, no measure is stated for it. For a measure indicates something that is composed of parts and details, and to reach the required quantity, one needs a specific measure. This is not so for a matter that is not in the category of a measure, because it is in the category of a point [similar to how the prohibition of idolatry is in any amount —because the prohibition of idolatry is not just a specific matter, but it is the point of the whole (as our Sages said, "Whoever acknowledges idolatry is like one who denies the entire Torah"), and therefore a measure does not apply to it]. And so it is regarding *charoses*, that since it is a remembrance of the generality of the evil of Egypt, it therefore has no measure.

H. We find a second matter in *charoses*—"a remembrance of the apple" (as it is in the *Gemara* and brought to *halachah* in the *Shulchan Aruch* of our Alter Rebbe). It is a remembrance of the fact "that they would give birth to their children there without pain... as it is written, 'Under the apple tree I awakened you.'" According to this interpretation, the obligation of *charoses* is a remembrance of a good thing—the merit of the Jewish people. Especially according to what the Geonim wrote (and so it is ruled in the *Rema* and the *Shulchan Aruch* of the Alter Rebbe) that one should make *charoses* with fruits that resemble the Congregation of Israel.

According to the above (section G), one must connect the two matters, "a remembrance of the clay" and "a remembrance of the apple":
It is written, "Also this corresponds to that, Hashem has made," from which it is understood that the opposition of the evil of Egypt to the good of the Jewish people is a kind of parallel. Since Egypt is the

## 52

general point of evil, it therefore opposes (not (only) the specific merits of the Jewish people, but) the point of Judaism, the essence of the good that is in the souls of the Jewish people. This is the matter of the redemption of Egypt—that it is a spiritual redemption—that the point of good that is in the souls of the Jewish people was redeemed from its exile under the control of the evil of Egypt.

[It is possible to say that this is the reason that the redemption of Egypt is an eternal redemption, to the point that everyone is obligated to see himself as if he went out from Egypt—because through the redemption of Egypt, the "essence of the good" that is in the souls of the Jewish people was redeemed forever from the impurity of Egypt, in such a way that there is no longer a possibility that the Jewish people will be under the servitude of the evil of Egypt. That is, it is not in the power of the generality of the point of evil to enslave the point of Judaism that is in the Jewish people. Consequently, in relation to the essential point that is in the souls of the Jewish people, since they went out from Egypt, they became "free men" forever (for the servitude to "evil" in other exiles (since the evil in these exiles is only the expansion of evil, therefore the servitude is also) only in the expansion (in the powers) of the person—his intellect, his emotions, and so on—but his (essential) soul is always "in faith with Him, may He be blessed"). It is possible to say that this is the content of what the Maharal wrote, that in the redemption of Egypt, the Jewish people received an essential quality of being free men, and the occurrence of the exile that followed does not nullify this at all.]

This is the matter that *charoses* is "a remembrance of the apple"—that this miracle (the birth of the children "without pain") came from the essential affection of Hashem for the Jewish people, from the essence of their being Jewish and their having the point of Judaism within them. Based on this, we can explain why *charoses* has no measure (also) in a positive sense—because the matter of *charoses* in the soul of the person corresponds to the point of Judaism, the essence of the soul, which is above measure and limit.

I. Based on the above, we can explain the two aforementioned laws regarding *charoses*—(a) bringing it to the table, (b) dipping (the *matzah*) in *charoses*—(also) in a positive sense:
The *mitzvah* in the very act of bringing the *charoses* to the table corresponds to the fact that *charoses* belongs to the point of Judaism, the (essence of the) soul in the Jew, whose merit is not defined and limited by the doing of good in practice, but rather that in its very essence it is good, and this is expressed by the fact that its very presence on the table is the *mitzvah*.

But the intention is not that the point of Judaism should remain by itself, but that it should expand and act in all the powers of the person, so that they will be permeated with the point and source of good that comes from the (essence of the) soul. This is hinted at in the second law regarding *charoses*, the obligation to dip the *matzah* in it—meaning, that even in the *matzah* (which indicates the revealed good that is in the person), the matter of *charoses* should be felt.

Based on this, we can understand the difference between the time of the *Beis HaMikdash* and the time of the exile regarding the dipping of *matzah* in *charoses*—for there is a fundamental difference in the need for the action of "charoses" between the time of the *Beis HaMikdash* and the time of the exile:
In the time of the *Beis HaMikdash*, the Jewish people are in a state of physical freedom—which also indicates spiritual freedom and redemption—the *Shechinah* dwells among the Jews, the spiritual state of the Jews is as it should be, their service of Hashem is as it should be, and all the powers of their soul are in a state of freedom from the evil inclination, and all are enslaved to Hashem and His service. However, since the powers of the person are in measurement and limitation, the person needs to reveal and draw down the essential point that is in his soul, which is above "measure" (above form, measurement, and limitation), so that through this, all his powers will be permeated with the power of self-sacrifice that comes from the (essence of the) soul. This is the content of the matter of dipping *matzah* in *charoses* in the time of the *Beis HaMikdash*, which leads to a state of freedom in completeness.

## 53

But in the time of the exile, when the divine soul is in exile and captivity under the rule of the evil inclination and the animal soul, whether in his emotions and intellect, and certainly when they have control over the person’s garments—in thought, speech, and action—which are not as


## שמיני

## 54

### Sicha 1

A. Regarding the verse "And he slaughtered the burnt-offering, and the sons of Aharon brought the blood to him, and he dashed it upon the altar round about," Rashi copies the word "vayamtzi'uhu" (and they brought/presented it) and explains it as "an expression of handing over and preparation."

Simply put, Rashi’s intent is to explain the word "vayamtzi'uhu"—so that one would not think it is derived from the word *metzi'ah* (finding) [similar to the commentary of the Ibn Ezra here, "from the root *matza*, that they found it at the time of need," meaning that the sons of Aharon caused the blood to be present/available to Aharon]—but rather that it is "an expression of handing over and preparation."

However, Rashi’s double expression—"an expression of handing over and preparation"—requires explanation, as seemingly it would have sufficed to say "an expression of handing over".

Moreover: "Handing over" (*hoshtah*) and "preparation" (*hazmanah*) are two distinct concepts. "Handing over" implies the transfer and giving from hand to hand, while "preparation" means *hachanah*, that they prepared the blood. What is the necessity to explain that "vayamtzi'uhu" includes both concepts—"an expression of handing over and preparation"?

Simply put, Rashi’s intent is to explain that this "handing over" was in the manner of "vayamtzi'uhu," meaning that through the handing over to his colleague, the item became prepared, ready, and present with him (similar to the Ibn Ezra's interpretation mentioned above). But seemingly, this is not a sufficient explanation, for because of this, Rashi did not need to emphasize that it was an act of *hazmanah* specifically. He could have said, "an expression of handing over so that it would be present," or something similar (like the language of the Ibn Ezra), which refers to the actual presence of the item (without distinguishing in what manner it became present with him, whether it was prepared or not). Why, then, does Rashi emphasize that "vayamtzi'uhu is an expression of handing over and preparation"?

B. It can be said that Rashi’s intent is (not only to explain the word "vayamtzi'uhu," but primarily) to resolve a change in the language of the verses here. Above, regarding the offering of Aharon’s "sin-offering calf," it is stated, "And Aharon approached the altar and slaughtered... and the sons of Aharon brought the blood to him, and he dipped his finger in the blood and placed it on the horns of the altar..." Here, regarding the burnt-offering, the verse specifies, "(And he slaughtered the burnt-offering) and the sons of Aharon brought/presented (*vayamtzi'uhu*) the blood to him, and he dashed it..." (And also in the subsequent verse, the verse specifies, "And they presented the burnt-offering to him in its pieces..."). Similarly, regarding the "peace-offerings of the people," it is stated later, "And the sons of Aharon brought/presented the blood to him." This requires explanation.

## 55

Simply put, one could say that this was the reality: after receiving the blood of the sin-offering, the sons of Aharon were not standing in close proximity to Aharon, and therefore they had to offer (and bring) the blood to him. In contrast, the reception of the blood of the burnt-offering (and likewise the peace-offerings) took place in close proximity to Aharon, and they were able to hand the blood of the burnt-offering (and its limbs) to him.

However, while this explanation resolves the change in the language of the verses here, it still does not address the essence of the matter:

Above, at the beginning of Parshas Vayikra, regarding the laws of the burnt-offering, it is stated, "And he shall slaughter the young bull before Hashem, and the sons of Aharon the Kohanim shall offer the blood and dash the blood upon the altar..." Rashi there states regarding "vehikrivu" (and they shall offer): "This is the *kabbalah* (reception of the blood), which is the first [act], and it also implies *holachah* (bringing/transporting); we learn that both are performed by the sons of Aharon." That is to say: the term "vehikrivu" includes both the service of receiving the blood and the service of bringing the blood (since both are performed specifically by the sons of Aharon the Kohanim). Based on this, it is puzzling: since the verse here comes to describe the participation of the sons of Aharon in the service of the eighth day of the *milu'im*, why did the verse omit these two services (reception and bringing) regarding the burnt-offering (and the peace-offerings) and say only "vayamtzi'uhu... the blood," which means the handing over of the blood into Aharon’s hand?

Regarding "bringing" (*holachah*), one could say that according to the plain meaning of the verse, even the handing over of the blood (from hand to hand) is included in the category of "bringing," for by doing so, they bring the blood closer to the altar.

[Regarding the *halacha*, there is room for debate as to whether this handing over carries the name *holachah*, for we hold that *holachah* not performed by foot is not considered *holachah*, and according to the Rambam, even when standing next to the altar, *holachah* must be performed by foot (and in *Tzafnas Paneach* on the Torah, he writes that the fact that the sons of Aharon only "presented" the blood was indeed "not according to the law"). However, according to the plain meaning, we do not find that *holachah* must be performed specifically by foot, and since the entire matter of *holachah* is learned from the word "vehikrivu," it is logical that handing over is also included in this, since through it the blood is brought closer to the altar, as mentioned above.]

But this still does not resolve the issue regarding the reception of the blood, which, simply put, is not included in the term "vayamtzi'uhu." For only regarding the term "vehikrivu" did they say that "this is the *kabbalah* which is the first [act]," and

## 56

specifically, it can be said that the reception of the blood is also included in the meaning of the term "vehikrivu", because the reception of the blood is the beginning of "bringing" the blood to the altar. In contrast, "vayamtzi'uhu" (which is "an expression of handing over") is only the act of giving from hand to hand, and it is understood that the reception of the blood is not included in this. If so, it is puzzling why the verse chose the term "vayamtzi'uhu" (and omitted the matter of the reception of the blood) and did not say (as above) "vayakrivu," which includes both the reception and the bringing.

[Granted, if the Torah had not written at all about the actions between the slaughter and the dashing, it would not be difficult, as we find in other places that not all the services performed in a sacrifice were detailed, only some of them. But since the verse proceeds to detail that the sons of Aharon "presented" (*himtzi'u*) the blood to Aharon, it is difficult, as mentioned above, why it omitted the matter of the reception of the blood.]

To resolve this question, Rashi adds "(an expression of handing over) and preparation," thereby emphasizing the innovation in "vayamtzi'uhu" compared to "vayakrivu," that specifically in this there is the element of preparation, as follows.

C. The verses here explicitly state that there is a difference between the dashing of the blood of the sin-offering and the dashing of the blood of the burnt-offering and the peace-offerings. Regarding the sin-offering, it is stated, "And he shall dip his finger in the blood and place it on the horns of the altar," whereas regarding the burnt-offering and peace-offerings, it is stated simply, "and he shall dash it upon the altar." That is to say, the dashing of the blood of the sin-offering is with the finger, and therefore the Kohen must first dip his finger in the blood and then dash it, whereas the dashing of the blood of the burnt-offering and peace-offerings is with a vessel, and there is no need to dip the finger in the blood.

It follows that there is a fundamental difference between the action of the sons of Aharon with the blood of the sin-offering and their action with the blood of the burnt-offering and peace-offerings: With the blood of the sin-offering, even after they brought the blood to Aharon, the blood was not yet ready and qualified for dashing, because the act of Aharon dipping his finger in the blood was still missing. In contrast, with the blood of the burnt-offering and peace-offerings, immediately after they handed the blood to Aharon, the blood became ready and prepared for dashing, without Aharon needing to do anything further.

Based on this, the reason for the term "vayamtzi'uhu the sons of Aharon..." specifically (and not "vayakrivu") is explained: The reason the Torah recounts the actions of the sons of Aharon in these sacrifices is (simply put) to emphasize their participation in Aharon’s service on this day. Therefore, the verse emphasizes that regarding the burnt-offering and peace-offerings, the degree of their participation in Aharon’s service was greater, for not only did they "bring" the blood, but they "presented" (*vayamtzi'uhu*) it—the innovation in this being not only in the manner of the action

## 57

(vayamtzi'uhu is an expression of handing over), but primarily regarding what was effected in the blood: that with the blood of the burnt-offering, the sons of Aharon performed an additional thing, that the blood would be completely ready and prepared for dashing.

This is also the emphasis of the verse regarding the limbs of the burnt-offering: "And the burnt-offering they presented to him in its pieces," meaning, the sons of Aharon cut the burnt-offering into pieces in such a way that immediately upon the pieces reaching Aharon, they were ready for him to "burn it upon the altar."

(From a Sicha of Shabbos Parshas Shemini, 5742)

## Sicha 2

### 58

A. In our Parsha, regarding the verse "And you shall not touch their carcass," Rashi explains: "One might think that Yisroel are warned against touching a carcass; therefore the verse states 'Speak to the Kohanim, etc.'—the Kohanim are warned, but Yisroel are not warned. It follows *a fortiori*: if regarding the impurity of the dead, which is severe, only the Kohanim are warned, then regarding the impurity of a carcass, which is light, is it not all the more so? Why then does the verse state 'You shall not touch'? [It refers to] the festival (and afterwards he concludes, according to the version before us, 'This is what they said: A person is obligated to purify himself on the festival', as will be explained below)."

The source for this Rashi is the Toras Kohanim on the verse and the Gemara in Rosh Hashanah. However, it is well known that it is not Rashi’s custom to copy the teachings of our Sages, etc., and where he does bring them, it is because they are necessary for the *peshuto shel mikra* (the literal meaning of the verse). This is especially true in our case, where Rashi does not preface his words with "Our Sages said" or similar, which proves that his interpretation is founded entirely upon the literal meaning of the verse.

The necessity for his interpretation here, in terms of the literal meaning, is understood from Rashi’s own words: It has been discussed many times that usually, Rashi does not present the difficulty in the verse; rather, he explains the meaning of the verse according to its literal sense, and the difficulty is resolved automatically. But in our case, Rashi also copies the initial assumption (*kasa d'aminah*): "One might think that Yisroel are warned against touching a carcass, etc."

This is because the content of the verses in this Parsha speaks of warnings to all of Yisroel, and regarding this verse itself, it says "You shall not eat of their flesh" without qualification—meaning all of Yisroel. Therefore, based on the literal reading of the verses, we would interpret that all of Yisroel are warned against touching a carcass, were it not for the necessity from another verse, "Speak to the Kohanim," that Yisroel are not warned regarding [that] impurity.

[Based on this, it is also understood why Rashi did not interpret it like the Ramban, who says: "It is not a warning to forbid us the touching, but rather it says 'And you shall not touch their carcass, for they are impure to you'—meaning, do not touch them unless you become impure. The matter is to say that anyone who touches them should consider himself impure and be careful regarding the Mikdash and the Kodshim." This is because the literal reading of the verse "And you shall not touch their carcass" implies that this is a warning just like "You shall not eat of their flesh."]

Therefore, Rashi must explain and specify that

## 59

this verse, even though its content is a warning to all of Yisroel, is not a command for all time, but only for a specific time—the festival.

However, we must understand: What is the source (and the reason) according to the literal meaning of the verse that the warning of "And you shall not touch their carcass" applies specifically during the festival?

It is true that in the Gemara and Toras Kohanim (the source of his interpretation) the reason for this is not stated. But the entire purpose of Rashi is to interpret the Scripture (and not to copy the language of the Sages as it is [as mentioned above]). Furthermore, we find many times that even when Rashi explicitly writes "Our Sages said" or "It is in the Toras Kohanim" or similar, he is not satisfied with bringing the language of the Sages (or its content)—rather, he brings it with an added explanation or similar, so that it will be understood according to the literal meaning of the verse.

B. The commentators on Rashi write the reason that "You shall not touch—on the festival" is "because all of Yisroel are obligated then to bring an *Olas Re'iyah* (an offering of appearance) and must enter the *Azarah* (Temple courtyard), which is not the case during the rest of the year."

However, based on this, it is not understood why Rashi wrote simply "on the festival"—meaning everywhere and for every person—and does not explain, or even hint, that it is due to the obligation of bringing a sacrifice (i.e., only regarding one who is obligated and able to bring a sacrifice).

Seemingly, one could say that since it was already stated above "And they shall not appear before Me empty-handed," and Rashi explains there that this is the command to bring an *Olas Re'iyah*, one could say that the warning to purify oneself on the festival is because of the obligation to bring the *Olas Re'iyah* which is already known, since at that time they must enter the *Azarah*.

But it still requires explanation: If the command of "You shall not touch" is for the sake of bringing the sacrifice, then logic dictates that this warning should not apply to the entire duration of the festival, because after entering the *Azarah* and bringing the *Olas Re'iyah*, he no longer has an obligation to be careful about the impurity of touching a carcass—yet the literal meaning of the word "on the festival" implies all the days of the festival.

Also: If the warning of impurity is only because of the obligation of bringing a sacrifice on the festival, what is different about this festival sacrifice compared to all other sacrifices? This obligation to be careful about impurity applies to all sacrifices that Bnei Yisroel bring (and eat from)—which were already discussed in the previous Parshiyos (Vayikra and Tzav: *Shelamim* offering, *Chatas*, etc.). If so, why does Rashi interpret and limit it, according to the literal meaning, that the warning of "And you shall not touch their carcass" is only "on the festival"?

And even if you were to say that this special warning comes only regarding a sacrifice that has a fixed time—the festival—unlike other sacrifices that do not have a fixed time, for which there is no command to be careful about impurity (for when must one be careful?), but rather it is included in the laws and obligations of bringing the sacrifice (that one may not offer (or eat) it unless he is pure)—there are many, many sacrifices that have a fixed time for their offering, such as the sacrifice of a *Metzora* on the eighth day of his purification, or the sacrifices of a woman after childbirth, which come after her days of purity. And even if we were to distinguish that these sacrifices also do not have a fixed time during the days of the year like the festival sacrifice, and therefore it is not as appropriate to warn about them—at the very least, this applies to the *Korban Pesach*, which comes on the 14th of Nissan in the afternoon, before the festival (about which they had already learned), for which he must be careful about impurity beforehand so that he can offer his sacrifice.

Therefore, it must be that even for the *Korban Pesach* (and all the more so for other sacrifices), it is not logical to require a special warning not to become impure because of the obligation of entering the Mikdash and offering sacrifices, because a person is already warned about this by virtue of these obligations.

But for this same reason, there is no need for a verse even regarding the festival—that "And you shall not touch their carcass"—because on the festival, he is commanded and stands ready to enter the *Azarah* and bring sacrifices, for all of which he must be pure.

C. Furthermore, and most importantly: According to the interpretation that "And you shall not touch their carcass—on the festival" is because all of Yisroel are obligated then to bring an *Olas Re'iyah*, it turns out that this warning is only at a time when the Beis Hamikdash exists and for those who are in Eretz Yisroel (and in Jerusalem).

If you were to say that this is Rashi’s intent—he should not have used the general term "on the festival" (and relied on the verse before it, "And they shall not appear before Me empty-handed"—as mentioned above), but rather added one word: "when they go up for the festival" or similar, so that it would be clear that the prohibition is only (in Eretz Yisroel and) at a time when the Beis Hamikdash exists [ftnref_508_20, 509_21].

Especially since the beginning of this verse, "You shall not eat of their flesh," is a command that applies in every place and at every time; surely Rashi should have explained in clear language (so that the distinction between the first and second half of the verse would be understood)—that even though "You shall not eat of their flesh" is a warning at every time and in every place, the second half of the verse, "And you shall not touch their carcass," is a warning only at the time of the Temple.

Since he uses the general term "on the festival," it is understood that this command relates to the time of the festival (and not all days of the year), but it applies in all places and all festivals, even when there is no Temple.

—It can be said that Rashi, in his commentary on the Torah, follows his own approach in his commentary on the Shas: In the Gemara it says: "An engaged woman does not mourn and does not become impure for him, and likewise he does not mourn and does not become impure for her." Rashi explains: "And does not become impure—she does not need to busy herself with him, etc. That is what I heard, but to me it seems: 'And does not become impure'—for example, on the festival, when Yisroel are warned about impurity from 'And you shall not touch their carcass'". From this it is understood that the caution against impurity on the festival is not because of entering the Mikdash, going up for the festival, and bringing an *Olas Re'iyah*—for women are not obligated in these, as they are time-bound positive commandments. Furthermore: the fact that the engaged woman "does not become impure for him" is simply equivalent to "and does not become impure" regarding the engaged Kohen, and the warning of impurity of the dead that applies to the Kohen is not only in Eretz Yisroel but also abroad, and even when there is no Temple. If so, it is understood that this too, that the engaged woman "does not become impure for him" (due to the obligation to purify oneself on the festival), applies also abroad and even when there is no Temple.

D. Based on this, we can explain the conclusion in Rashi (in most printed editions before us)—"This is what they said: A person is obligated to purify himself on the festival." Its source is in Rosh Hashanah there : "Rabbi Yitzchak said: A person is obligated to purify himself on the festival, as it is said, 'And you shall not touch their carcass,'" regarding which the Shas brings the *Baraisa* that was brought in Rashi, that "And you shall not touch their carcass" refers to "the festival."

Seemingly, this is puzzling: Rashi’s purpose in his commentary on the Torah is not to explain and interpret the statements of our Sages in the Shas.

Moreover: In the Gemara there, it is implied that these are two separate matters: 1) A warning against touching a carcass on the festival; 2) An obligation to purify oneself on the festival. For the statement of Rabbi Yitzchak that "a person is obligated to purify himself on the festival" is a precision and a novelty derived from the verse (not just a negation of touching, but also an obligation to purify), which is not implied by the *Baraisa*. And the proof that the Gemara brings from the *Baraisa* is (seemingly) only that the verse "And you shall not touch their carcass" refers to the festival, and not to the actual novelty of Rabbi Yitzchak that "a person is obligated, etc.".

But from Rashi’s language, "This is what they said: A person is obligated to purify himself on the festival," it is implied that it is the same matter and the same teaching.

And based on the above, it can be said:

## 62

1) With this, Rashi comes to prove that this matter applies at all times and not only at the time of the Temple, for "this is what they said" is general. Furthermore, this statement of our Sages (by Rabbi Yitzchak) was said after the destruction.

2) With this, he emphasizes that the caution against impurity and the obligation to purify are one and the same:
If you were to say that the warning "And you shall not touch their carcass" is because of entering the Mikdash and offering sacrifices, there would be room to say that these are two different matters, as mentioned above—that because of the obligation to go up for the festival and offer sacrifices, he is warned not to make himself impure, but this does not compel him to purify himself if he has already become impure, for since he is impure, one could say that the obligation of going up for the festival and bringing sacrifices has lapsed for him.
(Except that Rabbi Yitzchak adds the novelty that from "And you shall not touch their carcass" on the festival, we derive a second law: that a person is obligated (not only to be careful about impurity but also) to purify himself on the festival.)

But according to Rashi, the obligation is not because of entering the Mikdash and bringing sacrifices, but rather that because of the festival, he is warned not to make himself impure (and even at this time, as mentioned above)—then it is understood that the warning in "You shall not touch" is not only a negative matter, but rather that the festival obligates him to be in a state of purity, and therefore, even if he became impure, he must purify himself.

Therefore, Rashi specifies, "This is what they said: A person is obligated to purify himself on the festival"—because the content of this warning, "And you shall not touch their carcass—on the festival," is (also) the obligation to purify himself on the festival.

E. However, it still requires explanation: What is the source and the necessity to interpret, according to the literal meaning, that on the festival Yisroel are warned about impurity because of the festival and not because of the obligations of sacrifices within it ?

## 63

The Rambam wrote: "All of Yisroel are warned to be pure on every festival because they are prepared to enter the Mikdash and eat Kodshim, and this is what is said in the Torah, 'And you shall not touch their carcass'—on the festival only." The *Acharonim* wrote that from the precision of the Rambam’s language, "because they are prepared to enter, etc.," and not "because they are obligated to enter," "that they should be ready to enter," and the like, it is implied that the fact that Yisroel are warned to be pure on every festival is not for the reason that they might be able to enter the Mikdash (and bring sacrifices), but rather that this is a revelation regarding the time of the obligation: since this is a time when they are obligated to enter the Mikdash, it follows that they must be pure then, which we do not require at another time. Therefore, our Sages said that this warning, "And you shall not touch their carcass," is at the time of the festival.

But it is difficult to explain this in Rashi, who simply wrote "on the festival" and did not explain this reason, relying on the fact that the matter is self-understood! Moreover, this reason is only the logic for what our Sages said, that this warning is "on the festival"; but the main question remains in place—what is the source in the literal meaning of the verse that there is an obligation of purity on the festival?

To add to this: It is understandable later in Parshas Re'eh, when it is stated (in the verse) "And you shall not touch their carcass" and Rashi repeats the content of his interpretation here, that there Rashi prefaces, "Our Sages explained: on the festival, that a person is obligated to purify himself on the festival, etc."—meaning that the source for this interpretation is from "our Sages" (and not the literal meaning of the verse). Unlike here in our Parsha, where he does not write this preface but writes simply "One might think, etc.—on the festival"—it must be that there is a source in the literal meaning of the verse to say that there is an obligation of purity on the festival.

F. It can be said that Rashi relies on what he already explained above:
In Parshas Bo, the first time the matter of *Yom Tov* is mentioned in the Torah, that it is a "holy convocation" (*mikra kodesh*)—Rashi explains: "*Mikra* is a noun; he called it holy for eating, drinking, and clothing."

It follows that the holiness of *Yom Tov* is connected with a person’s eating, drinking, and clothing. Since this is so, it is understood that a person must be in a state of purity, for how is it possible that his eating and drinking, etc., in a state of impurity could sanctify the day?

Based on this, we learn—according to the literal meaning of the verse—several innovations in Halacha regarding the obligation to purify oneself on the festival:
According to the reason that the obligation to purify oneself on the festival is because of the sacrifices that one is obligated to

## 64

bring, the *Acharonim* wrote that because of the obligation of *Olas Re'iyah* and *Shelamim Chagigah*, one is only warned not to become impure with the prolonged impurity of the dead, which is a seven-day impurity. But regarding an impurity of one day, such as a creeping thing or a carcass, he is not warned. And even if he has not yet brought the *Olas Re'iyah* and *Shelamim Chagigah*, if he is still before the seventh day of the festival, he is permitted to become impure, since there is still time to purify himself and bring them before the festival ends, as they have a makeup period for all seven days. But because of the *Shelamim Simcha* (peace-offerings of joy), which he is obligated to eat throughout all the days of the festival, and there is no day of the festival on which he is not obligated in them, he cannot make himself impure on any of the days of the festival.

According to this, it turns out: (a) That the obligation to purify oneself is for all seven days of the festival, including *Chol Hamoed*. (b) On Rosh Hashanah and Yom Kippur, which do not have an obligation of going up for the festival and offering sacrifices, the warning of "And you shall not touch their carcass" does not apply. (c) After he has finished bringing all the festival sacrifices (on the seventh day), he no longer has an obligation to be pure until the very end of the festival (the seventh day).

But according to Rashi (that the obligation to purify oneself on the festival relates to the obligation of "holy convocation"—to sanctify it with eating, drinking, and clothing—which is connected with the very holiness of the *Yom Tov*), it is understood that:
a) Since *Chol Hamoed* is not called a "holy convocation", there is no obligation according to the literal meaning of the verse to purify oneself on *Chol Hamoed*.
b) Since Rosh Hashanah and Yom Kippur are also called "holy convocation" —they too have the obligation to purify oneself on the festival.
c) Since it is connected with the very holiness of the *Yom Tov*—it is understood that it must be for the entire time of the festival. And even at a time when he is not eating and drinking—because of the obligation of (sanctifying it with) clothing, he must still be pure all day.

G. The inner dimension of Torah in Rashi:
Seemingly, it appears that the matter of purity according to the approach that the obligation to purify oneself on the festival is

## 65

because of the obligation of going up for the festival, etc., is on a higher level than the matter of purity according to Rashi’s approach. For according to Rashi’s approach mentioned above, the purity is not connected with the Mikdash and its Kodshim, but with the matters and needs of the person: eating, drinking, and clothing.

But in the inner dimension of the matter, it can be said that, on the contrary:
If the obligation to purify oneself on the festival is because of the Mikdash and its Kodshim, then there is no drawing down of a new matter in this purity, but only the preparation of the person to receive this holiness of the Mikdash and Kodshim that exists on the festival.
Unlike if the purity is because of eating, drinking, and clothing, it turns out that there is a new matter here: that even though these things (eating, drinking, etc.) are not holy by their own nature, the person acts and draws down (purity and) holiness even into them. This means that even matters of permission become (permeated with) the purity and holiness of the festival.

And this is the inner content of the festival, which is called "holy convocation," unlike Shabbos, which is called "holy"—for on Shabbos the holiness is holiness by its own nature. Unlike *Yom Tov*, where we call and invite the supernal holiness to be drawn down below. This is what is meant by "Shabbos is sanctified and established", that it is a holy day separated from the six days of creation. Unlike *Yom Tov*, where "Yisroel are the ones who sanctify the times", that Bnei Yisroel make a weekday into a holy day (*Yom Tov*).

It can be said that this is also the reason in the inner dimension of the matters: that the obligation of purity because of the Mikdash and Kodshim is only at the time of the Temple, unlike the obligation of purity because of the "holy convocation" of *Yom Tov* in eating, drinking, and clothing, which is an obligation at all times, even in the time of exile—
For this is one of the elevations of the *avodah* during the time of exile: that we draw down and reveal Divine holiness even outside the boundary of holiness, abroad, and even lower, into the concealment and darkness of the exile.

And through the addition of purity by each and every one of Yisroel, even in the mundane and permissible matters of a person, "the purity of the body leads to the holiness of the soul from evil thoughts, and the holiness of the soul causes one to resemble the Shechinah, as it is said : 'And you shall sanctify yourselves and you shall be holy, for I, Hashem, am holy, who sanctifies you'".

(From the Sicha of Shabbos Parshas Shemini, 5735)

## Sicha 3

1. The Rambam writes at the end of Hilchos Maachalos Asuros — after listing the things that the Sages prohibited because they fall under the category of "Do not make your souls detestable" — "And anyone who is careful in these matters brings extra holiness and purity to his soul and cleanses his soul for the sake of the Holy One, blessed be He, as it is said: 'And you shall sanctify yourselves and you shall be holy, for I am holy.'"

On the face of it, the Rambam’s source is this very verse, for the text "And you shall sanctify yourselves and you shall be holy" follows the prohibition "Do not make your souls detestable," and serves as its rationale, as the verse begins, "For I am Hashem your G-d, and you shall sanctify yourselves..."

However, this requires understanding: The plain meaning of the Rambam’s language, "And anyone who is careful in these matters," implies that he refers (not to all the prohibitions in Hilchos Maachalos Asuros, but only) to those matters listed immediately beforehand. This means that the "extra holiness and purity" comes through the caution exercised regarding "these matters" that the Sages prohibited due to the prohibition of "Do not make your souls detestable." It is puzzling: why did the Rambam bypass the prohibition of "Do not make your souls detestable" that is explicit in the Torah—"with any swarming thing that swarms" (the prohibition of eating insects)—and instead cite the prohibitions of the Sages that (also) fall under the category of "Do not make your souls detestable"?

Furthermore, the order of the levels listed by the Rambam is unclear—"brings extra holiness and purity to his soul and cleanses his soul for the sake of the Holy One, blessed be He." That is, first "holiness," then "purity," and only at the end "cleanses his soul." Seemingly, he should have used the reverse order, from the easier to the more difficult: first the cleansing of the soul (which implies the removal of filth), then "extra purity" (which implies not just purity from impurity, but an addition of purity, similar to the five immersions of the Kohen Gadol on Yom Kippur), and then "holiness," which is higher than purity.

## 68

These matters should not be observed (merely) because it is appropriate for a person to behave this way (due to the nature of his body), but because "anyone who is careful in these matters brings extra holiness and purity to his soul and cleanses his soul for the sake of the Holy One, blessed be He," meaning that this touches upon the holiness and purity of the soul.

Even though physical cleanliness is a secondary matter, seemingly (and especially regarding spiritual cleanliness and purity)—as is also explained in the Moreh Nevuchim that "the cleanliness of clothes and the washing of the body and the removal of filth are also among the goals of this Torah, but only after the purification of deeds and the purification of the heart from defiling outlooks and defiling character traits... the primary goal is the restriction of desires, and the cleanliness of the exterior follows the cleanliness of the interior"—nevertheless, "the cleanliness of the exterior" also touches upon "the cleanliness of the interior," because by being careful in these matters, a person "brings extra holiness and purity to his soul" (because physical cleanliness prepares a person and makes him a more suitable vessel for the spirituality and refinement of his soul).

Based on this, we can also explain the reason the Rambam listed the levels in reverse order—holiness, purity, and "cleanses his soul":

In this, the Rambam hints that not only does "the cleanliness of the exterior" touch upon "the cleanliness of the interior," but furthermore, as a person ascends to a higher level of holiness, a greater degree of cleanliness is required of him. Similar to the concept of "a Torah scholar upon whose garment a stain is found," or "of builders (Torah scholars) on one side and of a simpleton on both sides," from which it is understood that when one ascends to a higher level as a "Torah scholar" ("holiness"), a greater cleanliness ("purity") is required of him, even regarding matters that previously would not have been considered a "stain" for him.

This is the Rambam’s intent in his precise wording, "brings extra holiness and purity to his soul and cleanses his soul..."—that upon reaching a state of extra holiness, the soul requires a renewed state of "purity," and furthermore, it must "cleanse its soul."

## 69

However, one must still understand the precision of the Rambam’s language, "(and cleanses his soul) for the sake of the Holy One, blessed be He"—what is the intent behind this addition?

Also, explanation is needed for several differences between the Rambam’s language here and his language at the end of Hilchos Tumas Ochlin:

a) In Hilchos Tumas Ochlin, the Rambam defines the concepts of "purity" and "holiness"—"purity of the body from evil deeds... holiness of the soul from evil opinions," whereas here (in Hilchos Maachalos Asuros, which precedes Hilchos Tumas Ochlin), he simply says "brings extra holiness and purity." Conversely, here he adds the word "extra."

b) Here, the Rambam specifies, as mentioned above, "(and cleanses his soul) for the sake of the Holy One, blessed be He," whereas at the end of Hilchos Tumas Ochlin, he writes "to resemble the Shechinah."

c) In both places, the Rambam was not satisfied with citing the verse "And you shall sanctify yourselves and you shall be holy," but also brought the end of the verse, "for I am holy." However, he did so with a variation.

## 70

The explanation of the matter:

The fact that a person serves Hashem in other areas of Torah and mitzvos (both Torah-ordained and Rabbinic) and performs them because Hashem commanded so, is still not clear proof that his service is truly "for its own sake" (*lishmah*), where he does not think about himself but only "for the sake of the Holy One, blessed be He." For it is possible that the reason he performs a certain mitzvah "for its own sake" is because he has not yet reached the point where he feels the necessity for the benefit of the mitzvah, and therefore he performs the matter because that is what Hashem decreed. This is certainly true regarding the performance of *chukim* (statutes), whose performance is only because "I have enacted a statute, I have issued a decree," but even regarding mitzvos that have a reason and benefit, since they are not matters of necessity (from the perspective of his bodily nature), it is possible that he still does not feel their necessity and benefit, and performs them only "for the sake of the Holy One, blessed be He."

Only when, even in these matters where caution is required due to the nature of his body, he is careful because the Sages so commanded, is it because he is entirely surrendered and given over to the will of Hashem, and his entire service in all his affairs is only "for the sake of the Holy One, blessed be He" alone.

It can be said that this is also the depth of the Rambam’s intent in writing "and cleanses his soul for the sake of the Holy One, blessed be He"—that his intent is not a cleansing from bad things, but that the person purifies himself from any feeling of "selfhood" and "existence," until in his service it is felt that he does everything only "for the sake of the Holy One, blessed be He."

## 70 (continued)

Based on this, we can add an explanation to the words of the Rambam in the aforementioned Moreh Nevuchim, that "the cleanliness of the exterior (comes) after the cleanliness of the interior" ("after the purification of deeds and the purification of the heart..."). The meaning of this is that the distinction between "cleanliness of the interior" and "cleanliness of the exterior" is not only a distinction between primary and secondary, but also involves a matter of precedence and sequence, that the "goal of the Torah" regarding "cleanliness of the exterior" is fulfilled only when it comes after "cleanliness of the interior."

Seemingly, "cleanliness of the exterior" prepares a person to be a more suitable vessel for spirituality (as mentioned above), and as is explicit in the *Baraisa d'Rabbi Pinchas ben Yair* mentioned above, "cleanliness leads to *perishus* (abstinence/separation)."

According to what we have said, it can be explained that the Rambam’s intent here is the "goal of the Torah" in the command regarding these matters. The perfection of the goal is when the caution in these matters is not only for the sake of cleanliness in practice (which is required even by human nature), and not even so that the person will be a better vessel for spiritual perfection ("cleanliness of the interior"), but that even "cleanliness of the exterior" will be solely because of Hashem’s command (through the Sages of the Torah). This feeling comes only after "cleanliness of the interior," for when can one feel regarding physical and natural matters that he does them only because of Hashem’s command? Only after he possesses "cleanliness of the interior," an absolute purity from undesirable opinions and character traits, etc., at which point it is possible for him to reach the "goal of the Torah" in "cleanliness of the exterior."

## 71

This is also the explanation of the difference between the Rambam’s words at the end of Hilchos Tumas Ochlin and his words here at the end of Hilchos Maachalos Asuros:

Eating *chullin* (non-sacred food) in a state of purity (which is what is discussed at the end of Hilchos Tumas Ochlin) is the matter of *perishus*, which is the beginning of service. Regarding this, the Rambam writes that through this *perishus*, a person will ascend from strength to strength: "*Perishus* leads to purity of the body from evil deeds, and purity of the body leads to holiness of the soul from evil opinions, and holiness of the soul causes one to resemble the Shechinah" (and therefore the Rambam details the concepts of the levels of purity and holiness).

But here, at the end of Hilchos Maachalos Asuros, the discussion is about the level of "cleanliness of the exterior" that comes after "cleanliness of the interior," meaning after he has already purified his body from evil deeds and sanctified his soul from evil opinions (and as per the Rambam’s precise language here, "brings extra holiness and purity to his soul"), and furthermore, that he has even reached the point of "resembling the Shechinah"—nevertheless, after all these levels, it is possible that his service is not "for the sake of the Holy One, blessed be He" alone, but because of the benefit to himself, as he wants to reach perfection until he is "like the Shechinah."

But the perfection of service is when he "cleanses his soul (only) for the sake of the Holy One, blessed be He," without thinking about his own perfection; the complete proof of this is specifically when this is the manner of his caution in those matters that the Sages prohibited due to "Do not make your souls detestable," as explained at length.

With this, the difference regarding the citation of the end of the verse is also understood: The Rambam’s intent here in bringing the end of the verse, "for I am holy," is not that the person sanctifies himself because he wants "to resemble the Shechinah" (as this is not mentioned at all by the Rambam here), but that in his service, only the concept of "for I am holy" (the holiness of the Holy One, blessed be He, which is above the holiness of man, "And you shall sanctify yourselves and you shall be holy") is felt—"for the sake of the Holy One, blessed be He."

Therefore, the Rambam did not bring here "(for I am holy) Hashem who sanctifies you" (as he brought in Hilchos Tumas Ochlin), because "who sanctifies you" means that Hashem sanctifies the person, and the Rambam’s intent here (in bringing the end of the verse) is not an addition of holiness to the person, but that his service is in the manner of "cleanses his soul for the sake of the Holy One, blessed be He" (and not for the sake of his own perfection).

[Unlike in Hilchos Tumas Ochlin, where he brings the verse as proof that "holiness of the soul causes one to resemble the Shechinah," therefore he also brought the end, "for I am holy, Hashem who sanctifies you," for through Hashem sanctifying the person with His holiness, the person becomes "like the Shechinah."]

## 71 (continued)

Based on all the above, one can explain more deeply the reason the Rambam placed holiness before purity (even though, in general, holiness is higher than purity):

As a person draws closer to the Holy One, blessed be He, he becomes more nullified from his own existence. Just as when standing before a king, the very fact that a person feels himself to be an "existence" and is not in a state of total nullification before him is considered a rebellion against the king. As the saying goes, even one who "gestures with his hand... before the king" is liable for death. So it is in the service of Hashem; specifically when a person ascends in his service and feels himself as standing before the King of Kings, the Holy One, blessed be He, he then feels the need to cleanse himself and purify himself more and more from any feeling of existence.

This is hinted at in the words of the Rambam, that "anyone who is careful in these matters brings extra holiness and purity to his soul and cleanses his soul for the sake of the Holy One, blessed be He," for as he draws closer to the Holy One, blessed be He ("holiness"), he feels the necessity to purify and cleanse his soul from his "existence," until he reaches a state where all his affairs are only "for the sake of the Holy One, blessed be He."


## אייר 

## 72

### Iyar

1. It is stated in the sefarim that "Iyar" is an acronym for "Ani Hashem Rofecha" (I am Hashem your healer), meaning that this month is auspicious for the matter of healing.

Now, regarding healing itself, there are several modes. Here, it is emphasized that the healing of "Iyar" pertains to "Ani Hashem Rofecha"—the healing of the Ribono shel Olam specifically. The novelty of this healing is explicitly stated in the verse: "All the sickness... I will not place upon you, for I am Hashem your healer." This means that the Ribono shel Olam prevents the sickness from coming upon the person in the first place (similar to the words of the Rambam, that if a person is as careful as required with his diet, etc., according to the conduct explained in his Hilchos Deos, "I guarantee him that he will not come to sickness all his days").

It should be added that the novelty of the Ribono shel Olam’s healing is not only that it prevents the arrival of sickness, but that even when a person’s conduct has caused sickness to come upon him, when his healing comes from "Ani Hashem Rofecha," it is in an entirely different manner than the healing of a human physician (even though the healing of a human physician also comes from the Ribono shel Olam, who gave "permission (and power) to the physician to heal").

The healing of a human being is effective (generally) only "from now on" [as explained in the Gemara regarding the difference between the annulment of vows by a Chacham, which "uproots the vow from its inception," and the healing of a blemish by a physician, which "heals only from now on"]. Not so the healing of the Ribono shel Olam (since its mode is "I will not place upon you [from the outset]"), which uproots the sickness as if it had never existed at all.

Furthermore: In Tzafenas Paneach it is explained that even in the healing of a human being, "there are two types of healing: one from now on... and one retroactively." But nevertheless, there is a novelty in the healing of the Ribono shel Olam even regarding healing that is "retroactive." For the fact that there can be healing (by a human) that acts "retroactively" is only because "the healing is merely to clarify"—meaning, that through the healing it is clarified that there was no blemish from the start. But if

## 73

he actually had a blemish, it is impossible for a human to uproot the blemish "retroactively". Not so the healing of the Ribono shel Olam in the manner of "Ani Hashem Rofecha"—since its mode is "I will not place upon you," even when sickness has already come upon the person, the healing uproots it as if it had not been placed upon the person from the outset.

And since "Iyar" is an acronym for "Ani Hashem Rofecha," it is understood that the quality of this month regarding healing is in the manner of "Ani Hashem Rofecha," which uproots every matter of sickness from its inception.

2. This will be understood by first noting the reason why, in the Five Books of Torah, the months are called by their ordinal number (the month of Nissan—the first month, the month of Iyar—the second month, etc.)—and the Torah did not establish a name for each and every month.

One of the reasons that can be said is that this hints that beyond the special content that each month has in its own right (as explained in many sefarim), the twelve months of the year are (also) one reality and one continuity. Therefore, the months are called by their ordinal number, which emphasizes that all the months constitute one continuity (and simply put—when one says "second," it emphasizes that it comes in continuation of the "first," etc.).

From this it is understood that the subject of each month is (also) a preparation and introduction to the subject of the month that follows it (and in continuation of it).

[It can be said that this is the (inner) reason why Shabbos Mevarchim Hachodesh is always—even when Rosh Chodesh falls on Shabbos—(the last Shabbos) of the preceding month, to indicate that the "blessing" and the empowerment for the avodah of the coming month comes from the month that preceded it.]

Based on this, it can be said regarding our subject—the month of Iyar, which is the second month coming in continuation of the first month, the month of Nissan (and is blessed from it)—that the fact that the healing of the month of Iyar is in the manner of "Ani Hashem Rofecha," the healing of

## 74

the Ribono shel Olam specifically, is because it comes after and in continuation of the month of Nissan:

The subject of the month of Nissan is the "month of redemption", and the intent here is a miraculous redemption, which is above nature. Like the redemption from Mitzrayim in the simple sense, which came through miracles and wonders that were entirely above nature (unlike the redemption of Purim in the month of Adar, which was through a miracle clothed in nature). And as is hinted also in the name "Nissan"—from the word "nes" (miracle), and within that itself—two *nun*s, which indicate "miracles of miracles". This is because the redemption from Mitzrayim came through the fact that "the King of Kings, the Ribono shel Olam, revealed Himself to them (in His glory and essence) and redeemed them," and therefore the redemption came without any clothing in the ways of nature at all.

This is the continuity between the two months of Nissan and Iyar—that the month of Nissan, the first month, empowers the month of Iyar that follows it, which is auspicious for the matter of healing (as mentioned above), so that the healing will come in the manner of "Ani Hashem Rofecha", the healing of the Ribono shel Olam in His glory and essence, which is a healing that is above nature, uprooting the sickness as if it had never existed in him at all.

3. It is known that just as there is sickness and healing of the body, so too there is sickness and healing of the soul, as explained at length in the Eight Chapters of the Rambam (in the name of the ancients). Based on this, it can be said that the essential matter of "Ani Hashem Rofecha" of the month of Iyar is in connection to the healing of the soul, and from this it branches out and descends afterwards to be auspicious also for the healing of the body.

[Especially according to what is known that the Torah speaks in the "upper realms" and hints in the "lower realms," meaning that the matters of the Torah speak first and foremost "in the upper realms" (as they are interpreted in their spiritual content), and their simple interpretation (as the words of Torah are interpreted "in the lower realms," in this physical world) is only the "hint" within the Torah. Likewise in our case, when the Torah speaks about the matter of "healing," its intent is first and foremost for healing in its spiritual content ("in the upper realms").]

In this, the connection of the matter of healing to the month of Iyar is understood—according to what is explained in Midrash Shmuel, one of the reasons why we say Pirkei Avos between Pesach and Atzeres is because "from here the time begins to warm up and physical desires are awakened and the *yetzer hara* begins to rule and gain strength, and this tractate (Avos) is full of rebukes and awakens the person to pursue every good trait and strengthens the *yetzer hatov* over the *yetzer hara*." Thus, it is found that from the month of Iyar begins the essential necessity for the healing of the soul (since then there is a strengthening of the *yetzer hara*, etc.).

## 75

And according to what we said above regarding the healing of Iyar—(a) that it comes in the manner of "Ani Hashem Rofecha," and (b) that the power for this is from the month of Nissan (the first month)—it must be said that it is the same regarding the healing of the soul in the month of Iyar, which has these two details, namely: (a) in the month of Iyar there is a giving of power from above so that the healing of the soul will come to the person in the manner of "Ani Hashem Rofecha", above nature; (b) the power for this healing comes from the month of Nissan that precedes it.

4. This will be understood by first explaining the difference between the month of Nissan and the month of Iyar in the avodah of the person:

Just as the month of Nissan is the "month of redemption" in the physical sense, so too it is the time of the redemption of Yisrael in the spiritual sense. As is known, the redemption from Mitzrayim, besides being a physical redemption—that Bnei Yisrael were redeemed from the land of Mitzrayim physically—was also a spiritual redemption, that Bnei Yisrael, while being slaves to Pharaoh, were sunk in the impurity of Mitzrayim, down to the forty-nine gates of impurity, and through the Ribono shel Olam in His glory and essence "revealing Himself to them... and redeeming them," they were redeemed from the impurity of Mitzrayim.

And this spiritual redemption was in the manner of "miracles of miracles," above nature, and not in any order or gradation at all—that the Ribono shel Olam elevated Yisrael above the impurity of Mitzrayim entirely.

And so it is every year when the month of Nissan arrives—that a person is obligated to see himself as though he went out of Mitzrayim, that in this month the power is given to each and every one of Yisrael to redeem himself from all undesirable matters, in such a way that he reaches the state of "free men," meaning, not only that he has the power to control his *yetzer* (and not the opposite, *chas v'shalom*), but furthermore, that he does not need to fight at all, because he is a "free man," in a state of freedom from the *yetzer hara*.
