And this spiritual redemption of the month of Nissan finds expression in the fact that it is the only month of all the months of the year in which we do not say *Tachanun* for all the days of the month. And as has been explained several times, it is impossible to say that on days when we do not say *Tachanun* (and the person does not ask for forgiveness for his sins), he is deprived of what is effected by the saying of *Tachanun* (namely, the pardon and forgiveness of sins)—for if so: precisely because one is in an auspicious day in which *Tachanun* is not said, the person would remain with his sins without pardon and forgiveness! Rather, it is certain that on days when we do not say *Tachanun*, the forgiveness of sins is effected by the quality of the day itself (without the person needing to ask for it). And this is why we do not say *Tachanun* throughout the entire month of Nissan, because since it is the "month of redemption," in which the Ribono shel Olam redeems each and every one of Yisrael and elevates him above all undesirable matters until he is in a state of a "free man"—there is no need for the saying of *Tachanun*.

Thus, in the month of Nissan, one is not in need of the matter of "healing" (healing of the sickness of the soul),

## 76

for one is in a state of a "free man" and above any category of the matter of sickness.

5. However, the subject of the month of Iyar is at the other extreme—both in the physical sense, for then begins the time of the strengthening of nature, meaning not only is it not a time of "miracles of miracles," but on the contrary—it is a time of the strengthening of nature, as we see tangibly that it is the time of the growth of trees, etc., and all the powers of nature are seen to be in their full strength, etc.; and in the spiritual sense (as mentioned above), for then is the time of the strengthening of the *yetzer* and one needs special power (starting with the study of Pirkei Avos) in order to overcome the *yetzer*, etc. (and it is understood that one thing depends on the other, that because it is a time of the strengthening of the power of nature in the world, this leads to the strengthening of the *yetzer* and the warmth and arousal of desires, etc.).

And this is the "blessing" that the month of Iyar is blessed with from the month of Nissan that precedes it—that a proper blessing has two matters: (a) the content of the blessing is in accordance with the power of the one who blesses, (b) and on the other hand, it is measured according to the needs of the recipient of the blessing (for otherwise there could be an influence of good that one cannot receive). And so in our case, the blessing of the month of Nissan is that it should be "Ani Hashem Rofecha," in which there are the two extremes: on one hand, this is a blessing in the matter of healing, which itself emphasizes that one is in a state of needing healing; but on the other hand, the mode of the healing is in accordance with the subject of the month of Nissan—"redemption" in a miraculous manner, above the nature of the world—the healing of the Ribono shel Olam that uproots the sickness entirely, as if it had never existed from the outset (as mentioned in section 1).

And simply put—this is an empowerment, that even in the month of Iyar the person can elevate himself above all his confusing matters, etc., until he is in a state of spiritual freedom.

(From the sicha of Shabbos Parshas Shemini, Mevarchim Hachodesh Iyar, 5747)


## תזריע

## Sicha 1

### 77

A. Regarding the laws of *nega'im* in our *parsha*, the text begins: "When a person has in the skin of his flesh a *se'es*, or a *sapachas*, or a *baheres*, and it becomes in the skin of his flesh a *nega tzaraas*," and it continues with the sign of white hair that renders one *tamei*, the laws of the first and second *hesger* (quarantine), and the spreading of the *nega*.

Afterward, it continues with a new *parsha*: "When a *nega tzaraas* shall be in a person, and he is brought to the *kohen*, and the *kohen* shall look, and behold, a white *se'es* in the skin, and it has turned white hair, and there is raw flesh in the *se'es*... and if it blossoms, blossoms... it has all turned white, he is *tahor*." Thus, in this (second) *parsha*, the law of the sign of *tumah* of "raw flesh" in the *nega* is added, as well as the law that "if it has all turned white, he is *tahor*."

This requires explanation: Why does the text begin the explanation of these laws in the style of a new subject—"When a *nega tzaraas* shall be in a person..."—when the introduction to the laws of *tzaraas* and many other laws have already been stated in our *parsha* previously ("When a person has..."), and here only further details of the laws of *tzaraas* are added? Furthermore, even in this case, it should have said (in contrast to the laws of the *maros* mentioned earlier)—"And if the *kohen* shall see, and behold, a *se'es*..." and the like. Regarding the other three laws of *nega'im* that follow—*shechin* (boil) and *michvah* (burn), as well as the *nega'im* of the head and beard (*nesek*)—it is understandable that it is appropriate to begin each of them as a new subject, since they concern different types of *nega'im*. However, in these two *parshiyos*, where both discuss the same type of *nega* (a *nega* in the skin of the flesh), what is the reason to interrupt between them and begin anew: "When a *nega tzaraas* shall be in a person..."?

In the *Toras Kohanim* it is explained that this verse ("*Nega tzaraas*...") comes to teach us that the sign of *michyah* (raw flesh) renders one *tamei* not only in the *marah* of *se'es* (as is explicit in the verse: "and behold, a white *se'es*... and raw flesh in the *se'es*"), but also in the other *maros*: "We have learned regarding *se'es* that it is *tamei* with *michyah*; from where do we include the other *maros*?... The text says *nega tzaraas*".

However, this teaching only resolves the redundancy of the words in the text ("*nega tzaraas*"), but it does not provide a reason for the style of the text, which implies that a new *parsha* regarding the laws of *tzaraas* is beginning here (as mentioned above).

Moreover, it is even more puzzling according to the *peshuto shel mikra* (the plain meaning of the verse): Although Rashi in his commentary on the Torah also brings this law, that the sign of *michyah* applies to all *maros* ("And even though *michyah* is only stated regarding *se'es*, it is a sign of *tumah* for all *maros* and their derivatives"), he does not write (as the *Toras Kohanim* does) that this is learned from the verse "*nega tzaraas*..." (he only brings the law itself). This implies that Rashi holds that this law is proven through logic (*sevara*).

### 78

B. Seemingly, one can explain the reason for this introduction and beginning ("*Nega tzaraas*..."):

This *parsha* discusses (only) the *marah* of "*se'es*," and the other *maros* are not mentioned in it (as above). Based on this, one could say that by the text beginning and emphasizing "*Nega tzaraas*... and behold, a *se'es*..." (after having grouped them together at the beginning of the *parsha* of *nega'im*: "When a person has in the skin of his flesh a *se'es*, or a *sapachas*, or a *baheres*"), it informs us that the *marah* of *se'es* is a distinct category. The practical difference (*nafka mina*) is that it does not combine with *baheres* regarding the required size (*shiur*) of the *nega*.

And even though raw flesh (*michyas basar chai*—which is mentioned in this *parsha*) is a sign of *tumah* not only in *se'es* but in all *maros* (as above) [for the text equates white hair and *michyah* here; just as white hair is a sign of *tumah* in all *maros* (as is explicit in the verse above), so too is it regarding the sign of *michyah*], there is still room to say that the text did not equate the *marah* of *se'es* to the other *maros* except regarding the sign of *tumah* of *michyah*, but not regarding the fact that they should combine with one another to reach the *shiur* of *tumah*.

Of course, one should not raise a difficulty against this from the explicit statement in the *Mishnah* that "these four *maros* combine with one another" for all matters—because, as has been discussed many times, Rashi in his commentary on the Torah interprets the verses according to *peshuto shel mikra*, even when it does not align with the conclusion in the halachic part of the Torah.

Especially in our case, where in the *Toras Kohanim* this law (that the *maros* of *nega'im* combine with one another) is learned from what is written "And it shall be (*v'hayah*)..." [for it should have said "*v'hayu*" (and they shall be), and since it is written "*v'hayah*" (singular), it implies that they all combine together to the *shiur* of one *nega*, such that even one *nega* from the two of them combine to the *shiur* of *tumah*], and since Rashi in his commentary on the Torah did not bring this teaching, it implies that according to Rashi, the text does not come to equate them regarding combination.

However, it is a great strain to interpret Rashi this way according to the *peshuto shel mikra*, because even though according to logic there is room to say that *se'es* does not combine with *baheres* because they are different *maros* and different names

### 79

of *nega'im*, it is nevertheless a great strain to say that this is what the text intended by the introduction of "*Nega tzaraas*..."—because (a) then Rashi should have explained it (similar to his explanation later in the *parsha* regarding *michvah* and *shechin*: "And the signs of *michvah* and *shechin* are the same; why did the text divide them? To say that they do not combine with one another; if half a *gris* was born in *shechin* and half a *gris* in *michvah*, they are not judged as a *gris*"); (b) for this, it is sufficient for the text to divide them (in the language of Rashi above), and there is no need for the introduction of an entire verse.

C. It can be said that the reason for the text's emphasis, "*Nega tzaraas*... and he is brought to the *kohen*," according to *peshuto shel mikra*, is not because of any specific law added in this *parsha*, but because in this *parsha* a new and general matter is introduced regarding the definition of *nega'im*, which was not stated previously in the laws of *nega'im* of the skin of the flesh.

This can be understood by prefacing that, in addition to the law of the sign of *michyah*, there is another fundamental law stated in this *parsha* (as above)—"And if the *tzaraas* blossoms in the skin, and the *tzaraas* covers all the skin of the *nega* from his head to his feet, as far as the eyes of the *kohen* can see, and the *kohen* shall look, and behold, the *tzaraas* has covered all his flesh, he shall declare the *nega* *tahor*; it has all turned white, he is *tahor*."

And this law, as it is stated in the text, is when "it has all turned white" after it had become *tamei*. However, before it became *tamei*, it is explicit in the *Toras Kohanim* (at the end of the *parsha*): "One might think that one who comes entirely white at the beginning should be *tahor*; the text says 'he is *tahor*'—he is *tahor*, but one who comes entirely white at the beginning is not *tahor*, but *tamei*."

And likewise, it is learned in the *Toras Kohanim* at the beginning of the *parsha* from the word "*ba'adam*" (in a person)—"to include one who comes entirely white, that the *michyah* should render him *tamei*... the text says *ba'adam* to include one who comes entirely white, that the *michyah* should render him *tamei*." And again they learned in the *Toras Kohanim*: "One might think that one who blossoms from the *tahor* should be *tahor*; the text says 'he is *tahor*'—he is *tahor*, but one who blossoms from the *tahor* is not *tahor*, but *tamei*" (meaning that we have several teachings regarding "entirely white": one who comes entirely white at the beginning regarding the days of *hesger*; one who comes entirely white at the beginning and has *michyah* in it regarding *hechlet* (confirmation); one who blossoms entirely white after he was released from *hesger* or from *hechlet*).

But Rashi in his commentary on the Torah did not mention anything about these specific details of the laws, and seemingly it is puzzling; especially since these words "he is *tahor*" are redundant, and this requires explanation according to the *peshuto shel mikra*, for after it says "and he shall declare the *nega* *tahor*, it has all turned white"—what does "he is *tahor*" add?

D. The explanation for this can be said to be as follows:

Regarding the signs of *tumah* of *nega'im*, we find a change in Rashi: regarding white hair, Rashi writes: "White hair is a sign of *tumah*, it is a decree of the text (a *gezeiras hakasuv*)". Whereas in our *parsha*, regarding the sign of *michyah*, on the verse "it is an old *tzaraas* in the skin of his flesh," Rashi explains: "It is an old wound beneath the *michyah*, and this mark appears healthy on top, while beneath it is full of moisture, so that you should not say: 'Since the *michyah* has healed, I will declare it *tahor*.'" This shows that Rashi holds that the sign of *tumah* of *michyah* is not a decree of the text, but is based on the reason: "it is an old wound beneath the *michyah*."

Based on this, one must examine the law of "it has all turned white, he is *tahor*": is it a decree of the text that this is a sign of *taharah*, or is it based on logic, that since it has all turned white, this indicates that it is not an illness (of a specific type) in a part of the body, but rather that this is the nature of his body?

It can be said that this is the difference between the understanding of the text according to the *peshuto shel mikra* and the understanding of the text according to the *halacha* in the *Toras Kohanim*:

According to the *peshuto shel mikra*, the *taharah* when it has all turned white is not from a decree of the text but from logic, as mentioned above, that this indicates that this is the nature of this person's body. Therefore, Rashi does not interpret the addition "(and he shall declare the *nega* *tahor*, it has all turned white) he is *tahor*" in the text, because "he is *tahor*" here refers to the person, and it is a kind of providing a reason for the *taharah* of the *nega*—that when "it has all turned white (this indicates that this person) is *tahor*."

This is in contrast to the *Toras Kohanim* (and according to *halacha*), where when "it has all turned white" it is a sign of *taharah* by decree of the text, like all laws of *nega'im*. Therefore, they hold (and this is the *halacha* in the Rambam) that this sign is only when it blossoms from the *tamei*, whereas one who blossoms from the *tahor*, and likewise one who comes entirely white at the beginning, is *tamei*, because in essence this is in the category of *nega tzaraas*.

Based on this, it turns out that according to the *peshuto shel mikra*, the law of "it has all turned white" is not only when it blossoms from the *tamei*, when there was a *tamei* *nega* in it, but in any case—even one who comes entirely white at the beginning, or blossoms from the *tahor*. Because since it is not a decree of the text that it is a sign of *taharah* in the *nega*, but rather a matter based on a reason—that this is the nature of this person—there is no *nafka mina* in what manner it comes; in any case, it is considered as if there is no *nega* here at all.

E. Based on all this, it can be said that the aforementioned law that "one who comes entirely white" at the beginning and one who blossoms from the *tahor* is "*tahor*" is not only because of the appearance of the *nega*, but that even if there is *michyah* (a sign of *tumah*) in the "one who comes entirely white"—it does not render him *tamei*.

And seemingly this is proven according to the *peshuto shel mikra*, by prefacing:

In the *Toras Kohanim* on the verse "And on the day that raw flesh appears in it, he shall be *tamei*," it explains: "To teach regarding the extremities of the limbs that were revealed, that they should be *tamei*." The commentators explain that they were revealed after they had already been covered by the blossoming, and this comes in continuation of the previous verse, that even though the extremities of the limbs are not rendered *tamei* by *michyah*, this is only "before the *tzaraas* covered all his flesh, as it is taught: 'And the *kohen* shall look, and he shall see them all as one,' that if it was on the tip of his nose or the tip of his finger, which slopes this way and that, it is not *tamei*... but after the *tzaraas* has covered all his flesh, and also all the extremities of his limbs, if it returns and is revealed on the tip of one of them, raw flesh, he is *tamei*." And this is the case in the commentators of the *Mishnah*, and according to *halacha* in the Rambam.

But Rashi on the aforementioned verse explains: "If *michyah* grew in it, it has already been explained that *michyah* is a sign of *tumah*. But here, the *nega* was on one of the twenty-four extremities of the limbs that are not rendered *tamei* due to *michyah*, because the *nega* does not appear all as one, as it slopes this way and that, and the tip of the limb returned and was revealed, sloping due to fat, for example, if it became healthy and became wide, and the *michyah* is seen in it, and the text teaches us that it shall be *tamei*."

The commentators asked: Why does Rashi not explain it like the simple interpretation in the *Toras Kohanim*, that it comes to teach regarding the extremities of the limbs that were revealed, etc., meaning that they were revealed after they were covered when the *tzaraas* blossomed over all of him (as the commentators in the *Toras Kohanim* explain)? Furthermore, according to Rashi, this verse has no place here, for the previous verse speaks of one who blossomed over all of him, and according to Rashi, a separate law is stated here, without connection to the law of one who blossomed over all of him, that sometimes even the extremities of the limbs are rendered *tamei* due to *michyah*.

And in general, what is the necessity to explain that it is speaking of the extremities of the limbs? Why does Rashi not explain it according to the simple meaning, that after the blossoming over all of him, "on the day that raw flesh appears in it" anywhere on the body, he shall be *tamei*, and it teaches us that even after the blossoming over all of him, where he is *tahor*, he is rendered *tamei* due to the sign of *michyah*?

It must be that according to Rashi—since the law of "it has all turned white" is not a decree of the text (that when it blossomed over all of him, a *tamei* *nega* is a sign of *taharah*), but rather that it is not in the category of *nega tzaraas* at all—there is no room to say that "on the day that raw flesh appears in it, he shall be *tamei*," because there is no sign of *tumah* of raw flesh in the *nega*, for there is no *nega* here.

Based on this, the reason for the introduction in this *parsha*, "When a *nega tzaraas* shall be in a person, and he is brought to the *kohen*," is understood simply according to *peshuto shel mikra*, because in this *parsha* a new and general law is stated regarding "*nega tzaraas*": that the status of a *nega* only exists when the *tzaraas* is in a specific part of the body, but when the appearance of the entire person is white, this is not in the category of "*nega tzaraas*," and consequently, the law of "and he is brought to the *kohen*" does not apply here.

### 82

F. It is stated in the *Gemara Sanhedrin*: "The son of David will not come until the entire kingdom is turned to heresy." Rava said: What is the verse? "It has all turned white, he is *tahor*" (When the *nega* spread over all the skin, so too when the entire kingdom is turned to heresy, the *Geulah* will come. Rashi). And this statement is also brought at the end of *Maseches Sotah*, and there it is brought among the signs that will be in the "footsteps of *Mashiach*," and it concludes: "And upon whom do we have to rely? Upon our Father in Heaven."

And according to what we explained above, that there are two ways to understand the reason for "it has all turned white, he is *tahor*," whether it is a decree of the text or from a reason and logic, which indicates that this is not a *nega*—it can be said similarly regarding the aforementioned matter (that the turning of the kingdom to heresy is a sign for the coming of *Mashiach Tzidkeinu*):

a) That this is a negative sign—that since it is a situation so low and bad, that there is nothing lower than it, there is, so to speak, no choice, and it is inevitable that *Hashem* will bring the *Geulah* immediately. b) On the contrary, the turning of the kingdom to heresy indicates that the world is becoming clarified, to such an extent that it comes to the revelation that every kingdom in the world that has no connection to the Kingdom of Heaven is in the category of "heresy," and only the Jewish people believe and know that *Hashem* is One. This matter is part of the clarification and refinement of the world, that "many will be clarified, and whitened, and refined", and then the son of David comes, and the Jewish people will clarify and fix the entire world to serve *Hashem* together, as it is said: "For then I will turn to the peoples a clear language, that they may all call upon the name of *Hashem*, to serve Him with one consent."

[Similar to the statement of the *Gemara* (the two ways) regarding the Jewish people: "The son of David will only come in a generation that is entirely righteous or entirely guilty. In a generation that is entirely righteous, as it is written: 'And your people are all righteous; they shall inherit the land forever.' In a generation that is entirely guilty, as it is written: 'And He saw that there was no man, and He was astonished that there was no intercessor,' and it is written: 'For My own sake, for My own sake, I will do it.'"]

And these two ways are similar to the two ways mentioned above: whether the fact that "it has all turned white, he is *tahor*" is a decree of the text, meaning that the *Geulah* is not related to the state of the world itself (as they are not worthy of *Geulah*, and on the contrary) but comes by decree and necessity from Above [similar to the above regarding Israel: "in a generation that is entirely guilty... for My own sake, for My own sake, I will do it," and it is explained in *Chiddushei Aggados* there that *Hashem* will set up a king for them, etc., through which they will do *teshuvah*, a necessary *teshuvah*]; or whether it is a matter based on a reason and logic, that "it has all turned white" indicates that there is no *nega* here, and this is as mentioned above, that the turning of the kingdom to heresy is an indication of the clarification and refinement of the world, that the world, from the perspective of its state, is worthy of *Geulah*, and consequently the son of David comes (similar to the above regarding a generation that is entirely righteous, that the Jewish people will do *teshuvah* willingly).

And just as it is regarding the Jewish people, that the Rambam rules as *halacha* that "the Torah has promised that Israel will eventually do *teshuvah* at the end of their exile," which can be said to mean (as explained elsewhere) a complete *teshuvah*, a willing *teshuvah*, "and immediately they are redeemed"—

### 83

similarly, it is regarding the nations of the world, that through the spreading of the seven *mitzvos* of the children of Noach among all the nations, this matter will be fulfilled for the good, including also that all the nations will recognize the greatness of the Jewish people who spread the faith in *Hashem* and His *mitzvos*, and they will be grateful to them for the spreading of justice and righteousness in the world, to the extent that already at the (end of the) time of exile, "And kings shall be your foster fathers, and their queens your nursing mothers" in every way, and with greater strength and greater power, that they will provide the Jewish people with all their needs in abundance.

And this matter will further hasten the coming of *Mashiach Tzidkeinu*, who "will fix the entire world to serve *Hashem* together, as it is said: 'For then I will turn to the peoples a clear language, that they may all call upon the name of *Hashem*, to serve Him with one consent'", speedily in our days and in our immediate time.

(From the *sicha* of *Shabbos Parshas Tazria-Metzora*, 5742)

## Sicha 2

### 84

A. Regarding the verse, "And if the tzaraas breaks out in the skin, and the tzaraas covers... from his head to his feet, to all that the eyes of the kohen see," the Toras Kohanim presents two expositions (on the phrase "to all that the eyes of the kohen see"):

1) "To all that the eyes of the kohen see"—excluding the 'hidden places' [which are not areas visible to the eyes of the kohen]. From here they said: 'A man is examined as he is hoeing, etc.; a woman as she is kneading, etc.' [for whatever is visible of them in this state is not considered a 'hidden place'].

2) "Another interpretation: 'To all that the eyes of the kohen see'—excluding a kohen whose eyesight has dimmed. From here they said: 'A kohen whose eyesight has dimmed, or one who is blind in one of his eyes, or whose vision has weakened, may not examine the plagues.'"

From the straightforward language of the Toras Kohanim ("Another interpretation"), it appears that these are conflicting interpretations of the verse.

However, in the Mishnah Nega'im, these two laws are brought as anonymous rulings: "A kohen who is blind in one of his eyes, or whose vision has weakened, may not examine the plagues, as it is said, 'to all that the eyes of the kohen see.'" And in the subsequent Mishnah: "How is the plague examined? A man is examined as he is hoeing, etc.; a woman as she is kneading, etc." Even though the second Mishnah does not explicitly cite the exposition "to all that the eyes of the kohen see—excluding the hidden places," all the commentators on the Mishnah (Rambam, R. Shimshon, the commentary of the Rosh, Rav Ovadiah of Bartenura, etc.) bring this exposition and explain that this is the intent of the Mishnah. Similarly, the Rambam in his Sefer HaYad cites the verse "to all that the eyes of the kohen see" as the reason for both laws, stating: "A blind man... and even a kohen whose vision has weakened may not examine the plagues, as it is said, 'to all that the eyes of the kohen see.'" And: "The kohen is not required to search under the armpits, etc., lest there be a plague there, as it is said, 'to all that the eyes of the kohen see.'"

B. Simply put, one could say that the Tanna of the Mishnah holds that both laws can be derived from this verse. The law of "excluding the hidden places" is derived from the general meaning of the verse, "And the tzaraas covers... from his head to his feet, to all that the eyes of the kohen see," which refers only to those parts of the body that are "to the sight of the eyes of the kohen." The second law (excluding a kohen who is "blind, etc., or whose vision has weakened") is derived from the extra word in the verse, as explained in the commentaries, that "the word 'all' is expounded, for it should have said 'to the sight of,' but it said 'to all the sight of,' to say that his entire eyesight must be as nature provided, and it must not be weakened."

[Based on this, one can resolve the words of Rashi in his commentary on the Torah, where he explains "to all that the eyes of the kohen see" as "excluding a kohen whose vision has dimmed."

This is puzzling: Why does Rashi choose this exposition and not the exposition "excluding the hidden places," which seemingly fits the plain meaning of the verse better, as the words "to all that the eyes of the kohen see" come to exclude specific parts of the body, following the previous statement, "And the tzaraas covers all the skin of the plague from his head to his feet (to all that the eyes of the kohen see)"?

According to the above, one could say (albeit with difficulty) that the exclusion of "hidden places" is the plain meaning of the verse, and Rashi did not need to explain it, as that is the simple meaning of the words "to all that the eyes of the kohen see"—the exposed parts of the body that are within the sight of the kohen. Rashi's intent is only to explain the extra wording of "to all that the eyes of the kohen see", and regarding this he wrote "excluding a kohen whose vision has dimmed."]

But according to this, it is difficult: Why does the Toras Kohanim preface this with the language "Another interpretation," which implies that they are contradictory expositions, as mentioned above?

C. This can be understood by first noting the difference between the language of the Mishnah and the language of the Toras Kohanim:

Regarding the second law, the Mishnah states two details: "A kohen who is blind in one of his eyes, or whose vision has weakened." In contrast, the Toras Kohanim begins with "excluding a kohen whose vision has dimmed," and only afterwards continues, "From here they said, etc. (and details the two aforementioned cases), blind in one of his eyes or whose vision has weakened." It must be that according to the Toras Kohanim, the foundation of the law is "a kohen whose vision has dimmed," and within this there are two modes: "blind in one of his eyes or whose vision has weakened."

One can say regarding this, based on what the Tiferes Yisrael wrote, that the law of "whose vision has weakened" refers to "both his eyes having weakened; but if only one eye has weakened, no, for the Mishnah teaches 'blind in one of his eyes'" (meaning that only if he is truly blind in one eye is he disqualified, but not if only one eye has weakened).

The commentators challenged him, saying that "since the vision of one eye has weakened, there is no longer 'all the sight of the eyes of the kohen'" (for the meaning of the verse is, as mentioned above, that the kohen's power of sight must be complete). They wrote that the fact that the Tanna mentioned "blind in one of his eyes" (even though he is also disqualified if one eye has weakened) is not a difficulty, because the Mishnah is teaching "not only this, but even that".

It appears that according to the Tiferes Yisrael, the two disqualifications—"blind (even) in one of his eyes" or "whose (two) eyes have weakened"—are two different laws, derived from two parts of the verse. The fact that he is disqualified if he is blind in one eye is derived from the phrase "the sight of the eyes of the kohen," in the plural, to exclude one who is blind in one eye and sees only with one. In contrast, the law that he is disqualified if "his vision has weakened" is derived from the extra word "all" (as mentioned above), that "the sight of the eyes of the kohen" must be complete, and not when there is a dimming in (both) his eyes.

Based on this, one can say that this is the reason for the change in the language of the Toras Kohanim from the language of the Mishnah. For the Mishnah (according to the Tiferes Yisrael) has two laws (and disqualifications): "blind in one of his eyes or whose vision has weakened." In contrast, for the Toras Kohanim, there is only one law here: "a kohen whose vision has dimmed," because "there is no 'all the sight of the eyes of the kohen' here", and this is the foundation of the disqualification. Therefore, it prefaces "to all that the eyes of the kohen see—excluding a kohen whose vision has dimmed," and only afterwards details the two modes of "blind in one of his eyes or whose vision has weakened".

[Seemingly, one should note the language of Rashi in his commentary on the Torah, where he wrote (as mentioned above) "excluding a kohen whose vision has dimmed," and he deviates in two ways: (a) He omitted the word "eyes," even though both in the Toras Kohanim and in the Mishnah the language is "the vision of his eyes". (b) He omitted the clause "blind in one of his eyes". It seems, ostensibly, that even according to Rashi, even if only one of his eyes has weakened, he is already disqualified from examining plagues if his "vision has dimmed" (similar to the above in the Toras Kohanim). Therefore: (a) He omitted the word "eyes," from which it could be implied (as the Tiferes Yisrael holds) that the dimming must be in both eyes. (b) He omitted the case of "blind in one of his eyes," because it is included in "his vision has dimmed"].

D. It can be said that the foundation of the difference between the Tanna of the Mishnah and the Toras Kohanim lies in the very definition of the law "excluding a kohen whose vision has dimmed," which can be explained in two ways:

1) This is a disqualification of the person (*hegvara*). One of the conditions required for a kohen to be qualified to see the plague is that his power of sight must be complete, similar to a *ba'al mum* (one with a blemish) who is disqualified from the *avodah*. This is the direction of the Rambam's words in Sefer HaYad, where he writes: "A *challal* is disqualified from examining plagues, as it is said, 'one of the sons of the kohanim in their priesthood.' However, *ba'alei mumim* are qualified to examine plagues, provided he is not blind, even in one of his eyes, and even a kohen whose vision has weakened may not examine the plagues, as it is said, 'to all that the eyes of the kohen see.'" Thus, this is a disqualification of the person, similar to a *challal* or a *ba'al mum*.

2) The disqualification is due to a deficiency in the act of seeing. When the kohen's power of sight is lacking, the examination of the plague ("and the kohen shall see") is deficient, for we derive from "to all that the eyes of the kohen see" that the plague must be seen (by the eyes of the kohen) in its completeness, and when the kohen's power of sight is lacking, the plague has not been seen in its completeness. This is also somewhat implied by the language of the Rambam in his commentary on the Mishnah, where he explains the law of "or whose vision has weakened" as: "his vision has weakened until he does not see with a true vision," implying that the deficiency is that the plague is not seen with a "true vision."

One can say there is a practical difference (*nafka mina*) in halacha between these two ways:

In the examination of plagues, there are two laws: (a) The act of seeing the plague, and in this, "everyone is qualified to examine the plagues" (even a Yisrael), if he is wise and expert, etc. (b) To rule whether it is *tamei* or *tahor*, for "the *tumah* and *taharah* depend on the kohen (specifically)", to the point that even "a kohen who does not know how to examine, a wise man (even if he is a Yisrael) examines it and tells him, 'Say *tamei*,' and the kohen says *tamei*, etc. And even if the kohen is a minor or mentally incompetent, the wise man tells him and he declares it, etc.".

Based on this, one can inquire regarding the disqualification of one blind in one eye and a kohen whose vision has weakened, whether it is only regarding the act of seeing the plague, or also regarding ruling on *tumah* and *taharah*. In the Meiri, two opinions are brought regarding this: some say he is disqualified only from seeing the plague, but he can rule on *tumah* and *taharah*, etc., like a mentally incompetent kohen who declares *tumah*, etc., based on the instruction of a wise man. However, others say that he is also disqualified from ruling on *tumah* and *taharah*, etc.

It can be said that this depends on the two aforementioned ways: According to the first way, that it is a disqualification of the person (similar to a *ba'al mum* for the *avodah*), it is reasonable to say that he is completely removed from the entire law of examining plagues, and he cannot rule on *tumah* or *taharah* [and seemingly this is implied by the words of the Rambam, who brought (as mentioned above) the disqualification of the blind and the weak-sighted following the disqualification of a *challal*, and a *challal* is not qualified to rule on *tumah* and *taharah*]. In contrast, if we say that he is disqualified in the act of seeing the plague (because the plague must be seen in its completeness), then it is reasonable that the blind man is removed only from seeing the plague, but not from ruling on *tumah* and *taharah*.

E. This is the difference between the Tanna of the Mishnah and the Toras Kohanim:

For the Tanna of the Mishnah, the disqualification of "whose vision has weakened" is because there is a deficiency in the act of seeing the plague (like the second way).

Based on this, the reason the Tanna of the Mishnah appended at the end of this Mishnah the law that "in a dark house, one may not open windows to see its plague" is understood and sweetened. Seemingly, what does this law regarding house plagues (which is derived from what is written, "a plague like a plague has appeared to me in the house"—"to me, and not to my light") have to do with the law that "a kohen who is blind in one of his eyes or whose vision has weakened may not examine the plagues"? According to the above, one can say that with this, the Tanna wanted to emphasize and clarify the definition of the disqualification of a kohen whose vision has weakened: he is not disqualified as a person, as if he were a *ba'al mum* (regarding the examination of plagues), but rather the disqualification is due to the deficiency in the act of seeing the plague, similar to the law that "in a dark house, one may not open windows to see its plague," because the examination of the plague must be a complete act of seeing by the eyes of the kohen (without light from the outside).

One can say that this is the foundation of the Tiferes Yisrael's interpretation that "whose vision has weakened" means specifically when both his eyes have weakened, and not when only one eye has weakened—even though it is said "to all that the eyes of the kohen see," which implies that "the sight of the eyes of the kohen" must be complete, as mentioned above—because when only one eye has weakened and the sight of his second eye is complete, then there is no deficiency in the act of seeing the plague, and there is no reason to disqualify him. Therefore, he holds that it must be deficient in both eyes (at which point there is a deficiency in the act of seeing the plague).

[And the fact that even one who is blind in one eye may not examine the plagues—(a) one must distinguish between one whose eye has weakened (which does not cause weakness in the second eye) and one who is blind in one eye. (b) And primarily: the law of one who is blind in one eye is not because there is a deficiency in the sight ("all the sight"), but because of a *gezeiras hakasuv* (scriptural decree) that the kohen must have "eyes" in the plural, as mentioned above.]

In contrast, the Toras Kohanim holds that this is a disqualification of the person (like the first way mentioned above). Therefore, it holds that the two disqualifications of "blind in one of his eyes" and "whose vision has weakened" have one foundation: the Torah decreed that a kohen who has any weakness in the sight of his eyes is considered a *ba'al mum* regarding the examination of plagues. This is the law of "to all that the eyes of the kohen see," that the power of sight must be complete for him. If so, even for one who is blind in one eye, the disqualification is not because he lacks "eyes" in the plural, but because "his vision has dimmed," and he lacks "all that the eyes of the kohen see."

F. Based on all the above, the difference between the Tanna of the Mishnah and the Toras Kohanim is also understood regarding whether they can derive from "to all that the eyes of the kohen see" both laws—"excluding the hidden places" and "excluding a kohen whose vision has dimmed"—or not.

For the exclusion of "excluding the hidden places" is a law regarding the act of seeing the plague: only those parts of the body visible to the eyes of the kohen can become *tamei* with a plague. This is the reason for the Toras Kohanim, which holds that both exclusions—"excluding the hidden places" and "excluding a kohen whose vision has dimmed"—are two expositions ("Another interpretation"), because each of the expositions has a different intent in the verse: The exclusion "excluding the hidden places" comes to define the act of seeing the plague, that it must be "to all that the eyes of the kohen see," that the plague must be seen in its completeness by the kohen, and therefore "hidden places" are excluded, as they are not places visible to the kohen. In contrast, the exclusion of "a kohen whose vision has dimmed" does not relate to the act of seeing the plague, but is a disqualification of the person, that the verse teaches us that when the kohen lacks "the sight of his eyes," he is a *ba'al mum* regarding the examination of plagues. Therefore, the Toras Kohanim holds that one who taught this did not teach that.

In contrast, for the Tanna of the Mishnah, who holds that even the disqualification of "whose vision has weakened" is due to the deficiency in the act of seeing the plague, then both expositions have the same point: that the plague must be seen in its completeness by the eyes of the kohen. Therefore, both are excluded: (a) hidden places, the parts of the body that are not "to the sight of the eyes of the kohen," and (b) a kohen whose vision has weakened, and consequently the plague is not seen by his eyes in its completeness.

G. The explanation of this in the inner dimension of the matters:

It has been explained in many places regarding the *tumah* of plagues, that the reason why in the *tumah* of plagues—which is one of the most severe *tumos*—the title "Adam" is used specifically, for the name "Adam" is the name of the excellence of Yisrael—is because the matter of plagues that come in the "skin of the flesh" indicates that the evil has not entered into the interior of the person (which is clear), and the evil and the waste are only in his exterior, at the end of his garments, and from this comes the plague "in the skin of his flesh" (as explained at length in Likkutei Torah).

Based on this, one can explain the reason that a kohen whose "vision has dimmed" does not see the plagues, and furthermore, that according to the Toras Kohanim, he cannot rule on *tumah* and *taharah*—because the nature of a person whose "vision has dimmed" is that his power of sight is in such great intensity that it cannot be constricted into physical sight [which is why the blind man is called "Sagi Nahor", which means 'abundance of light,' even though ostensibly it is the opposite of the matter of a blind man who does not see. Rather, it is because in "Sagi Nahor," the essence of the power of sight shines without covering or concealment, and therefore the expansion of the light of sight is not found in him].

Its spiritual meaning is that he sees only the interior of the person.

Therefore, a kohen who is blind in one of his eyes or whose vision has dimmed does not see the plagues, because for him, the matter of plagues—that is, the waste found only in the exterior of the skin—does not exist, for he sees only the interior of the person, which is entirely good and clear.

And just as he does not see the plagues to declare it *tamei*, so too he does not see to declare it *tahor*, because even the purification of the evil in the exterior must come from the power of the kohen, the man of *chesed*, to draw down and influence that the good should come into his exterior garments.

(From the Sicha of Shabbos Parshas Tazria-Metzora, 5748)


## מצורע

## Sicha 1

### 91

A. Regarding the verse, "When you come to the land of Canaan which I give to you as a possession, and I place a plague of *tzaraas* in a house in the land of your possession," the Midrash states: Is this good news for them, that plagues will come upon them? Rather, when the Canaanites heard that the Israelites were coming against them, they stood and hid their wealth in their houses and fields. What does the Holy One, Blessed be He, do? He causes plagues to break out in his house, and he tears it down and finds a treasure [or: *otzar*] (his field is burned, and as he turns it over, he finds a treasure beneath it). And who came and told the Canaanites that the Israelites were entering the land? Joshua sent three proclamations [letters] to them: whoever wishes to evacuate, etc., to make peace, etc., to wage war, etc.

This statement is also brought in Rashi’s commentary on the verse, with several changes. He writes: "It is good news for them that the plagues are coming upon them, because the Amorites had hidden caches of gold in the walls of their houses throughout the forty years that the Israelites were in the desert, and through the plague, he tears down the house and finds them."

Among the (primary) changes between Rashi’s language and the language of the Midrash:

1) In the Midrash, it says "Canaanites," while Rashi changes it and writes "Amorites". This change requires explanation, especially since the verse explicitly states, "When you come to the land of Canaan." How is it possible that Rashi, who comes to explain the plain meaning of the text (*peshuto shel mikra*), deviates from the language of the verse (Canaanites) and writes "Amorites"?

2) The Midrash states, "When the Canaanites heard that the Israelites were coming against them, they stood and hid, etc.," which was (as it explains later) at the time of the conquest of the land, when Joshua sent the letters, etc., through which it became known to them "that the Israelites were entering the land." Rashi, however, writes, "that they hid, etc., throughout the forty years that the Israelites were in the desert". This requires study: whence does he know this, especially according to the plain meaning of the text?

3) In the Midrash, the language is "hid their wealth," while in Rashi, it is "hid... caches of gold."

4) In the Midrash, it says "in their houses and in their fields," while in Rashi, it says "in the walls of their houses" (omitting "and in their fields").

The reason for the last change is simply understood: the purpose of Rashi’s commentary on the Torah is to interpret the verses (not to recount the stories of the Sages). Therefore, there is no place in his commentary to mention the wealth in the fields, which is irrelevant to the interpretation of the verse, "and I place a plague of *tzaraas* in a house in the land of your possession".

### 92

Furthermore, and most importantly: a) The burning of the fields (and the finding of the wealth through it) is only near the time of the conquest of the land, and b) it is not related to the plagues.

For this reason, Rashi specifies "in the walls of their houses"—because by tearing down a plague-stricken house, one can only find the caches that were in the walls of the house (which they tear down), but not what was hidden deep in the ground of the house.

Based on this, we can also explain the preceding change—"their wealth" (in general) versus "gold"—for the one depends on the other: the Midrash speaks about everything they hid "in the houses and in the fields," and therefore it uses a general term, "their wealth." This is not the case in Rashi, who specifies "in the walls of their houses"; he is forced to interpret that they hid only gold, because the walls of a house are a narrow and limited space, unable to contain all types of wealth—silver and copper—in quantities that would justify the effort of hiding them. This is not the case with gold, where even a small amount constitutes great wealth.

However, the first two changes mentioned above require explanation.

B. This will be understood by first noting the wonderment regarding the words of the Midrash: "And who came and told the Canaanites that the Israelites were entering the land? Joshua sent three proclamations to them, etc." Seemingly:

What is the Midrash’s question, "And who came and told the Canaanites that the Israelites were entering the land?" The Torah itself explicitly states that at the time of the splitting of the Red Sea, "all the inhabitants of Canaan melted away", and as Rashi explains there, "They said, 'They are coming against us to destroy us and to inherit our land'"! This is also explicit in the Book of Joshua, where Rahab said to the two spies, "I know that Hashem has given you the land... and that all the inhabitants of the land have melted away before you, for we have heard how Hashem dried up the waters of the Red Sea before you when you left Egypt, etc."

[Some commentators suggest that this is the reason Rashi changed from the language of the Midrash (as above) and wrote "throughout the forty years that the Israelites were in the desert." But the words of the Midrash require explanation.]

One can say, simply, that even though the Canaanites knew that the Israelites were destined to enter their land, and at the time of the splitting of the sea they already "melted away" because the Israelites would come to destroy them, etc., this is still not a reason for them to hide their wealth from the Israelites from that moment on. There was still time until the Israelites would approach their land and be ready to enter it in practice, especially after they saw that the Israelites were delayed for a long time in the desert.

This is the question of the Midrash: "And who came and told the Canaanites that the Israelites were entering the land?" That is to say, how did they know that the time for their entry into the land had now arrived, such that they hid their wealth? And it answers that this became known to them through the sending of the letters, etc.

However, based on this, we must understand Rashi: the explanation that the hiding of their wealth was in proximity to the Israelites' entry into the land (and not forty years earlier, when they heard about the splitting of the sea, even though they "melted away" then) is also true according to the plain meaning of the text.

If so, it is difficult to explain Rashi ("that they hid... throughout the forty years that the Israelites were in the desert")—why did they hide it at a time when there was still no reason for it? Moreover, after the nations saw that the Israelites were delayed in the desert, they still feared all that time that the Israelites were about to enter immediately (without delays), and because of this, "throughout the forty years that the Israelites were in the desert," there were caches of gold, and they could not use this gold!

One can say that Rashi resolves this difficulty through the change from "Canaanites" to "Amorites," as follows.

### 93

C. It has been explained many times that Rashi wrote his commentary on the Torah in such a way that the student can grasp his intent without needing to search in other books. In places where it seems that Rashi did not explain his words, it is because the matter is understood and explained from what is already explicit in the verses (and Rashi’s commentaries) that precede it.

Similarly, we can say regarding our subject, that the special connection of the "Amorites" to this matter (which is why Rashi changes "Canaanites" to "Amorites") is understood from what is explicit in the verse above—that the Torah specifies the "Amorite" as an exception among the seven nations!

In the promise of the land in the Covenant Between the Pieces, it is said to Abraham, "And the fourth generation shall return here, for the iniquity of the Amorite is not yet full until now." Even though in the subsequent verses, ten nations are listed that his descendants would inherit their land, and the Amorite is only one of them, nevertheless, the Torah ties the determination of the time for returning to the land ("And the fourth generation shall return here") specifically to the "iniquity of the Amorite being full".

It is possible to say that this is what Rashi hints at by writing, "that the Amorites hid, etc.": since at the time of the Exodus from Egypt, there was already a "fourth generation (that) shall return here," the Amorites knew that their iniquity was full ("the iniquity of the Amorite is full") and the time had already arrived "to be driven out of his land". For this reason, they began immediately after the Exodus to hide their gold, etc., because they knew that the time fixed for the return of Abraham’s seed to this land had arrived.

### 94

D. However, the matter is still not fully resolved, for according to this, one must innovate that the nations also knew about the details of the Covenant Between the Pieces.

And while it is understandable why Rashi did not adopt the interpretation of the Midrash (that the Canaanites hid, etc., because they knew about the Israelites' entry into the land from the letters Joshua sent, etc.), as we do not find in the text that Joshua sent letters to the inhabitants of the land; it is still possible to interpret simply that the Canaanites began to hide their gold after the defeat of Sihon and Og (on whom the nations were "relying"), thinking that from now on, there was no one who could stand before the Israelites to prevent them from crossing the Jordan and entering the land. What is the compulsion for Rashi to interpret that the Amorites hid their gold "throughout the forty years that the Israelites were in the desert" (and the knowledge about the entry into the land was because "the iniquity of the Amorite was full")?

One can say the explanation is this:
The hiding of the wealth, etc., contains a paradox: on one hand, it is proof, as is understood, of their fear and dread that they would be driven from their houses and land, etc. But on the other hand, the act of hiding indicates that they were not completely despairing, and there was still hope in their hearts that, in the end, they would return to their houses and find the caches they had hidden (for it is very difficult to say that their intent in hiding was only so that their wealth would not reach the Israelites, in the sense of "neither mine nor yours shall it be").

Based on this, it is simply understood that one cannot say that their fear of the Israelites—both after the splitting of the sea (when "all the inhabitants of Canaan melted away") and after the fall of Sihon and Og—is the reason they hid their wealth. If so, what is the benefit of hiding the wealth when they are destined to be driven from the places where they hid it?

It must be that they did this for another reason and rationale—and this is the difference between the opinion of the Midrash and the opinion of Rashi:

According to the Midrash, the reason for hiding their wealth was the letters Joshua sent, in one of which it was written, "Whoever wishes to make peace, let him make peace." From this, the Canaanites understood that it was not certain they would be driven from their land (since there was the possibility of making peace). Therefore, even though in practice they did not want to make peace but went to war, they did not completely despair of the possibility that even if they were defeated in war, they (or their descendants) could make peace and return to their homes. Therefore, they hid their wealth.

But according to Rashi (who did not bring the matter of "Joshua sent three proclamations, etc."), the reason for hiding their gold (and their hope to return and take it) is from the very fact that they knew (as above, section 3) that the time fixed for the Israelites to "return here" had arrived, because "the iniquity of the Amorite was full, to be driven out of his land."

The explanation: since the "merit" of dwelling in the land depends on the conduct of its inhabitants (which is why the Amorite was not driven from his land until his iniquity was full), the Amorites said: just as they (the Amorites) were about to be driven from their land due to their iniquities, so too it is possible that the Israelites might be driven from the land due to their own iniquities (at least when their measure is full). Therefore, "the Amorites hid caches of gold" [for a "cache" (*matmon*) is a storage intended for many days], perhaps in the course of time the Israelites would be driven from the land, and they would return to their houses and find those caches.

### 95

E. However, after all this, there is still room for a question:
It is true that Rashi adopted the term "Amorites" relying on what was written previously, "for the iniquity of the Amorite is not yet full." But this itself requires a reason: why was the "iniquity of the Amorite" specifically mentioned in the Covenant Between the Pieces, when the subject is the promise of the entire land of Canaan (which includes ten, or at least seven, nations)?

Ibn Ezra wrote that "he mentioned the Amorite because of his strength... and so said the prophet ('Yet I destroyed the Amorite before them, etc.') who was as tall as the cedars."

But in the plain meaning of the text, we have not yet learned about the strength of the Amorite (relative to the rest of the seven nations). And why did Rashi remain silent and not explain the reason why the "Amorite" was specifically mentioned in the Parsha of Lech Lecha?

One can say that according to Rashi, what is written, "And the fourth generation shall return here, for the iniquity of the Amorite is not yet full until now (and Rashi explains) to be driven out of his land until that time, for the Holy One, Blessed be He, does not exact punishment from a nation, etc."—means as the plain language states: that at "that time," only the iniquity of the Amorite was full (and not that of the other nations), and only he was driven out (at that time) from his land. This is not the case for the other nations, who were driven from their land only after that time. As is explicit in the text, Sihon, King of the Amorites [who was across the Jordan and] was conquered before all the nations by Moses our Teacher, and the other nations were conquered afterward by Joshua (and furthermore—they were not completely driven from their land until after the death of Joshua, as is recounted in the Prophets).

### 96

F. According to the above (that for Rashi, the "Amorite" mentioned in the Parsha of Lech Lecha refers only to this nation, and not to the other nations in the land of Canaan)—it emerges, seemingly, that according to Rashi, there were no "caches of gold" (upon the Israelites' entry into the land) in the entire land of Canaan, but only among the Amorites, because only regarding them did the time fixed for "they shall return here" (to be driven out of his land) arrive.

According to this, we can also explain the length of the verse here—that it begins, "When you come to the land of Canaan which I give to you as a possession," and then repeats, "(and I place a plague of *tzaraas*) in a house in the land of your possession"—seemingly, it should have been brief and said, "in your houses"?

Also: at the beginning of the verse, it says, "which I give to you as a possession," while at the end of the verse, it says, "the land of your possession" (as noted by the *Kli Yakar*, that "he attributed the possession to Israel as if they were the ones holding the inheritance").

According to the above, one can say:
The beginning of the verse deals with the general law of house plagues, which only took effect after their entry into the land in a manner of "possession," meaning that they settled in the land (unlike when they were in the desert, where they did not have houses, and consequently there was not yet the law of house plagues).

But at the end of the verse, the intent of the text is to inform us of a special matter regarding house plagues: that (in addition to the fact that they come as a punishment for sin, like human plagues (which come for the sin of *lashon hara*)), the Holy One, Blessed be He, will place a "plague of *tzaraas*" in the houses so that the Israelites would find the "caches of gold" that were hidden there by the inhabitants of the land.

And since this was not in the entire land of Canaan but only among the Amorites (as above), who were (also or primarily) across the Jordan (the land of Sihon (and Og)), therefore it is said regarding this, "the land of your possession"—because the land across the Jordan (the land of the Amorites) was not given (initially) by Hashem to the Israelites as a possession, but it became "your possession" through the request of the children of Gad and the children of Reuben, who took it for themselves as a possession.

### 97

G. It has been said many times that in Rashi’s commentary there are "wondrous matters" regarding the hint and the secret ("the wine of Torah")—and so it is in our case, that in this change from "Canaanites" to "Amorites," one can say that Rashi hints at the special connection between the matter of the plagues and the Amorite specifically.

The explanation:
Plagues come, as Rashi explains, because of *lashon hara*, that is, evil speech. As explained in the Rambam at the end of *Hilchos Tumas Tzaraas*, the intent is not *lashon hara* specifically, but "the conversation of the wicked" in general (as he elaborates there, that the very fact that they "engage in idle talk" leads to the point where "they come to speak against the honor of the righteous, etc., to speak against G-d, and they deny the fundamental principles").

This is also the essence of the *kelipah* of the "Amorite," and especially according to our Rebbe the Alter Rebbe—that "the Amorite is an expression of speech and talk... a sign of foolish words... and in a multitude of words, transgression is not lacking... because of the multitude of words of the fool, who is called an Amorite, who multiplies words, etc." (similar to the words of the Rambam above regarding "the conversation of the wicked").

However, on the other hand, specifically in the houses of the Amorites were "caches of gold" found—because the ultimate intent in the matter of the plagues is not only to nullify and negate "the conversation of the wicked," but to transform the power of speech of the human being, so that instead of the "Amorite" of the *sitra achra*, there will be speech and talk of holiness, "I have taken from the hand of the Amorite", for through good and holy speech and words, one reveals the "caches" within the soul.

As is also hinted at in the Rambam there, that after the elaboration regarding "the conversation of the wicked," he concludes regarding the great merit of "the conversation of the righteous of Israel," that "it is only in words of Torah and wisdom; therefore the Holy One, Blessed be He, helps them and grants them merit, as it is said, 'Then those who feared Hashem spoke to one another, and Hashem listened and heard, and a book of remembrance was written before Him for those who fear Hashem and for those who think of His name'".

(From a Sicha of Shabbos Parshas Metzora, 5741)


## אחרי

## Sicha 1

### 98

1. Regarding the explanation of why the Torah specifies that the command to Aharon, "He shall not come at all times into the Holy," was given "after the death of the two sons of Aharon, when they drew near before Hashem and died": Our Sages explain that this is because the command was intended to urge him more strongly than the sin his sons had committed, when they entered the Holy (drawing near before Hashem) and died. Therefore, Aharon was commanded, "He shall not come at all times into the Holy... that he die not"—meaning, "that he not die in the manner that his sons died."

The Ohr HaChayim explains (according to one approach) that the verse at the beginning of the parsha, "And Hashem spoke... after the death... when they drew near," is a statement in its own right. Its content is the greatness of the service of Aharon’s sons, who died "when they drew near before Hashem." In his words: "Hashem spoke to Moshe by way of their death, which was in this manner, 'when they drew near before Hashem'—meaning that they drew near before the Supernal Light in the affection of the Holy, and in this they died (and this is the secret of the *mitas neshikah*—the death by a kiss—in which the *tzaddikim* die)." The verse alludes to "the wonder of the affection of the *tzaddikim*, that even though they felt their death, they did not refrain from drawing near to the *dveikus*, the pleasantness, sweetness, friendship, love, kisses, and delight, until their souls expired from them."

[According to this interpretation, this also resolves why the verse is silent here and does not specify their sin (which is explicitly stated in Parshas Shemini: "and they offered before Hashem strange fire, which He had not commanded them"), but only says "when they drew near before Hashem"—since the content of this matter ("drawing near before Hashem") is, in itself, a matter of high spiritual standing.]

Seemingly, according to this interpretation, it requires explanation: why is the place of this statement (narrative) in proximity to the command to Aharon that "he shall not come at all times into the Holy"?

One must say that even according to this interpretation, the notification regarding the manner of their death (that it was a *mitas neshikah*, etc.) serves as a preface to the aforementioned command ("he shall not come at all times into the Holy"), but not as the interpretation (above) suggests—as a warning against sin—but rather as a positive elevation.

2. It is also necessary to understand the words of the Ohr HaChayim mentioned above: How does his interpretation of "when they drew near before Hashem and died" ("that they drew near before the Supernal Light in the affection of the Holy... until their souls expired from them") fit with the simple narrative in Parshas Shemini, that "they took... each man his censer, and put fire in them, and laid incense thereon, and offered before Hashem strange fire, which He had not commanded them," implying that they performed an act "which He had not commanded them" (i.e., that there was a sin here, something "strange" against the will of Hashem)?

There are those who explain that according to the opinion that Aharon’s sons died because they entered the Sanctuary intoxicated with wine, "the explanation of 'which He had not commanded them' is not the cause of their death, but rather to inform us that they volunteered it of their own accord, and not that He had commanded them... and the explanation of 'when they offered strange fire' is 'at the time they offered the strange fire,' and the explanation of 'when they drew near' is 'at the time they drew near,' not that they died because of it."

Similarly, one can explain the verse according to the Ohr HaChayim (that their death was due to the intensity of their "drawing near before Hashem"): that which is written, "and they offered... strange fire, which He had not commanded them," is not a narrative of an undesirable act, but rather a parenthetical statement (that they died at the time they offered fire which He had not commanded them, but rather they volunteered it of their own accord).

However, it still requires explanation, for according to all the opinions brought in the Midrash, etc., the death of Aharon’s sons was in the category of a punishment for a sin (and the dispute is only as to what the sin was, and accordingly, the magnitude and severity of their sin); whereas according to the Ohr HaChayim, a novelty emerges: that their death was not a punishment for a sin (because they did not sin), but on the contrary—a *mitas neshikah* (in which *tzaddikim* die), which is the expiration of the soul due to the intensity of the *dveikus* to Hashem.

3. It can be said that the foundation of the Ohr HaChayim’s interpretation is (also) in the simple meaning of the verses in Parshas Shemini:

In the continuation of the narrative of the death of Aharon’s sons there, it is told that Moshe said to Aharon, "This is what Hashem spoke, saying: 'By those who are near to Me I will be sanctified,'" and our Sages explain (and it is brought in Rashi—the simple meaning of the verse—on the text) that Moshe said, "Aharon, my brother, I knew that the House would be sanctified by those who are intimate with the Omnipresent, and I thought it would be either through me or through you; now I see that they are greater than I and than you."

The content of the matter of "By those who are near to Me I will be sanctified" is explained by commentators based on the language of the Gemara, "in order to sanctify the honor of the Omnipresent through them"—"to make known that He is awesome to those who fear Him and are intimate with Him." This means that because they were "those who fear Him and are intimate with Him," who were extremely close before Hashem, therefore the Holy One, Blessed be He, was exacting with them, judging them even for a small error, and through this (i.e., through the punishment for [the error]), the honor of the Omnipresent was sanctified.

4. [Regarding the distinction between the service of the Outer Altar and the service of the Incense on the Inner Altar:]

The offering of sacrifices on the Outer Altar has as its content the elevation of physicality to Hashem, as it is in its simple sense: one brings a physical animal (and the like) and elevates its fat and blood upon the Altar, and fire descends from Heaven and consumes it, and it is included in the fire of Hashem.

That is to say, the content of the service of sacrifices is the action of man upon the objects of the world outside of himself, to elevate them to Hashem.

However, the incense on the Inner Altar—its primary content is not the elevation of physical things to Hashem (and we were commanded, "You shall not offer upon it [strange incense and] burnt offering, nor meal offering, nor shall you pour drink offering upon it"), but rather the entry of the Kohen to the inner sanctum for the incense and the *hiskashrus* (connection) with the Holy One, Blessed be He, through the elevation of the fire, the incense, and the cloud.

And in the service of man: the service of sacrifices on the Outer Altar is the service that stems from the externality of the heart, whose content is the action in the external part of man, that man should fulfill the will of Hashem in deed, speech, and thought, which are the garments of the soul of man;

And the service of the incense on the Inner Altar is the connection of man with the Holy One, Blessed be He, through the revelation of the interiority of his soul (which is connected with the Holy One, Blessed be He)—and this revelation acts automatically upon his garments of deed, speech, and thought, so that they will be as required, according to the will of Hashem.

This matter is also alluded to in the difference between the term "sacrifices" (*korbanos*) and the term "incense" (*ketores*): *Korban* is from the word *kiruv* (drawing near), for through the sacrifices, man draws near to Hashem, and it is possible that despite drawing near to Hashem, he still remains a separate entity that is close to Hashem; whereas *ketores* is from the word *kesher* (knot) and connection (similar to what is written, "and his soul is bound to his soul," and likewise regarding Yehonasan and David it is said, "and the soul of Yehonasan was bound to the soul of David, and Yehonasan loved him as his own soul")—because the content of the matter of incense is not only that man draws near to Hashem (through his service in deed, speech, and thought), but that he is bound and connected to Hashem.

5. According to this, the connection between the two matters in the service of the two sons of Aharon will be understood: (a) their drawing near before Hashem and dying, (b) the offering of incense:

The content of "when they drew near before Hashem and died"—the greatness of their drawing near and their *dveikus* to Hashem, to the point of the actual expiration of their soul—is exactly what they offered as incense inside. That is, at that time (on the eighth day of the inauguration), the interiority of their soul shone forth in revelation, and from this perspective they offered incense—they were connected to the Holy One, Blessed be He (similar to what is said of Rashbi, "In one knot I am tied to Him, to the Holy One, Blessed be He").

And this is also what is meant by "and they offered... strange fire, which He had not commanded them": Regarding the service on the Outer Altar (the externality of the heart), there is an element of command, because when man is a separate existence, his drawing near to Hashem is through the fulfillment of the command of Hashem in deed, speech, and thought; this is not so in the service of the incense, where man binds and connects his soul to Hashem. When his connection is in an extremely lofty manner, then sometimes there is no need for a command to fulfill His will, for the fulfillment of His will comes automatically, because the will and intent of Hashem is so felt by him that he is connected with Him.

[It can be said that this is akin to the words of the Rambam, that when a man’s *yetzer* overcomes him (and his inner will is covered and hidden), there is a need to coerce him to fulfill the will of Hashem—but when his true will is revealed, there is no need for coercion, for the inner and true will of every Jew is to fulfill the will of Hashem.]

And this is "which He had not commanded them"—that the service of the sons of Aharon was in the category of "which He had not commanded them," for the definition of a command does not apply to it, since they were at this level where they did not need a command, and all their actions and matters were done according to the will of Hashem, spontaneously and automatically.

6. According to this, one must also explain why it was specifically about them that it was said, "By those who are near to Me I will be sanctified," and Moshe said to Aharon, "Aharon, my brother, I knew that the House would be sanctified by those who are intimate with the Omnipresent, and I thought it would be either through me or through you; now I see that they are greater than I and than you."

For seemingly, one must clarify what the content of the matter of "the House will be sanctified" is—after all, this was after "all the seven days of the inauguration that Moshe set up the Tabernacle and served in it," and after the services on the eighth day of the inauguration, when "the *Shechinah* descended to Israel," as is explicitly stated in the verse: "And the glory of Hashem appeared to all the people. And fire came out from before Hashem and consumed upon the Altar the burnt offering and the fat; and all the people saw, and they sang (and praised), and fell on their faces"—

And since the *Shechinah* had already rested upon Israel, what was still lacking in the holiness of the Tabernacle?

Rather, just as in the service of man himself there are two modes and levels—the service of sacrifices belonging to the Outer Altar (externality of the heart), where the person is a separate existence and fulfills the command of the Creator, and the service of the incense on the Inner Altar (interiority of the heart), where man fulfills the command of the Creator in the manner of incense, that he is connected and attached to the Creator until he becomes one thing—

So too it is regarding the object of the Tabernacle, that it has two modes and levels of holiness and the resting of the *Shechinah*: a) "the *Shechinah* descended to Israel," meaning that the Tabernacle is a separate entity and the *Shechinah* descends into it; b) that the House is sanctified, that the resting of the *Shechinah* and the holiness in the House is not in a manner where they are two separate things that draw near, but rather that the holiness is in the essence and body of the House.

It can be said that these are the two levels in the "fire" that descended from Hashem:

"And fire came out from before Hashem and consumed upon the Altar the burnt offering and the fat, etc." is that the *Shechinah* descended to Israel, which came about through the service of Aharon, according to the precision of Rashi’s language, "Aharon, my brother, you are more worthy and important than I, for through your sacrifices and service the *Shechinah* will rest upon you," meaning through the service of the sacrifices on the outside—the Outer Altar;

"And fire came out from before Hashem and consumed them"—this is that the House was sanctified, and this came about (not through sacrifices—service on the outside, the externality of the heart, but) through the incense of Nadav and Avihu, "and they offered before Hashem strange fire, which He had not commanded them," a connection and *dveikus* to Hashem that is above command.

Therefore Moshe said, "Now I see that they are greater than I and than you," because he saw that through his service and the service of Aharon, the House had not yet been sanctified in ultimate perfection, similar to what Rashi said earlier, "that all the seven days of the inauguration that Moshe set up the Tabernacle and served in it and dismantled it every day, the *Shechinah* did not rest in it, and Israel were ashamed, etc. Therefore he said to them, 'This is the thing which Hashem commanded that you should do, and the glory of Hashem will appear to you.' Aharon, my brother, you are more worthy and important than I, for through your sacrifices and service the *Shechinah* will rest upon you." And similarly, even through Aharon, who brought about the resting of the *Shechinah*, the House was still not "sanctified" in the ultimate sense.

Specifically Nadav and Avihu, whose service was in the incense that is above command, the ultimate connection and *dveikus* to Hashem, brought about that the House was sanctified—and therefore "now I see that they are greater than I and than you," because they specifically were "those who are intimate with the Omnipresent," through whom the House was sanctified.

7. According to this, one can also explain, according to the interpretation of the Ohr HaChayim, why it is written here specifically about the (greatness of the service and) death of the sons of Aharon, which was in the manner of "when they drew near before Hashem and died," because this matter is a preface to the following parsha, in which it is explained about the entry of the High Priest once a year into the Holy of Holies:

The content of the service of Yom Kippur, where the High Priest enters as the emissary of all Israel to offer incense in the Holy of Holies, is the entry of the interiority of every Jew into the most holy place and the Holy of Holies of the world, in which the holiness of Hashem rests and is unified (which is why the place of the Ark is not included in the measurements, because in it the level that is above space shone and was revealed in the physical place of the Holy of Holies).
