And this itself is the reason that Yom Kippur atones, "For on this day He shall atone for you, to cleanse you from all your sins before Hashem you shall be cleansed," because the entire matter of sin and iniquity ("they separate between you and your God") is only from the perspective of the level where the Jewish people are a separate existence, and their connection to Hashem is through the fulfillment of His decrees, and therefore, through the fact that they transgress, Heaven forbid, the command of Hashem, a weakening in the connection is created; but when the interiority of the soul is revealed in the Jewish people—which even at the time of sin was in faith with Him, which is embraced and attached to You, a *yechidah* to unify You—which belongs to "before Hashem," the interiority and above the Name *Havayah*—from the perspective of this level, the sins and iniquities that were committed with the external powers of man are nullified.

As all this is explained at length elsewhere.

And regarding this level of Yom Kippur, it is said at the end of the parsha, "And this shall be for you an everlasting statute... once a year," that this level of "one" (*yechidah*) that shines on Yom Kippur must act throughout all the days of the year, that every Jew should be in such a standing and state that the interiority of his heart should shine within him in revelation, in a way that all matters of Torah and *mitzvos* will be for him not (only) in the manner of a burden and coercion, but that it will be his desire and the interiority of his will, and automatically he fulfills them as if spontaneously and automatically, "which He had not commanded them," in the manner of "from his own soul he bows," for the better.

And as it is regarding the matter of the fast on Yom Kippur: from the perspective of the greatness of the level of the Jewish people—that on Yom Kippur they are "like the ministering angels," the fact that they do not eat on Yom Kippur is not only because of the command, but because of the holiness of the day, "who wants to eat!" (as is known from the saying of the *tzaddik*... Rav Levi Yitzchak of Berditchev), and to such an extent that the fast of Yom Kippur is a matter of *oneg* (delight), as it is written, "to keep them alive in famine," that they receive vitality (delight) from the famine itself.

And this is the preface of the statement, "after the death of the two sons of Aharon, when they drew near before Hashem and died," that because of the greatness of their drawing near and the *dveikus* of their soul to Hashem until the expiration of the soul, it is brought about and drawn into every Jew that "once a year" on Yom Kippur he can come to the revelation of the interiority of his soul in *dveikus* to Hashem, in a way that he will not need a command to fulfill the Supernal will.

However, at the dedication of the Tabernacle on the eighth day of the inauguration, this was in the manner of "and they died," the expiration of the soul in actuality, because at that time it was necessary that "the House be sanctified (by those who are intimate with the Omnipresent)";

But after this, the revelation of the level of the interiority of the soul of every Jew must be specifically in the manner of "that he die not," a soul within a body specifically, in longevity of days and good years, to make for Him, blessed be He, a dwelling place in the lower worlds.

(From the Sicha of Shabbos Parshas Acharei-Kedoshim, 5745)

## Sicha 2

### 106

**A.** We learned in Maseches Yoma (at the end of the High Priest’s service on Yom Kippur): "He sanctified his hands and feet, and took off [the golden vestments]. They brought him his own garments, and he dressed. They would accompany him to his house, and he would make a Yom Tov for his loved ones when he emerged in peace from the Holy."

Regarding the conclusion of the Mishnah, "and he would make a Yom Tov, etc.," there are some who have the version "upon emerging" instead of "when he emerged" (and this is how the Rambam writes it in Sefer HaYad).

Commentators explain this version to mean that "the intention is not that he made the Yom Tov immediately upon emerging from the Holy on that very day, for he was fasting, but rather he would make it the following day. This is the precision of the Rambam, who wrote 'a Yom Tov he would make upon emerging in peace from the Holy,' and did not write 'when he emerged' [as is said in the text of the Yom Kippur Avodah service], even though that is more concise. He lengthened his language to inform us that he would make it the following day."

However, this has already been challenged, for "it is possible... that it continued until the evening, after the lighting of the lamps, until he put on his own garments and walked with dignity, walking in his integrity, such that when he reached his house it was night, and it would be 'when he emerged' specifically."

**B.** It appears that the two versions depend on the two reasons given for this Yom Tov:
1) The Yom Tov was an act of thanksgiving for having emerged from a place of danger, similar to the four who must...

### 108

...places the one who brings the atonement in certain mortal danger, such that he must bring a thanksgiving offering for it! And the reason—that he habituates himself, etc.].

**C.** However, there is room to discuss the approach of the Rambam, who brought the statement "and a Yom Tov he would make, etc."—for seemingly this is a book of laws, not a narrative [and even though the High Priest would practice this Yom Tov, like the case of the four who must give thanks, it is not a halacha].

Even though at the beginning of this chapter the Rambam prefaces, "The order of all the actions on this day is as follows," meaning that in this chapter he does not intend to teach us the laws of the Yom Kippur service, but rather to recount the "order of all the actions" as they were done in practice [which is why the Rambam repeated several details in this chapter, even though he had already written them in previous chapters, since here he is describing the "order of all the actions"]—nevertheless, it is understood that his intent in "the order of all the actions on this day" is for actions that belong in their content to the service of this day. According to the aforementioned approach of the Rambam [who adopted the version "upon emerging in peace," meaning he holds that the making of the Yom Tov was (not because his service was accepted, etc., but) because he emerged in peace], the Yom Tov does not belong to the "actions of this day." This is not only according to the logic (mentioned above) that he made the Yom Tov only the next day—in which case it certainly does not belong to the "actions of this day"—but even if you were to say that according to the Rambam he made the Yom Tov immediately at night after the completion of the day's service, since its content is the personal thanksgiving of the High Priest and his custom, it is not appropriate to include it in the "actions of this day".

Indeed, it is emphasized in the language of the Rambam that the Yom Tov was not (merely) his private joy. For in the Mishnah the language is, "And they would accompany him to his house, and a Yom Tov he would make for his loved ones," whereas the Rambam changed this in two ways: (a) he stated, "And all the people would accompany him to his house", (b) he omitted the word "for his loved ones." This is to inform us that the High Priest’s Yom Tov (was not a private joy that he celebrated only with his loved ones, but) belonged to "all the people" who accompanied him to his house (and therefore he omitted the word "for his loved ones").

We must therefore conclude that according to the Rambam, even though the Yom Tov was "upon emerging in peace from the Holy" [and not upon the service of Yom Kippur itself], it nevertheless belongs to the laws of the Yom Kippur service. Therefore, all the people participated in the accompaniment and the Yom Tov he made, since the High Priest performs his service on Yom Kippur as their emissary and representative of all Israel.

### 109

**D.** This will be understood by prefacing another change in the Rambam’s language compared to the Mishnah’s language:
In the Mishnah it is stated concisely, "And they would accompany him to his house," whereas the Rambam adds before this, "And he goes out to his house, and all the people accompany him to his house," from which it is understood that the High Priest "goes out to his house." Why did the Rambam add these words to the language of the Mishnah?

It also requires explanation: what is the precision in the "going out to his house" specifically? Seemingly, what difference does it make where the High Priest goes, whether to his house or to another place ?

This implies that the going out to his house is also part of the High Priest’s actions on this day. Therefore, the Rambam changed the language of the Mishnah (where the going out was mentioned at the end of the day only in connection with those accompanying him) and placed "and he goes out to his house" first (with emphasis on the High Priest)—in continuation of all his previous actions described by the Rambam, that after he "sanctifies his hands and feet and takes off the golden vestments and puts on his own garments," he "goes out to his house" (except that the manner of his going out to his house is that "all the people accompany him to his house").

One must understand: in what way is this matter (that he "goes out to his house") considered one of the details of the High Priest’s service on this day?

The explanation for this is:
The fact that the High Priest "goes out to his house" is considered the conclusion and end of his service on Yom Kippur. Since the beginning of his service is that "seven days before Yom Kippur, they separate the High Priest from his house", as we learn from the days of the inauguration or from the Torah, that the Yom Kippur service requires a state of the High Priest’s separation from his house [from which it is understood that the separation is not a peripheral matter, so that he can prepare himself properly, but rather that the content of the service requires that he be in a state of separation from his house]—it follows that the return (going out) to his house is the conclusion and end of his service.

Therefore, the Rambam also included the going out to his house (and its manner) in the "order of all the actions on this day," and was not satisfied with describing his service until his emergence from the Holy, because the act of going out to his house is also included in the "actions of this day".

It can be said that this is also the reason why the High Priest’s going out to his house was in such a manner that "all the people would accompany him," for it can be said that this accompaniment is not only for the sake of honor, but (primarily) to show and emphasize that the going out to his house is part of the actions of this day that belong to all the people, as mentioned above.

Based on this, it is also understood regarding the continuation of the Rambam’s language, "and a Yom Tov he would make upon emerging in peace from the Holy"—that on one hand it implies that the Yom Tov is upon the emergence in peace (and not...

### 110

...thanksgiving for the service), and on the other hand, this Yom Tov belonged to all the people (who were accompanying him), as mentioned above—for since the emergence from the Holy is also part of the High Priest’s actions on this day (which were not completed in their entirety until he was in his house), all of which the High Priest performed as the emissary of all Israel, the joy "upon emerging in peace from the Holy" (meaning the joy over the completion of the service) is also the joy of all the people.

**E.** The content of the matter of "going out to his house" can be explained more deeply:
In the Yom Kippur service, we find a special innovation: that the atonement of the High Priest’s bull was not only for himself but also for his "house," "his house—this is his wife" [in addition to his brethren the Kohanim]; and this matter (that there must be an atonement for his "house") relates to the totality of the day’s service, as the Rambam rules that the services of the day "are all performed by a married High Priest, as it is said, 'And he shall make atonement for himself and for his house'—his house, this is his wife."

These matters require explanation: why did the Torah say that the High Priest’s service on Yom Kippur must be performed by a "married High Priest"? It is even more wondrous, for the service itself must be performed specifically while in a state of separation from his house (and his wife).

One of the explanations for this is :
The atonement of the Yom Kippur goat is an atonement for all of Israel, as the Rambam writes, "The scapegoat, because it is an atonement for all of Israel, the High Priest confesses upon it on behalf of all of Israel," meaning that the High Priest says the confession as the emissary of all Israel.

On the other hand, the Rambam rules that the goat does not atone for all the transgressions in the Torah unless "he has done teshuvah". This means that the atonement of the goat, which belongs to the community (and not an individual), and is brought by the High Priest who is the communal emissary of all Israel, does not take effect upon the individual unless he has done teshuvah.

Thus, in this atonement there is a paradox: even though the goat is a matter of the community [which means—a matter that belongs to the entire collective, without a special connection to the individual, similar to communal funds, which must be "handed over to the community properly"], at the same time, the atonement of the goat for the community is in a manner that takes effect upon each individual personally, and if the individual has not done teshuvah, this atonement is lacking for that individual.

### 111

It can be said that this is one of the reasons why the High Priest brought a bull for his own atonement and was not satisfied with being included in the atonement of all of Israel —because as the emissary of Israel in the service and atonement of Yom Kippur, there were also these two aspects within him: that he is a communal figure, the emissary and representative of all Israel, and on the other hand, his existence as an individual was not nullified (in this very matter), to the point that he brought a bull that was his own for his own atonement.

For this very reason, the Torah required that the High Priest be "married," and that he include his "house" in his own atonement, because in this it is emphasized that the fact that he brings a "bull" for himself and confesses upon it, he does this (not as a "community," whose entire existence is only for the collective, but) as a private individual.

With this we can explain what the Rambam says, that at the completion of the day’s service he "goes out (the High Priest) to his house," which means (as mentioned above) that this itself is part of the act of service—for the fact that he "goes out to his house" is not only to indicate that his service has already ended and therefore he is permitted to return to his house, but on the contrary, the essence of the going out to his house is the conclusion and purpose of the Yom Kippur service, which is the atonement achieved by the individual, which for the High Priest was expressed in the atonement of his house (as mentioned above).

**F.** Explaining the matter according to Chassidus:
The High Priest’s service on Yom Kippur was in a state of absolute separation and holiness, especially upon his entry into the Holy of Holies, the most sanctified place in the world (in space), on Yom Kippur, the most sanctified day in the year. But the ultimate intent is not separation from the world, but rather to draw down holiness into the world, in the language of the Midrash (which is brought in Tanya), to make for Him, may He be blessed, a "dwelling place in the lower realms."

For this reason, immediately after the completion of the Yom Kippur service, he "goes out (immediately) to his house"—from one extreme to the other without any "intermediary" in between—because this is the purpose of all his service and his wondrous standing and state during his service on Yom Kippur in the Holy of Holies: to draw this holiness also "into his house."

This is also the content of the Yom Tov he made "upon emerging in peace from the Holy"—similar to the language of Chazal regarding Rabbi Akiva, that he "entered in peace and emerged in peace," that his entry into the Pardes was in a manner that he "emerged in peace," because his entry into the Pardes was "in peace," meaning not for the sake of departing from the world, but so that he would have an addition of vitality in his service below, a soul in a body —and likewise with the High Priest, that despite the magnitude of his elevation and his separation from the world upon his entry into the Holy of Holies, he "emerged in peace," and this found expression in the fact that immediately upon his emergence from the Holy—he "went out to his house," in order to complete the intent of creation, to make for Him, may He be blessed, a "dwelling place in the lower realms" specifically.

(From a Sicha of Shabbos Parshas Re'eh, 5746)


## קדושים

## Sicha 1

### 112

a. The Rambam writes: "Anyone who robs his fellow of even a *prutah* is as if he takes his soul from him, as it is said, 'So are the ways of everyone who is greedy for gain; it takes away the soul of its owners.'" According to its plain meaning, the source for this is the statement of Rabbi Yochanan in *Bava Kamma*.

However, the Gemara there continues: "And it says, 'And he shall eat your harvest and your bread [they shall eat] your sons and your daughters,' and it says, 'Because of the violence of the children of Judah, who shed innocent blood in their land,' and it says, 'To Saul and to the bloody house, because he killed the Gibeonites.'" The Gemara explains: "What is meant by 'and it says'? If you would say [it refers only to] his own soul, but not the soul of his sons and daughters, come and hear: 'the flesh of his sons and daughters.' And if you would say this applies only where he does not pay compensation, but where he pays compensation it does not apply, come and hear: 'Because of the violence of the children of Judah, who shed innocent blood.' And if you would say this applies only where he acts with his own hands, but not through *grama* (indirect causation), come and hear: 'To Saul and to the bloody house...'"

It requires understanding why the Rambam did not bring the continuation—that one who robs, etc., is as if he sheds the blood of his sons and daughters as well, and that this prohibition applies also to a *chamsan* (one who forcibly takes by paying), and even through *grama*?

Simply put, one could say that the continuation of Rabbi Yochanan’s statement was not said as *halacha*, but rather as *aggadah* regarding the severity of the sin of robbery. Therefore, the Rambam did not include them, as it is not the purpose of the *Yad HaChazakah*—a book of *halachos*—to bring all the *drashos* regarding the severity of the prohibition of robbery.

However, it is evident from the Rambam’s words that he holds the statement of Rabbi Yochanan, "Anyone who robs, etc.," was said as *halacha*, for in the continuation of this statement he writes (and specifically—in that same *halacha*): "Nevertheless, if the stolen object no longer exists and the robber wishes to repent and comes of his own accord and returns the value of the robbery, it is a *takanas chachamim* that we do not accept it from him, but rather we assist him and forgive him in order to bring the straight path closer to those who repent, and anyone who accepts the value of the robbery from him, the spirit of the Sages is not pleased with him."

This means the Rambam brought this statement not (only) to explain the severity of the prohibition of robbery (and to note

### 113

that if this were the Rambam’s intent, he should have brought it immediately at the beginning of *Hilchos Gezeilah*, or at the end, as is his custom in such matters), but in connection with the *halacha* regarding the *takanas hashavim* (the ordinance for penitents). We must therefore conclude that he holds that this severity in the prohibition of robbery ("as if he takes his soul, etc.") is a matter of *halacha*. If so, why did he not bring that this applies also to *chamas* and through *grama* (and likewise, that it is as if he takes the souls of his sons and daughters)?

b. This can be understood by first explaining the reason for the matter: why one who robs his fellow of a *prutah* is as if he takes his soul. Tosafos wrote in another place that it is "because sometimes there is a severe famine and he has nothing with which to buy [food]." That is, this law—that one who robs his fellow of a *prutah* is as if he takes his soul—applies when the victim is lacking "so much" that in a state of "severe famine" he would have bought with this *prutah* the life of his soul, and thus the robbery can lead to the taking of a soul in practice.

[It is understood that it is not at all reasonable to say that Tosafos’s intent is that because there is sometimes a possibility (which is not common at all) that the robbery leads to the taking of a soul ("severe famine and he has nothing with which to buy"), therefore it is considered "Anyone who robs... a *prutah* is as if he takes his soul"!

Furthermore: Tosafos’s intent here is to resolve the words of the Gemara, "Verbal wronging (*ona'as devarim*) is greater than monetary wronging (*ona'as mamon*)," and Rabbeinu Tam explains, "The former is to his body and the latter is to his money"—even though "one who robs his fellow of a *prutah* is as if he robbed his soul," for this [statement] of "one who robs his fellow, etc.—is because sometimes there is a severe famine and he has nothing with which to buy, but there are many people who are not lacking so much." It is proven from this that Tosafos holds that Rabbi Yochanan’s words "as if he takes his soul" were not said regarding every robbery; on the contrary, for "many people" it is not applicable to say so, and therefore "verbal wronging is greater than monetary wronging... the former is to his body and the latter is (only) to his money."]

But it is impossible to interpret the Rambam’s words this way, as he writes explicitly, "Anyone who robs his fellow, etc.," which implies that this is the case in all circumstances.

[Regarding Tosafos’s opinion, it seems one must say that they had the version "One who robs his fellow," and not "Anyone who robs, etc.," and it is explicitly copied there: "And that which we say in the final chapter of *Bava Kamma*, 'One who robs his fellow, etc.'"]

### 114

Furthermore: According to Tosafos, one must say that what they said, "One who robs his fellow... is as if he takes his soul," is not precise, for the same applies to one who steals from his fellow a *prutah*, since this reason, "because sometimes there is a severe famine and he has nothing with which to buy," applies also to theft, where in any case he is taking his soul from him. This is proven by Tosafos’s question on the Gemara’s words, "Verbal wronging is greater than monetary wronging... the former is to his body and the latter is to his money," which discusses monetary wronging, and we must conclude that Tosafos holds that even one who wrongs his fellow [monetarily] by a *prutah* is as if he takes his soul.

This is not the case according to the Rambam, for the fact that the Rambam uses the general phrasing (as in the *Shas*), "Anyone who robs, etc." (and brought it in *Hilchos Gezeilah*), implies that it applies only to robbery and not to other ways of taking one’s fellow’s money.

[This difficulty exists even if we interpret the Rambam’s view that the reason for "Anyone who robs, etc., is as if he takes his soul" is (as written in the commentaries) because "money... is also considered like one of his parts... as the Sages said many times, 'Anyone who robs his fellow of a *prutah* is as if he takes his soul'... the righteous hold their money dearer than their bodies" [and especially according to the words of the Alter Rebbe regarding *tzedakah*, which is considered as if he "gives the life of his soul to Hashem," "since with these coins [that he gives to *tzedakah*] he could have bought the life of his soul"]—because even according to this explanation, there is no difference between robbery and theft or other ways of taking one’s fellow’s money, for since he loses his money, which is "like one of his parts," it is "as if he takes his soul"].

c. Seemingly, there is room to explain the reason for the matter based on what the Rambam writes in the preceding *halachos*: "Desire leads to coveting, and coveting leads to robbery, for if the owners do not wish to sell, even if he offers them much money and pleads with them, he will come to robbery, as it is said, 'They covet houses and rob them,' and if the owners stand before him to save their money or prevent him from robbing, he will come to bloodshed." Based on this, the Rambam adds, "Anyone who robs his fellow of a *prutah* is as if he takes his soul from him," for since robbery can lead to bloodshed, the Torah said that from the very moment of the robbery, this status of "as if he takes his soul" applies to him.

That is to say: The fact that "Anyone who robs, etc., is as if he takes his soul" is not because this act of robbery itself leads to the taking of a soul in practice (like the opinion of Tosafos, "because sometimes there is a severe famine and he has nothing with which to buy"), but because the act of robbery can lead to an act of bloodshed, and therefore the act of robbery itself is considered an act of bloodshed.

### 115

[And therefore this law applies only to robbery, because the reality of the owners standing before him to save their money or prevent the robbery, in a way that leads to bloodshed, is—"as a general rule"—in robbery, and not in theft or other ways of taking money.]

But it is difficult to interpret the Rambam’s intent this way because:
1) If the Rambam’s intent is that these *halachos* are a continuation of one theme from one matter to another—that when one forbidden act leads to another forbidden act, the first act is considered as if it includes the act that follows from it—then he should have said first, "Anyone who covets is as if he robs," and the like—similar to what he wrote later, "Anyone who robs... a *prutah* is as if he takes his soul." The fact that he only stated "Anyone who robs, etc." implies that this is a unique matter regarding the prohibition of robbery specifically.
2) Why did he use the expression "as if he takes his soul from him," and change from the expression "bloodshed" used in the previous *halacha*?
3) From the way the *halachos* are divided in the Rambam, it is implied that the statement "Anyone who robs his fellow, etc., is as if he takes his soul" is not a continuation and conclusion of the preceding *halachos* (as in the *halacha* preceding this one—"Thus you have learned that one who desires transgresses one negative commandment, etc."), but is a new matter.

d. Therefore, it seems to me that the Rambam holds that the reason for "as if he takes his soul" is not because the act of robbery is liable to lead to bloodshed (in practice), but from the very nature of taking "a person’s money by force", against the will of the owner: taking "a person’s money by force" is not just an act of taking one’s fellow’s money, but in this act he nullifies and expropriates the person’s ownership over his property, and therefore it is "as if he takes his soul from him." And we learn this from the verse, "It takes away the soul of its owners," that since his ownership of this object is expropriated by the robbery, it is like the taking of his soul.

And this rule applies to anyone who robs his fellow of a *prutah*, without distinction whether his fellow is poor or rich [except that if it is less than a *prutah*, for there is no monetary value less than a *prutah*, it is not considered "taking his soul", because in this case he does not nullify ownership over "property"], and it is understood that this is only when he robs his fellow,

### 116

and not in theft or other ways of monetary wronging.

[And according to the Rambam, this is not included in that which is said in *Bava Metzia* that verbal wronging is greater than monetary wronging (for that is to his body and this is to his money), for that applies only to monetary wronging and not to one who robs his fellow.]

Based on this, we can resolve why the Rambam did not bring the continuation of the *Shas*’s words, which also apply to the soul of the victim’s sons and daughters, and also to *chamas* and *grama*, because the Rambam holds that the essence of the law is learned only from the first verse, "It takes away the soul of its owners," and Rabbi Yochanan’s intent in bringing the other verses is to add an additional severity that applies only under specific conditions: if the victim has children who might die as a result of this robbery—their "death" is attributed to the robber; a special innovation in *chamas* which is not of the same type as "as if he takes his soul from him" as in robbery, for since he pays compensation, and does not simply take it against the owner’s will, he does not thereby nullify (to the same extent) the person’s ownership of his money (and on the contrary, he acknowledges his ownership and therefore gives payment); *grama*—when he causes death in practice, where the death is attributed to him, or when by his action he causes the deprivation of the other’s entire source of livelihood (as with the Gibeonites).

Therefore, the Rambam did not bring these details and these verses, because here he brought only what relates to the essence of the act of robbery [even robbery of a *prutah*, which is a matter that is equal in all cases (without additional conditions)], meaning that every act of robbery to which the negative commandment "Do not rob" applies, this severity of "as if he takes his soul" also applies to it, due to the very act of taking a person’s money by force.

e. Based on all the above, we must also explain the Rambam’s words in the continuation of this *halacha*: "Nevertheless, if the stolen object no longer exists and the robber wishes to repent and comes of his own accord and returns the value of the robbery, it is a *takanas chachamim* that we do not accept it from him, but rather we assist him and forgive him in order to bring the straight path closer to those who repent, and anyone who accepts the value of the robbery from him, the spirit of the Sages is not pleased with him."

For seemingly it is not understood: in this chapter, a few *halachos* earlier, the Rambam already brought about the "takanas hashavim"—that if "he robbed a beam and built it into a building, since it has not changed, the law of the Torah is that he must tear down the entire building and return the beam to the owners, but the Sages ordained for the sake of the *takanas hashavim* that he should pay its value and not

### 117

lose the building." If so, it requires study: why did the Rambam write this detail regarding the *takanas hashavim* (when the stolen object does not exist) after several *halachos*, and did not write it above, in continuation of the *takanas hashavim* not to lose the building?

Based on the above, it can be said that this is because the details of this ordinance (as the Rambam elaborated)—(a) that it applies only when the stolen object does not exist, (b) that the robber wishes to repent, (c) and furthermore—that "he comes of his own accord and returns the value of the robbery," (d) that not only do we not accept it from him, "but we assist him and forgive him in order to bring the straight path closer to those who repent"—belong to the aforementioned category of the prohibition of robbery, that it is as if he takes his soul, due to the very act of taking by "force," against the will of the owner.

Explanation of the matter:
The Rambam’s intent in the continuation of his language in this *halacha*—"Anyone who robs, etc., is as if he takes his soul... nevertheless, etc."—can be said to be [not only to explain that since robbery is as severe as bloodshed, one might think the robber’s repentance is extremely difficult, and therefore he informs us of the *takanas hashavim* where they were lenient—but even more so] to explain the essence and definition of the "takanas hashavim" (as below in section v).

For seemingly there is room to wonder: regarding the monetary damage in the act of robbery, there is room for this ordinance, for money can be forgiven by the victim (and the *Beis Din* also has the power to expropriate money), but since the act of robbery is considered as the taking of a soul, how did the Sages ordain that one should forgo the return of the robbery, when the forgiveness of money has no power to nullify the fact that the act of robbery is as the taking of a soul?

The Rambam alludes to this wonder in his words, "Anyone who robs, etc., is as if he takes his soul from him, etc., and nevertheless, etc.," meaning: since the act of robbery is considered as the taking of a soul, therefore, seemingly, it is a great innovation that the Sages ordained that "we do not accept it from him."

In continuation of this, the Rambam writes several details in this ordinance, because only under these conditions is there room for this ordinance, since the act of robbery is as the taking of a soul, as follows.

f. The first condition is that the *takanas hashavim* was ordained only when the stolen object does not exist, but if the stolen object exists, he is obligated to return the robbery that he robbed.

The reason for this: the return of the robbery itself has the power to rectify the sin from its root, unlike when he pays money and does not return the robbery; he only rectifies the victim’s monetary loss but not the essence of the transgression [and similar to what is regarding the negative commandment of robbery, as is understood from the Rambam’s words at the beginning of *Hilchos Gezeilah* regarding the reason why one is not lashed for the negative commandment of "Do not rob," "for the verse redirected it to a positive commandment, that if he robbed, he is obligated to return... and even if he burned the robbery, he is not lashed, for he is obligated to pay its value, and any negative commandment that is given for payment, one is not lashed for it," for when he returns the stolen object itself, he rectifies the negative commandment (retroactively), unlike

## Kedoshim

## 121

Rashi then continues, "such as drowning in a river, or a wild beast or bandits coming upon him."

One must understand: a situation of mortal danger is straightforward; why is it necessary to provide examples? And even if we assume that examples are required, why were these specific examples chosen? 

Although these examples are brought in the Gemara and the Toras Kohanim, we must remember that the primary purpose of Rashi’s commentary on the Torah is—in Rashi’s own words —to explain the *peshuto shel mikra* (the plain meaning of the verse), rather than to merely copy the language of Chazal. Yet, in this instance, Rashi departs from the phrasing of Chazal:

In the Gemara and Toras Kohanim, there are three examples : "drowning in a river, *or* a wild beast... *or* bandits, etc." Rashi, however, changes the phrasing and writes: "drowning in a river, *and* a wild beast, or bandits, etc." This implies that for Rashi, there are not three separate examples, but rather a combination of two states of danger: in addition to the person drowning in the river, a wild beast or bandits are also coming upon him.

[One cannot argue that the *vav* in "and a wild beast" (*v'chayah*) functions as an "or" (as we find in several places where a *vav* is used in the sense of "or"), because Rashi himself continues, "or bandits" (*o listim*). This proves that the *vav* he used previously in "and a wild beast" is meant in its literal sense—a *vav* of addition. Especially since the Gemara and Toras Kohanim use the word "or," and Rashi changes it to "and," it is certain that Rashi intends the plain, literal meaning.]

Furthermore, it is puzzling in general: why did Rashi choose such a complex example—a combination of three life-threatening situations together—as if the Torah only commanded "do not stand idly by the blood of your neighbor" in such a specific, extreme situation?

Also, one must note: (a) Rashi in his commentary...

## 123

...the rescue might cause danger to the rescuer in a way that casts doubt on the possibility of success—in which case it is logical to say (based on the *peshat*) that one is not obligated to exert himself to save his neighbor.

This is the precision of Rashi’s language, "and you are able to save him." Although the Torah warns, "do not stand idly by the blood of your neighbor," this applies—and one is obligated to place himself in a state of potential danger—only when it is clear that the person has the ability to save his neighbor, "you are able to save him", and the fear of danger will not compromise the certainty of the rescue. But if the matter is in doubt, a person should not endanger himself for the sake of a doubtful rescue of his neighbor.

Based on this, it is understood why Rashi needs to bring a specific example of a danger situation regarding which the Torah commanded "do not stand idly by the blood of your neighbor"—to describe a tangible reality that contains both extremes: a fear of danger to the rescuer (which is why the verse must innovate the command "do not stand idly by"), and on the other hand, the danger does not contradict the rescue, and the rescue is certain: "you are able to save him."

Such an example is found in the combination of the two or three danger scenarios mentioned above, as follows.

D. Explaining the matter:
Rashi has already established a great principle in his commentary (many times): "The Torah speaks in the language of the common occurrence" (*davar hakatuv bahoveh*). It is understood that, accordingly, Rashi also brings examples from the common and ordinary. This is the case here as well: the example Rashi brings is a "common" and ordinary situation (especially in Rashi’s time) where the fear of danger to the rescuer is such that it does not cast doubt on the act of rescue.

That is to say:
According to the ordinary course of events, the danger of bandits or a wild beast applies to a person who is traveling alone, without a caravan, etc. Thus, a person who encounters his neighbor (while traveling on the road) when a wild beast or bandits are coming upon him, is not, in the ordinary course of events, in a situation where "you are able to save him" (certainly). On the contrary, he is in the very same danger as his neighbor, and there is no certainty of ability or rescue.

Therefore, Rashi brings the example of "drowning in a river," which is a common and ordinary situation in general, and also in the specific detail of "you are able to save him"—for when a person is drowning in a river, it is because...


## אמור

## Sicha 1

### 127

1. The order of the festivals in the section of the festivals (in our Parsha) follows the order of the months, which begin with the month of Nissan ("This month shall be for you the first of the months of the year"). After the introduction of the mitzvah of Shabbos, it explains the mitzvah of the festival of Pesach (the 15th of Nissan), the mitzvah of offering the Omer (the 16th of that month), the counting of the Omer, the offering of the two loaves on the festival of Shavuos and the holy convocation of that day, Rosh Hashanah, Yom Kippur, and Sukkos.

Rabbeinu HaKadosh arranged several tractates in Seder Moed according to this order, as the Rambam writes that "Rabbeinu HaKadosh followed the section of the festivals in Parshas Emor"—which is why he began with Maseches Shabbos, and after Maseches Eruvin ("which is a matter pertaining to Shabbos"), he arranged Maseches Pesachim, which "is the second mitzvah after Shabbos in the section of the festivals, etc.".

However, in his *Sefer HaYad* (Mishneh Torah), in *Sefer Zemanim*, which includes "all the mitzvos that are at specific times, such as Shabbos and the festivals", the Rambam arranged the laws differently: at the beginning of *Sefer Zemanim* are the laws of Shabbos and Eruvin (as in the Mishnah), followed by the laws of *Shevisas Asor* (Yom Kippur), *Shevisas Yom Tov*, *Chametz U’Matzah*, *Shofar*, *Sukkah*, and *Lulav*, etc.

The *Maggid Mishneh* explains this order by noting that he began with the laws of Shabbos because "the mitzvah of Shabbos is stated in the Tablets with great publicity, and because it is more severe than the others... and because Shabbos is constant... and because the reason for its mitzvah is the creation of the world, and it is a fundamental pillar of the faith of the Torah of Moshe Rabbeinu, peace be upon him." Afterward, he arranged "the laws of Eruvin, which follow the laws of Shabbos and whose subject matter is of the same nature as Shabbos... the laws of *Shevisas Asor*, and it would have been appropriate to place them before the others because it is more severe than the others... and because it is a day of atonement... and because the prohibition of work on it is equal to the prohibition of Shabbos... it is appropriate to place its laws adjacent to the laws of Shabbos... the laws of *Shevisas Yom Tov*, which includes the three festivals and Rosh Hashanah, because the prohibition of work in them is equal, and it is appropriate for the general category to precede the specific... the laws of *Chametz U’Matzah*, as our teacher now comes to explain in detail the obligations of every Yom Tov beyond the prohibition of work, and he began with the explanation of the matters of the festival of Pesach because it is mentioned in the Torah first, and because the month of Nissan, in which Pesach falls, is the first of the months of the year, and because the matter of Chametz carries the punishment of *kares*, and the more severe precedes the less... the laws of *Shofar*, *Sukkah*, and *Lulav*... and their matters are in the seventh month... and they are positive mitzvos, etc." [He then continues to explain the reason for the order of the remaining laws in *Sefer Zemanim*: *Shekalim*, *Kiddush HaChodesh*, *Ta'anis*, (mitzvos of Torah origin, and afterward mitzvos of Rabbinic origin—) *Megillah* and *Chanukah*].

### 128

Seemingly, this requires explanation: The fact that he did not include in *Sefer Zemanim* the laws of offering the Omer, the two loaves, and the sacrificial offerings of all the festivals mentioned in the "section of the festivals" is understandable, as the Rambam arranged them according to their subject matter in *Sefer Avodah* and *Sefer Korbanos*. But regarding the laws of the festivals and holidays themselves, since the content of this book is, as its name suggests, *Sefer Zemanim*—"mitzvos that are at specific times"—what is the strength of the aforementioned reasons in the *Maggid Mishneh* to arrange the laws of the festivals in a manner different from how the festivals are arranged in the Written Torah (and in the Mishnah)?

It also requires explanation why he delayed the laws of *Kiddush HaChodesh* until after all the laws of the festivals—near the end of *Sefer Zemanim*. Especially since the determination of all the festivals depends on *Kiddush HaChodesh*, as the Rambam writes, "It is a positive commandment from the Torah for the Beis Din to calculate and know, etc., and to send and inform the rest of the people on which day is Rosh Chodesh, so that they will know on which day the festivals fall, as it is said, 'which you shall proclaim as holy convocations'". If so, the Rambam should have placed the laws of *Kiddush HaChodesh* before all the laws of the festivals in *Sefer Zemanim* (i.e., even before the laws of *Shevisas Asor*), just as it is in the Written Torah, where before the section of the festivals, the verses "which you shall proclaim as holy convocations" (the aforementioned Rambam) and "These are the festivals of Hashem, holy convocations, which you shall proclaim in their appointed times" precede it.

Furthermore: In the enumeration of the mitzvos at the beginning of *Sefer HaYad* (and similarly in *Sefer HaMitzvos*), the Rambam does indeed count the mitzvos according to their order in the year, and according to their order in the Written Torah in the section of the festivals. He begins with Shabbos, and afterward the mitzvos of Pesach together with the mitzvah of the prohibition of work on it, the counting of the Omer, the prohibition of work on Shavuos, Rosh Hashanah, and Yom Kippur (and the fast on it), the prohibition of work on the first and eighth days of Sukkos,

### 129

the mitzvos of *Sukkah* and *Lulav*. And the mitzvah of *Kiddush HaChodesh* comes before the mitzvos of all the festivals, even before Shabbos, just as in the Written Torah, where the verse "the festivals of Hashem, which you shall proclaim as holy convocations" also comes before the commandment of Shabbos in our Parsha.

2. This can be understood by first explaining the verse that the Rambam places before *Sefer Zemanim*—"I have inherited Your testimonies forever, for they are the joy of my heart".

It must be understood: It is simple that the verses the Rambam places before every book of *Sefer HaYad* serve as a kind of introduction and opening to the (content of the) laws in that book. Seemingly, the content of this verse (not only is it unrelated to the laws in *Sefer Zemanim*, but) is the opposite of the book's content: *Sefer Zemanim* contains "all the mitzvos that are at specific times," i.e., mitzvos and laws whose obligation is not constant but only at specific times—Shabbos, Yom Kippur, Yom Tov, *Shofar*, *Sukkah*, *Lulav*, etc. Conversely, the verse "I have inherited Your testimonies forever" indicates and emphasizes the concept of constancy in the mitzvos, that they are a constant inheritance and heritage, the opposite of the emphasis on these mitzvos being at specific times.

Furthermore: The Rambam himself emphasizes that this is the distinction between the mitzvos of *Sefer Ahavah* and the mitzvos of *Sefer Zemanim*. In *Sefer Ahavah*, "I will include all the mitzvos that are constant, which we were commanded in order to love the Place and to remember Him always, such as *Krias Shema*, *Tefillah*, *Tefillin*, *Birkas Kohanim*, and *Milah* in general, because it is a sign in our flesh to remember always at a time when there is no *Tefillin* or *Tzitzis*, etc." This is in contrast to *Sefer Zemanim*, where "I will include all the mitzvos that are at specific times," as mentioned above.

Since the Rambam himself defines the distinction between *Sefer Zemanim* and the book preceding it by the fact that the mitzvos within it are not constant, it is highly puzzling why he would preface this book with a verse that emphasizes the constancy of the mitzvos—"I have inherited Your testimonies forever."

[It is forced to say that it is because of the end of the verse, "for they are the joy of my heart," since *Yomim Tovim* are festivals for joy, for this is not the content of the mitzvos in this book, according to what the Rambam himself wrote as mentioned above, and it is obvious that this does not apply to the mitzvos of the entire book, such as Yom Kippur, *Shekalim*, and others.]

It can be said that in this verse, the Rambam comes to define the essence of *Sefer Zemanim*, and this also explains the order of the laws in this book, as follows.

3. The explanation of the matter:
Mitzvos whose obligation is at a specific time have two aspects: the application of the mitzvah to the time, that this time possesses this mitzvah, and the application of the obligation to the person. It can be said that these two orders correspond to these two aspects: from the perspective of the application to the time, it is appropriate to arrange the obligations according to the order of the times in the year; and from the perspective of the application of the obligation to the person, the obligation on the person is a constant obligation, and therefore the order of the laws corresponds to their content (as per the *Maggid Mishneh* and similar interpretations).

This is the difference between the order in the Written Torah (and in the Mishnah) and the order of the Rambam:
In the section of the festivals in the Torah, the concept of the times is emphasized—that there are specific times that are distinct and special from the rest of the days of the year, and as a result of their greatness, a person must proclaim them as holy convocations and fulfill the obligations and mitzvos pertaining to them.

Therefore, the introduction to the section of the festivals is "the festivals of Hashem, which you shall proclaim as holy convocations, these are the festivals," "These are the festivals of Hashem, holy convocations, which you shall proclaim in their appointed times" (and it emphasizes the determination of the time of each festival—"In the first month, on the fourteenth day of the month between the evenings, is the Pesach for Hashem, and on the fifteenth day of this month is the festival of Matzos for Hashem, etc.", etc.). This means that there are special days that are distinct from other days, "the festivals of Hashem," and consequently, a person must fulfill the mitzvos on them, the prohibition of work, and the fulfillment of the mitzvah of eating Matzah, etc.

However, the laws of the festivals as they are in the Rambam's book, in the book of laws and human obligations—their essence is based on the obligations of the person: rest on Shabbos, rest on *Asor*, rest on *Yom Tov*, fulfillment of the mitzvos—the laws of *Chametz U’Matzah*, *Shofar*, *Sukkah*, and *Lulav*, without emphasizing the uniqueness of the time (Yom Kippur, the festival of Pesach, etc.) that causes the obligations and warnings for the person.

Furthermore, it can be said that the definition of the obligation on the person (in the book of laws) is that there are certain human obligations: rest from work, rest from eating and drinking, fulfillment of specific mitzvos—and the Torah established special times in which a person must fulfill these mitzvos and warnings (in practice).

### 131

Therefore, the laws of the festivals are presented not according to the order of the times, but according to their order in content, in the fulfillment of the person, as per the *Maggid Mishneh*:
They begin with the warnings and prohibitions, the laws of Shabbos and the laws of *Shevisas Asor*—"Any work for which one is liable to *sekilah* for intentional violation on Shabbos, one is liable to *kares* for intentional violation on *Asor*, etc.", and the laws of *Shevisas Yom Tov*—"The rest of all of them (the 'six days': the first and seventh of Pesach, the first and eighth of Sukkos, the day of the festival of Shavuos, the day of Rosh Hashanah) is equal, that they are prohibited in all work of labor except for work that is for the need of eating, etc." Afterward, he explains the obligations and fulfillments of the mitzvos of the festivals.

4. This is why the Rambam prefaced *Sefer Zemanim* with the verse "I have inherited Your testimonies forever, for they are the joy of my heart," because this verse defines and emphasizes this principle regarding temporary obligations:
When speaking about mitzvos whose obligation is constant and frequent, there is no need for proof from verses that they possess the element of constancy.
But when speaking about *Sefer Zemanim*—mitzvos that are at fixed and specific times, such as Shabbos and the festivals, and not mitzvos whose obligation is constant—the Rambam emphasizes: Do not think that the entirety of these obligations is merely a result of the times. Rather, they are also constant obligations, "I have inherited Your testimonies forever," as mentioned above. According to the Rambam, the definition of the mitzvos of these times is a constant obligation on the person, such that the warning of rest on Shabbos and *Yom Tov*, for example, "enslaves" the person "forever", in such a way that for the person, there is the enslavement of the concept of rest in a constant manner.

This is also the precision of "I have inherited Your testimonies forever," that "they are like an inheritance to me... that I am held in them like a person who is held in his inheritance forever, so that I will not depart from them for one hour without holding onto them".

This is also what continues, "for they are the joy of my heart": If the obligations of the festivals were only a result of the time, this would be a matter that is not constant. But since they are the "joy of my heart" of the person, that the person feels joy in his heart that he was commanded in them, this matter is not only at the time of their actual fulfillment but "I have inherited Your testimonies forever," as mentioned above.

5. It should be added regarding the content of the intent of the laws of the mitzvos of *Sefer Zemanim*, based on the precision of the word "Your testimonies" (*edosecha*), for as is known, Shabbos and *Yom Tov* are mitzvos that fall under the category of "testimony" (*eidus*). In the words of the Rambam (at the end of the laws of Shabbos), "And Shabbos is the sign between the Holy One, blessed be He, and us forever" (and similarly regarding *Yomim Tovim*, "for they are all Sabbaths of Hashem").

One must explain the precision of the Rambam's language, "And Shabbos is the sign between the Holy One, blessed be He, and us forever"—that the fact that the sign of Shabbos is "forever" is not a secondary matter, but is necessitated by the very fact that Shabbos is a "sign" and "testimony." For true "testimony" is the establishment of the reality through which it is forever (for through the testimony, it is established forever that such was the reality). So it is with the "testimony" of "Shabbos," that the sign

### 132

between Israel and the Holy One, blessed be He, which exists through Shabbos, is not only on the day of Shabbos, but is a sign "forever," existing in a constant manner.

It turns out that the definition of "forever"—constancy—in these mitzvos of *Sefer Zemanim* is not only because of the obligation of the person, that the obligation upon him is constant, but also because the content of these mitzvos exists "forever." Simply put, the effect of the mitzvos at these times also acts upon all the time in between: Shabbos—on all six days of the week; *Yom Tov*—on the time between one *Yom Tov* and the next; *Shevisas Asor*—on the entire year until the next *Shevisas Asor*.

So it is with the effect of fulfilling the mitzvos at specific times: the mitzvah of recounting the Exodus from Egypt and the other mitzvos and actions in the laws of *Chametz U’Matzah*, their essence and content is that they should remember the Exodus from Egypt throughout the entire year. This is as it is literally, that the remembrance of the Exodus from Egypt every day comes and is drawn from the remembrance of the Exodus from Egypt in speech and action on the night of Pesach. Similarly with the mitzvos of *Shofar*, *Sukkah*, and *Lulav*, these actions influence and act upon the entire year.

This is emphasized most strongly in the words of the Rambam at the end of the laws of *Lulav*, where he concludes regarding the joy, "that a person should rejoice in the performance of the mitzvah and in the love of the Almighty who commanded them," which is "a great service... and anyone who prevents himself from this joy, etc." Seemingly: "More than our teacher (the Rambam) was wise in the laws of the Torah, etc., he also balanced, investigated, and corrected through the excellence of his arrangement in his books", and every law he wrote in his book, he wrote in its proper place. The obligation of joy in the performance of the mitzvos and the love of the Almighty does not belong to the laws of *Sukkah* and *Lulav*; why did the Rambam include it here? Rather, the power for the joy of a mitzvah and the love of the Almighty that a person must have always [for one must love Hashem always, and fulfill His mitzvos—for "I was created to serve my Creator"], is drawn from the joy that the Rambam explains there beforehand—the "extra joy" on the festival of Sukkos.

The Rambam is informing us that this is not only a law regarding the joy of a mitzvah, which comes and is drawn from the "extra joy" on the festival of Sukkos, but (also) conversely, that the joy a person should have in the performance of the mitzvah is a law in the laws of *Sukkah* and *Lulav*. That is, it is a detail of the content of these laws, meaning: the perfection of the definition of the joy of *Yom Tov*, and especially the extra joy of the festival of Sukkos, is when the festival acts and influences within the person an extra joy of mitzvah always, all the days.

6. More deeply, it can be said that the definition of "forever" in these mitzvos is not only in the fulfillment of the person, that their fulfillment at this time influences the person's actions throughout the entire year, but also regarding the very object (*cheftza*) of "time."

This answers why the Rambam called this book *Sefer Zemanim* and not *Sefer Moed(im)*. Seemingly, what is the reason he changed it from the name as it is in the Mishnah, *Seder Moed*, especially since "Moed" indicates a special time—whereas "Zeman" (Zemanim) is something that exists and is present in the very creation.

The explanation for this is:

### 133

Regarding mitzvos whose fulfillment depends on time, it can be said there are two ways (similar to the above): a) That the time exists and is distinct in itself as a special day, Shabbos and *Yom Tov* (through the sanctification of the Beis Din, etc.), and this time causes the person who is in this time to fulfill the mitzvos within it. b) That the essence is the obligation of the mitzvos of the person, but their actual fulfillment is conditional upon this time, and for this way, seemingly, the person and the time have no direct connection.

This is what the Rambam informs us by arranging the laws not according to the order of their fixed time in the order of the year (as in the Written Torah), and writing primarily the aspect of the human obligation therein. On the other hand, he calls the book *Sefer Zemanim* (and not *Sefer Moed*)—to emphasize that even though in their essence they are obligations upon the person, nevertheless, the greatness of the fulfillment of the mitzvos of the person is that it acts within time, and even more—that it acts upon the very object of time. This means that not only does he fulfill mitzvos at a time that is already distinct from other times—the days of the festival—but he acts upon the very object of time, even in time that is not sanctified with the holiness of Shabbos and *Yom Tov*.

This is emphasized most strongly in the fact that the Rambam did not place the laws of *Kiddush HaChodesh* before all the mitzvos that depend on specific times, and at the same time calls it *Sefer Zemanim*, to inform us that the main innovation in *Sefer Zemanim* is not (only) that the Beis Din sanctifies months and intercalates years, that Rosh Chodesh and the days of *Yom Tov* are sanctified, that these days are distinct from the rest of the days of the year in their holiness, but rather the action that is done in the body of the object of time (which is not a festival), and this is through the fulfillment of the mitzvos of the person.

It can be said that this is the (inner) interpretation of the verse that the Rambam brings, "I have inherited Your testimonies forever," that through the fulfillment of the mitzvos of "Your testimonies" in the manner of "I have inherited," he acts "forever." This means he renews and adds to the very definition of time, for time itself is defined by changes and divisions, and the person, by fulfilling the mitzvos that depend on these times, *achshavei* (elevates/regards as important) the time, acting within it with fulfillment and constancy.

(From the Sichos of Erev Shavuos 5744, 5745)

## Sicha 2

### 134

A. We learned in the Mishnah: The Omer permits the new grain in the country (to eat the *chadash* in all places), and the Two Loaves permit it in the Beis Hamikdash (for before the Two Loaves, one may not bring a *mincha* from the new grain). One may not bring *minchos*, etc., before the Omer; if one did bring them, they are invalid. Before the Two Loaves, one should not bring them; if one did bring them, they are valid.

Regarding the reason for the distinction between the Omer and the Two Loaves—that "before the Omer, if one brought it, it is invalid," whereas "before the Two Loaves... if one brought it, it is valid"—we find (in the commentaries) several approaches, which depend on the definition of the "permission" granted by the offering of the Two Loaves (and the prohibition against bringing a *mincha* from new grain before the Two Loaves). This can be explained in three ways:

1) The prohibition of *chadash* in the Beis Hamikdash before the Two Loaves is the very same prohibition of *chadash* that existed before the Omer. However, before the Omer, it was forbidden both to a person and to the Altar, whereas after the Omer, it was permitted to a person, yet remained forbidden to the Altar.

According to this view, the reason that "before the Two Loaves... if one brought it, it is valid" is because once "it was permitted in its general sense to a layperson," "the power of the prohibition is diminished since it has taken on a partial permission, such that the prohibition is no longer as severe as it was initially; therefore, even for the Altar, if one brought it, it is valid."

2) The prohibition of *chadash* for the Altar (which is permitted through the Two Loaves) is a separate prohibition (and is not included in the *chadash* prohibition of a layperson). This prohibition (for the Altar) is only *l'chatchilah* (initially) and is not *l'ikuva* (a requirement for validity)—for regarding *kodshim*, there is a rule that we require the verse to explicitly state that a matter is essential, and here, regarding the Two Loaves, we do not find that the verse explicitly stated it as essential.

[However, "before the Omer," when it is forbidden to a layperson, it is also invalidated for the Altar (even *b'dieved*—after the fact)—"because we require it to be from the 'produce of Israel'" (meaning, from that which is permitted to an Israelite). Since "before the Omer" it is forbidden to a layperson, it is also invalid for the Altar.]

### 135

3) The Omer and the Two Loaves differ in their very essence: The Omer is in the category of a "permitter"—meaning, the Torah prohibited the new grain, and it is only permitted through the offering of the Omer. In contrast, regarding the Two Loaves, the Torah did not impose a prohibition against offering new grain before the Two Loaves—for we do not find a prohibition for this in the verse (as we do regarding the Omer, where it is explicitly written, "And bread, and parched corn, and fresh ears you shall not eat..."). Rather, the requirement is that the Two Loaves must be a "new *mincha*" (the first offering from the new grain). This is the reason why it is forbidden to offer a *mincha* from new grain before the Two Loaves, because then the Two Loaves would not be a "new *mincha*".

This is the reason that "before the Two Loaves... if one brought it, it is valid"—because "the invalidity is not in the offering itself, but rather that a deficiency was created in the mitzvah of the Two Loaves" (in that they will not be a "new *mincha*").

[And a practical difference in *halacha*—according to this third approach, if one transgressed and brought a *mincha* from the new grain before the Two Loaves, one could say that there is no longer a prohibition against bringing a *mincha* from the new grain before the Two Loaves, since in any case the Two Loaves will not be a "new *mincha*".]

B. Our Sages have a rule that every opinion (and reasoning) brought in the Torah has a place, for "these and these are the words of the living G-d." Even though the *halachic* ruling is only according to one opinion, there is still a place (in reasoning) for the other opinions as well.

And on a deeper level: The rule that "these and these are the words of the living G-d" informs us that in addition to all opinions being part of our Torah regarding their study, every opinion has a place in the actual *avodah* of Hashem. For the fact that the *halacha* is ruled according to one opinion is only regarding practical conduct, as it is impossible to act in practice according to two contradictory opinions; but regarding the spirituality of the *mitzvos*, there is a place for all opinions.

Meaning: Every reasoning and opinion in the Torah has a conclusion regarding spiritual *avodah* as well—a matter in which it is possible (and sometimes even necessary) to fulfill, even if regarding practical *halacha*, the ruling is not according to it.

So too in our case—the three aforementioned reasonings regarding the Two Loaves are three modes of *avodah* and conduct of the soul [especially since we are speaking of a sacrifice, and a primary aspect of sacrifices is the intention and thought of the person at the time of offering the sacrifice, as the *Chinuch* writes regarding the mitzvah of the Two Loaves that "the matter of the sacrifice... is that through the action, the person’s thought will be aroused toward the

### 136

matters, and therefore, according to the importance of the sacrifice and its proper preparation, the person’s heart will be aroused toward it more."]

C. We must first preface the difference between the "Omer" and the "Two Loaves" in the *avodah* of the soul—which stems from the simple difference between them: The Omer comes from barley, which is animal food, and the Two Loaves are from wheat, which is human food.

The explanation for this is well-known: The Omer, which is brought on the festival of Pesach (the time of the Exodus from Egypt), alludes to the beginning of a person’s *avodah* (immediately upon leaving Egypt), when the "animal" and the evil within his soul are still in their strength. The bringing of the Omer from barley, animal food, alludes to the offering of the "animal" within the person (his animal soul) to Hashem, for he must slaughter his *yetzer* and subjugate it to His service, blessed be He.

This is the content of the *avodah* of the days of *Sefiras HaOmer*, which come in succession to the offering of the Omer, during which a person refines and clarifies his evil character traits and purifies himself from his filth (as we say in the *Yehi Ratzon* after *Sefiras HaOmer*, that "You have commanded us... to count *Sefiras HaOmer* in order to purify us from our *kelipos* and our impurities... so that they may be purified... from their filth").

In contrast, on the festival of Shavuos, which comes after the completion of *Sefiras HaOmer*, we bring the Two Loaves from wheat, human food, because from now on he is at the level of "man," whose closeness to Hashem is (not only the *bitul* and clarification of the "animal" within him, but) the elevation and drawing near of the "man" ("the spirit of the children of man that ascends upward") within him.

And since he has already ascended to *avodah* in holiness itself, his (primary) *avodah* is not in the nullification of his existence, but in an orderly *avodah* according to the *taam* (reason) and *daas* (knowledge) of holiness, the content of which is ascents within holiness itself (the study of Torah and the fulfillment of *mitzvos*).

This is also the reason that the Two Loaves are brought as *chametz*—"They shall be baked leavened": At the beginning of the *avodah*, when the "animal" in man is in its strength, the *avodah* is by way of coercion and acceptance of the yoke, and this is alluded to in "matzah," which is "bread of affliction", which has no "taste". In contrast, after the person elevates until his *avodah* is within holiness itself, and consequently through understanding and comprehension of holiness, it possesses "taste" and *taanug*.

D. Based on this, it is also understood why the Omer permits *chadash* to a layperson, and the Two Loaves—to the Altar:

The offering of the "first of your harvest" of barley (animal food) to Hashem, the content of which is the *bitul* and clarification of the "layperson" (the mundane) within man, relates to the mundane actions of the person, meaning the permissible matters of the person. For before he can derive benefit from the new grain for his needs, he must offer the Omer sacrifice from the barley [meaning the slaughtering of the "animal" within him], which thereby establishes in his heart that his involvement in "all your deeds" will be "for the sake of Heaven" (similar to

### 137

the obligation to pray beforehand (which permits) the doing of his affairs, the obligation to recite a blessing before deriving benefit from this world, etc.).

In contrast, the offering of the Two Loaves pertains to the Altar (the Divine)—meaning the person’s *avodah* in matters of holiness, Torah and *mitzvos*. For after he has elevated to the level of "man" and performs his *avodah* according to *taam* and *daas*, then in order for his *avodah* in holiness itself to be as it should, there must be the preface of offering the Two Loaves.

The explanation for this:
The foundation of all *avodah* (even *avodah* of *taam* and *daas*) is *bitul* and acceptance of the yoke, the precedence of "we will do" to "we will hear" [as is explained in many places that this is the concept of the blessing on the Torah—that one must "bless the Torah first", for before studying Torah with understanding and comprehension, there must be the blessing and the acknowledgment that "He gave us His Torah," which is the Torah of the Holy One, blessed be He].

This is the concept of the offering of the Two Loaves, for the manner of their offering is that they bring two sheep and wave the sheep together with the bread—the connection of the sheep with the bread. Bread is an allusion to Torah, the understanding of man; and a sheep indicates *bitul* (for sheep "are weak by nature... and the nature of *bitul* is felt in them")—and this is the content of offering the Two Loaves with the two sheep: to infuse the movement of *bitul* into the understanding and comprehension of man.

[And this matter is also alluded to in the offering of the bread itself—that they bring "Two Loaves" specifically:
It is explained in the *Zohar* that the "Two Loaves" refer to the Written Torah and the Oral Torah. The essence of the Oral Torah is the understanding of man, and according to the *halachic* ruling, in the Oral Torah, if one does not understand what he is studying, "it is not considered study at all". In contrast, the Written Torah is not as clothed in the understanding of man [for without the explanation of the Oral Torah, it is impossible to understand the Written Torah in its truth], which is why one fulfills the mitzvah of Torah study even by reading the Written Torah even if the reader does not know what he is saying (and he recites a blessing on this reading), and therefore, in the study of the Written Torah, the *bitul* to the Giver of the Torah is felt more.

The offering of the "Two Loaves" indicates the connection of the two parts of the Torah, that the understanding and comprehension of the Oral Torah must be infused by the *bitul* that is in the Written Torah.]

### 138

E. According to this reasoning regarding the difference between the Omer and the Two Loaves, the reason for the aforementioned distinction is understood—that "before the Omer... if one brought it, it is invalid; before the Two Loaves... if one brought it, it is valid":

The offering of the Omer—the "animal" within him—which relates to the mundane actions of the person, is *l'ikuva* (essential). For if he does not offer his *yetzer* to Hashem, then not only will his physical matters not be done for the sake of Heaven, but on the contrary, they will pull the person downward, "the spirit of the animal that descends downward".

This is the reason that "before the Omer... if one brought it, it is invalid," because before the person has offered his animal soul to Hashem, and the evil within him is still in its strength, then even in his *avodah* in Torah and *mitzvos* (matters of the Divine), the evil of his animal soul is mixed in. Therefore, when it comes to a matter of *chadash*, new grain—and his *yetzer* (and nature) "preceded the claim"—it is forbidden for him to bring a sacrifice from it, and this invalidates his *avodah* in holiness (similar to what our Sages say regarding the study of Torah—"if he merits, it becomes a potion of life; if he does not merit, it becomes, etc.").

In contrast, the offering of the Two Loaves, which comes after the offering of the Omer (after the person has already offered his animal soul to Hashem), and whose purpose is for the *avodah* and elevation within holiness itself, so that it will be in greater perfection (through greater *bitul*)—is not *l'ikuva*, but only a matter of *l'chatchilah*, for the sake of the perfection of the *avodah*. But even if this perfection of the preface is missing, there is no invalidation, for the person is already at a level of holiness.

F. And the three aforementioned approaches regarding the definition of the Two Loaves can be said to correspond to three levels in the *avodah* of Hashem:

The first approach—that the prohibition of *chadash* for the Divine and the prohibition of *chadash* for the layperson are one prohibition—refers to a lower level in the *avodah* of Hashem, where even when the person is occupied with matters of holiness, there is room for the concern that if he does not have the necessary prefaces and preparations, then not only will he not ascend and go from strength to strength (in holiness), but on the contrary, he will fall from his level. Meaning: even if he has already offered the Omer (the "animal" within him), nevertheless, if he lacks the offering of the Two Loaves (the *bitul* as it should be), it is still possible that he will eventually fall from his level, etc.

At this level, the two prohibitions of *chadash*—for the layperson and for the Divine—are one definition, namely, the concern that the person will fall into matters of the opposite, etc., and the sacrifices—the Omer and the Two Loaves—are both in the category of a "permitter," to permit the connection with materiality, to ensure that the *avodah* that follows will be without a mixture of evil and invalidity.

[However, within this itself there is a difference between the Omer and the Two Loaves, for the offering of the Omer is *l'ikuva*, because otherwise the evil within the person is in its strength; in contrast, after the bringing of the Omer, the concern that he will fall into evil is weakened (and "the power of the prohibition is diminished"), and therefore, although *l'chatchilah* one must preface the offering of the Two Loaves (due to the concern that without this preface he will fall from his level)—nevertheless, since this is only a concern that his second nature will change (because it is "second" and there is the "preceded the claim"), it is not *l'ikuva* to invalidate the offering.]

### 139

The second approach—that the prohibition of *chadash* for the Divine is a separate prohibition, and *l'chatchilah* it is a light prohibition, for the verse did not state it as essential—refers to a higher level in *avodah*, when there is no concern that the person will fall into a state of evil, G-d forbid, but rather that the offering of the Two Loaves relates only to the perfection and beauty of his *avodah*. For when this preface of *bitul* exists, then all his subsequent *avodah* is at a more elevated level; for the more a person is *batel* to Hashem, his understanding in Torah is also at a higher level, such that he aims more toward the truth of Torah.

But even according to this opinion, the Two Loaves are in the category of a "permitter," meaning that their purpose is a preface to the *avodah* that follows, so that it will be as it should.

However, the highest level in the *avodah* of Hashem is—that the prohibition of *chadash* for the Divine is only so that the Two Loaves will be a "new *mincha*." Meaning: the sacrifice of the Two Loaves is not a preface to the *avodah* that follows, but is a matter in itself; this is his *avodah*, to connect the "Two Loaves" (the Written Torah and the Oral Torah).

And in order for this *avodah* to be as it should, the Torah said that it should come as a "new *mincha*," for the beginning of everything is its choicest part, and also, that in the doing of a new thing there is more vitality and *taanug*. Therefore, in order for this elevated *avodah* of offering the Two Loaves to be in the ultimate perfection, it must be a "new *mincha*."

(From the *Sichos* of Shavuos 5711, 5714)

## Sicha 3

### 140

A. The author [of the Shulchan Aruch] writes at the beginning of the Laws of Sukkah: "‘In Sukkos you shall dwell for seven days... for in Sukkos I caused the Children of Israel to dwell,’ these are the Clouds of Glory with which He surrounded them, so that the heat and the sun would not strike them." His source is the Tur, who cites the language of the verses: "‘In Sukkos you shall dwell for seven days... so that your generations may know that I caused the Children of Israel to dwell in Sukkos when I brought them out...’" Further on, the matter is explained: "The Sukkos that the verse says He caused us to dwell in are His Clouds of Glory with which He surrounded them so that the heat and the sun would not strike them, and as a symbol of this, He commanded us to make Sukkos so that we remember His wonders and His awe-inspiring deeds."

This requires clarification: Is it not the custom of the author to avoid explaining the reasons for the laws or citing their source in the Written Torah, and instead only to rule the law for practical application?

The later authorities explain that this is indeed a law: there is an obligation to have in mind, at the time of fulfilling the mitzvah of dwelling in the Sukkah, that it is a remembrance of the "Clouds of Glory with which He surrounded them." This is along the lines of the words of the Bach, who raised this very question regarding the Tur (that he "spoke at length to explain and expound upon the verse ‘In Sukkos you shall dwell’"), and he explains that "he holds that since it is written ‘so that they may know,’ one does not fulfill the mitzvah as it should be if one does not know the intent of the mitzvah of Sukkah according to its plain meaning."

However, the Beis Yosef disagrees with the Bach regarding the nature of this intent. The Bach holds that "the essence of the intent in dwelling in the Sukkah is that one should remember the Exodus from Egypt" (and according to his view, this is the intent of the Tur, who explained here the reason that "the verse links the mitzvah of Sukkah to the Exodus from Egypt," to inform us that the mitzvah of Sukkah is a remembrance of the Exodus). The Beis Yosef, however, holds that the mitzvah of Sukkah is a remembrance of this specific miracle of the "Clouds of Glory with which He surrounded them so that the heat and the sun would not strike them," and it is not a remembrance of the Exodus from Egypt.
