Therefore, the author changed the language of the Tur and did not copy the end of the verse, "when I brought them out, etc." (writing only, "In Sukkos you shall dwell... for in Sukkos I caused the Children of Israel to dwell, these are the Clouds of Glory, etc."), because in his opinion, a person is obligated to have in mind only that the mitzvah of Sukkah is a remembrance of the Clouds of Glory.

## 141

[With this, we can also understand the precision of the language of the Alter Rebbe in his Shulchan Aruch, where he changed the language of the author and copied (as the Tur did) also the end of the verse, "when I brought them out of the land of Egypt," because the Alter Rebbe ruled according to the view of the Bach that "every person must have in mind when sitting in the Sukkah that he is sitting there in order to fulfill the mitzvah of the Holy One, Blessed be He, who commanded us to sit in a Sukkah as a remembrance of the Exodus from Egypt." Therefore, he copied the words "when I brought them out of the land of Egypt" at the beginning of the section, because this is the essence of "so that they may know"—the knowledge and the intent that the mitzvah of Sukkah is a remembrance of the Exodus from Egypt.]

B. One must examine this: If so, the author should have written explicitly, "And he should have in mind when sitting, etc.," just as he wrote in the Laws of Tzitzis, "He should have in mind when wrapping himself, etc., so that we may remember, etc.," and similarly in the Laws of Tefillin, he wrote, "He should have in mind when placing them, etc., so that we may remember, etc." [And as explained by the Bach, that in these three mitzvos—Tzitzis, Tefillin, and Sukkah—"the essence of the mitzvah and its fulfillment depends on its intent," since it is said regarding them, "so that, etc."].

It is even more difficult that even the Tur here did not write "And he should have in mind, etc.," as he did regarding Tzitzis and Tefillin. The simple reason for this is that he also copied from the verse the words "so that your generations may know," in which it is explicitly stated that the mitzvah is knowledge and intent; therefore, there is no place to write that the person must have in mind, because this is explicitly stated in the language of the verse that he cited.

Based on this, it is surprising that the author omitted these words ("so that your generations may know"), and there is nothing in his language ("In Sukkos you shall dwell... for in Sukkos I caused... these are the Clouds of Glory, etc.") except an explanation of the reason for the mitzvah, and there is no instruction or command to the person in this.

Therefore, it seems that the intent of the author should be interpreted (not that this reason is part of the intent of the mitzvah, but) that in this reason and in this verse, he is explaining the definition of the mitzvah of Sukkah, from which there are practical differences for several laws, as will be explained below.

C. This can be understood according to the language of the Alter Rebbe there, who wrote: "‘In Sukkos you shall dwell for seven days, so that your generations may know that I caused the Children of Israel to dwell in Sukkos when I brought them out of the land of Egypt,’ these were His Clouds of Glory with which He surrounded them for shade, so that the heat and the sun would not strike them, and as a symbol of this, He commanded us to make Sukkos that are made for shade, so that we may remember His wonders and His awe-inspiring deeds."

One must clarify the content of the Alter Rebbe's addition to the language of the Tur (and the Shulchan Aruch), "His Clouds of Glory with which He surrounded them for shade, so that the heat and the sun would not strike them." Seemingly, the fact that they were "for shade" is included in what is written, "so that the heat and the sun would not strike them." What is added by the words "for shade"? Similarly, in the continuation of his language, the Alter Rebbe adds to the language of the Tur—"and as a symbol of this, He commanded us to make Sukkos that are made for shade," for these words ("that are made for shade") are not in the Tur.

It can be said that he is teaching us a great deal: from the fact that the verse links the mitzvah of Sukkah to "His Clouds of Glory with which He surrounded them for shade," we learn that the definition of the mitzvah of Sukkah is that it must be "made for shade." That is to say, in addition to the condition of the Sukkah that its shade must be greater than its sun, the definition

## 142

and essence of the Sukkah (of the mitzvah) is—a Sukkah made for shade (and a Sukkah that lacks this condition does not have the name Sukkah at all).

[It can be said that this matter is one of the practical differences in law between the two opinions, whether "in Sukkos I caused to dwell" means "actual Sukkos" or "Clouds of Glory": according to the opinion that they were "actual Sukkos," the essence of the definition of a Sukkah is a temporary dwelling, similar to the Sukkos that the Israelites made in the desert, which were a "temporary dwelling"; whereas according to the opinion that they are "Clouds of Glory," the Sukkos that we make are a remembrance (only) of this detail of the Clouds of Glory, "with which He surrounded them for shade so that the heat and the sun would not strike them" (as below in paragraph 7), and therefore the definition of a Sukkah is that it is made for shade.]

This foundation is explicit in the language of the Alter Rebbe immediately at the beginning of the following section (in which he begins to explain the laws of actually making the Sukkah), where he wrote: "A person does not fulfill his obligation except with a Sukkah that is made only for shade, like the Clouds of Glory. But if it is made also for a dwelling... and not to sit in its shade alone, or if it is made also to use it for acts of modesty... or if it is made also for shelter and protection from a downpour and rain, as long as it is not made for shade alone, this is not a Sukkah but a house, etc."

In the Gemara, this condition is mentioned only regarding the validity of a Sukkah made by a thief, etc. (that even though these Sukkos were not made for the sake of the mitzvah, they are valid if they were made for the sake of shade), but the Alter Rebbe writes that this is a condition in every Sukkah, to the point that the intent of its making must be solely and exclusively for the sake of "shade alone," without a mixture of any other intent—"to use it (also) for acts of modesty," or even that it should be "also for shelter and protection from a downpour and rain" [even though it is explicit in the verse that this is

## 143

the purpose of a Sukkah, "And a Sukkah shall be for shade by day from the heat, and for shelter and for protection from a downpour and rain"]—because according to the Alter Rebbe, this is the definition of the mitzvah of Sukkah, that it must be made for shade alone, like the Clouds of Glory "with which He surrounded them for shade."

It can be said that this is why the Alter Rebbe began the laws of making the Sukkah with this law [unlike the Tur (and similarly the Shulchan Aruch), who did not mention this law here at all, and the section begins with the laws of "the proper place (for making a Sukkah), how?," that one should make it under the open sky, for it is written "In Sukkos you shall dwell," and we expound that it is not in a Sukkah that is under a Sukkah, nor in a Sukkah that is under a house, nor in a Sukkah that is under a tree"], because this is not one of the conditions of the Sukkah, but rather this is the definition of a Sukkah, that "as long as it is not made for shade alone, this is not a Sukkah."

Based on this, it can be said that the intent of the Alter Rebbe here (in copying the language of the author, and adding to it (a) the word "(with which He surrounded them) for shade," (b) and in the language of the Tur, "and as a symbol of this, He commanded us to make Sukkos," he adds "that are made for shade") is to explain the words of the author, that in the fact that the author brought the opinion that "in Sukkos I caused to dwell" are "the Clouds of Glory with which He surrounded them so that the heat and the sun would not strike them," his intent is to define that also the Sukkos that we were commanded to make, their definition is that they must be "made for shade."

D. It should be added here, and by way of introduction, that it is difficult why the Alter Rebbe added the word "for shade"—"His Clouds of Glory with which He surrounded them for shade, so that the heat and the sun would not strike them"—for even though this is in accordance with the continuation of his language, "He commanded us to make Sukkos that are made for shade" (as above), this is a redundancy, and even if he had written only "so that the heat and the sun would not strike them," the continuation to this, that "He commanded us to make Sukkos that are made for shade," would have been understood.

And according to the above, that the intent of the Alter Rebbe here is to define the essence of a Sukkah in law, it can be said that this precision also touches upon the law.

This can be understood by way of introduction to the simple difference between "for shade" or "so that the heat and the sun would not strike them": in "so that the heat and the sun would not strike them," the benefit to the person sitting in the Sukkah is emphasized, that the heat of the sun does not strike him; in "for shade," the reality of the "shade" (created by the Sukkah) in and of itself is emphasized.

It can be said that there is a practical difference between them regarding the law that its shade must be greater than its sun, whether this is a measurement in the *sechach*, that its shade must be greater than its sun, or whether the essence is that for the person sitting in the Sukkah, the shade must be greater than the sun.

According to this—in the fact that the Alter Rebbe adds that the Clouds of Glory were "for shade, so that the heat and the sun would not strike them," he hints that in the shade of the Sukkah there are two laws: (a) from the side of the *sechach*—similar to the Clouds of Glory that were "for shade," (b) from the side of the person, just as the Clouds of Glory acted "so that the heat and the sun would not strike them (the person)."

With this, one can explain the words of the Alter Rebbe regarding the law of its shade being greater than its sun, and his words are: "If

## 144

the *sechach* is not greater than the open space, but they are equal, it is invalid, because the sun that enters the open space of the *sechach*, when it reaches the floor of the Sukkah, spreads out more than it is above in the open space of the *sechach*, and it turns out that the Sukkah below at its floor has more sun than shade." And in the following section, he continues, "But if below at its floor its sun and its shade are equal, it is valid, because it is known that above in the *sechach* the open space is less than the *sechach*, and its shade above is greater than its sun, and this is a complete *sechach*."

Seemingly, his words contradict each other, for from his words in the first law, it follows that if the shade and the sun were equal, the Sukkah should have been valid, and the reason that we invalidate it is only because "the Sukkah below at its floor has more sun than shade." Whereas from his words in the second law, it follows the opposite, that if the shade and the sun are equal, this is not enough to validate the Sukkah, and the fact that the Sukkah is valid when they are equal below is only because "its shade above is greater than its sun."

Also, one must understand the precision of his language there, that "if below... its sun and its shade are equal, it is valid because... (that) its shade above is greater than its sun, and this is a complete *sechach*." Seemingly, this is a redundancy, and why is the reason that "its shade above is greater than its sun" not enough?

And according to the above, it can be said that the measurement of the *sechach* "above" and the measurement of the *sechach* "below" are two different laws, distinct in their very definition: the law of the shade of the Sukkah in relation to the person ("below") is primarily a negative law, meaning it is invalid if for him its sun is greater than its shade, because then it is not similar to the Clouds of Glory that acted "so that the heat and the sun would not strike them" (but if they are equal, there is no invalidation here); whereas the law regarding the *sechach* of the Sukkah ("above") is a positive matter, that the *sechach* of the Sukkah must be "for shade," and this is specifically when "its shade is greater than its sun," for then it is a "complete *sechach*" (but even when they are only equal, there is no invalidation here, it is just not a "complete *sechach*"—as per the precision of the Alter Rebbe's language).

And this is the difference between the two laws: the first law comes to teach us the law of the measurement of the *sechach* "below," which is invalid as above, and therefore the reason that we invalidate the Sukkah when above its shade and its sun are equal is because "the Sukkah below... has more sun than shade" (and because of this, the name "invalid" applies to the Sukkah); and the second law comes to inform us of the measurement of the *sechach* "above,"

## 145

which is a positive law, that a "complete *sechach*" is only when its shade is greater than its sun, and therefore, the reason that this Sukkah (where below they are equal) is valid is only because "its shade above is greater than its sun, and this is a complete *sechach*" (whereas if even above they were (only) equal, it is not a valid Sukkah, because it does not have a "complete *sechach*").

E. Furthermore, it can be said that in the fact that in the definition of the mitzvah of Sukkah the Alter Rebbe emphasizes that the Sukkah is "for shade" (as above), his intent is to hint that the essence of the definition of the Sukkah is not that for the person it is forbidden to have a situation where "its sun is greater than its shade" (similar to "so that the heat and the sun would not strike them"), but rather the obligation is that the *sechach* must be made "for shade," "its shade is greater than its sun."

The practical difference: if the *sechach* above has shade greater than its sun, but the Sukkah below has sun greater than its shade (due to "the sun that enters the open space of the *sechach*, when it reaches the floor of the Sukkah, spreads out more than it is above in the open space of the *sechach*"), it turns out that above there is a "complete *sechach*," but below there is the invalidation of its sun being greater than its shade.

Now, it is reasonable to say that since there is an invalidation in the Sukkah, what good is it that there is a "complete *sechach*" above? But according to the above (that the essence of the definition of the Sukkah is that the *sechach* must be made "for shade"), it can be said that if above there is a "complete *sechach*," we do not take into account what happens below at the floor of the Sukkah; for only when above they are equal (and there is no "complete *sechach*") is the Sukkah invalid when below its sun is greater than its shade, but if above there is a "complete *sechach*," it makes no difference to us that below its shade is not greater than its sun, since the essence of the definition of the Sukkah is that the Sukkah (meaning the *sechach*) is "made for shade."

[And based on this, perhaps one can add an explanation to the addition of the Alter Rebbe, who adds (as above) that when "its shade above is greater than its sun" it is valid because "this is a complete *sechach*"—that it can be said that his intent in this is also to teach us that when a person makes a "complete *sechach*" (where its shade is greater than its sun), it makes no difference to us that below its shade is not greater than its sun, since the essence of the definition of the Sukkah is that the Sukkah (meaning the *sechach*) is "made for shade."]

F. Now, the above explanation of the words of the Alter Rebbe, that his intent (at the beginning of the Laws of Sukkah) is to define the essence of a Sukkah (and not to explain the reasons for the mitzvah or its intent), is proven also from the continuation of his language in this section, that after he brought the words of the Tur and the Shulchan Aruch, "‘In Sukkos you shall dwell... in Sukkos I caused to dwell... these were His Clouds of Glory with which He surrounded them for shade... and as a symbol of this, He commanded us to make Sukkos that are made for shade, etc.,’" he continues (in the words of the Magen Avraham, from the Bach), "And so too, every person must

## 146

have in mind when sitting in the Sukkah that he is sitting there in order to fulfill the mitzvah of the Holy One, Blessed be He, who commanded us to sit in a Sukkah as a remembrance of the Exodus from Egypt."

For his language, "And so too, every person must have in mind, etc.," is difficult, for seemingly he should have said, "And every person must have in mind, etc." (since he did not mention before this any intent that the person must have in mind)?

Now, according to the words of the later authorities mentioned above (paragraph A) that according to the author one must have in mind that the mitzvah of Sukkah is a remembrance of the miracle that "in Sukkos I caused to dwell," with which He surrounded them with Clouds of Glory, etc., seemingly it can be said that this is the intent of the Alter Rebbe, that at first he brought the words of the (Tur and) Shulchan Aruch that the Sukkos are a remembrance of the Clouds of Glory [and in particular, that (a) the Alter Rebbe copies also the words "so that your generations may know," (b) he copies the continuation of the language of the Tur, "and as a symbol of this, He commanded us to make Sukkos... so that we may remember His wonders and His awe-inspiring deeds," and this means that the person must have this in mind when sitting in the Sukkah], and afterwards he adds, "And so too, every person must have in mind when sitting in the Sukkah [another intent, that is] that he is sitting there in order to fulfill the mitzvah of the Holy One, Blessed be He... as a remembrance of the Exodus from Egypt."

But it is forced to explain the language of the Alter Rebbe this way, for his language is clear language, and if his intent were that the person must have in mind when sitting in the Sukkah (or when making the Sukkah) that it is a remembrance of the Clouds of Glory, he should have said so explicitly.

Therefore, it seems to interpret the words of the Alter Rebbe that from the fact that "‘In Sukkos you shall dwell... so that they may know... for in Sukkos I caused to dwell... these... were His Clouds of Glory, etc.,’" two matters branch out:

(a) A law regarding the manner of making the Sukkah—"and as a symbol of this, He commanded us to make Sukkos that are made for shade so that we may remember, etc.," that we are obligated "to make Sukkos that are made for shade" [that is, "for shade alone," as above], and this law is not a matter of the intent of the mitzvah (that the person must have in mind when making the Sukkah that it is a remembrance of the Clouds of Glory), but rather the very object of the Sukkah, to be "made for shade," is a remembrance of the Clouds of Glory "with which He surrounded them for shade."

(b) "And so too, every person must have in mind when sitting in the Sukkah that he is sitting there in order to fulfill the mitzvah of the Holy One, Blessed be He, who commanded us to sit in a Sukkah as a remembrance of the Exodus from Egypt"—that in addition to the law of making a Sukkah in a manner of being "made for shade" (alone), there is an obligation to have in mind when sitting in the Sukkah that it is a remembrance of the Exodus from Egypt.

G. However, one still needs clarification regarding what was explained above, that since the mitzvah of Sukkah is a remembrance of the Clouds of Glory "with which He surrounded them for shade," therefore the essence of a Sukkah is a Sukkah made for shade alone—for seemingly, the Clouds of Glory that surrounded

## 147

the Children of Israel were not for shade alone, but served for many other matters, as explained in Chazal that the cloud would level the mountains and kill the snakes, and also protected against the arrows of the enemies, so why is this detail of the Clouds of Glory "with which He surrounded them for shade" singled out, such that the remembrance of the Clouds of Glory is specifically and only in the making of Sukkos that are made for shade (alone)?

It can be said that, on the contrary, with this the question of the Bach (and others) is answered, for "how many good favors did the Omnipresent bestow upon us, that He fed us the manna and gave us the well and other kindnesses, and why did He command more in the mitzvah of Sukkah to remember the kindness of dwelling in Sukkos more than other kindnesses?" And it is explained in the commentaries "that the well and the manna were... a necessary thing, without which it was impossible to exist at all," and therefore "a hint was not established for them, but the Clouds of Glory, which were not so necessary as they were, but only so that they would not be in the heat by day and the cold by night, the days of the festival were established as a hint to their matter."

And similarly, it is regarding the matter of the details of the things for which the Clouds of Glory were used, that the matters of protection in them (such as the protection from enemies and the killing of snakes and scorpions) that were related to life itself, we do not make a remembrance for them [and even the fact that they leveled the mountains (lowering the high, etc.), since this belongs to the very essence of their walking in the desert, it can be said that this is like a necessary matter, so that they could reach the Land of Israel], but the remembrance of the festival of Sukkos was established from the outset only on this detail "with which He surrounded them for shade so that the heat and the sun would not strike them," which is a kindness and extra affection so that their walking in the desert would be in a manner that had no suffering at all.

Based on this, the question of the Re'em (which was brought in the Bach here) is also simply answered: "Why did the Torah not command that they make in the Sukkah one [wall] above and one below and four from the four sides,

## 148

similar to the Clouds of Glory [and other commentators added to the question, that in a Sukkah one does not even need four walls, but two walls and the third a *tefach* are enough]?"

Some have answered "that the question from the outset is not a question, since the Sukkah is only a symbol in general so that we may remember His wonders and His awe-inspiring deeds [as the language of the Tur, 'and as a symbol of this, He commanded us to make Sukkos so that we may remember His wonders and His awe-inspiring deeds'] that He did for our ancestors in the surrounding of the clouds, therefore in a symbol and a remembrance, any amount is sufficient, etc."

And according to the above, perhaps it can be said that this is not just "a symbol and a remembrance of any amount," but on the contrary, this is exactly [the point]. For since the entire remembrance is only on this detail of the Clouds of Glory "with which He surrounded them for shade," therefore it is sufficient "to make Sukkos that are made for shade," for which two walls and a *tefach* with *sechach* above are enough.

(From the Sichos of the second day of Chol Hamoed Sukkos and Simchas Torah 5726)


## ל"ג בעומר

## 149

### Lag B'Omer

1. It is a well-known Gemara story that "Rabbi Akiva had twelve thousand pairs of students... and they all died in one period (between Pesach and Shavuos) because they did not treat one another with respect," and on Lag B'Omer, "they ceased dying".

Seemingly, this account requires explanation:

It is a simple matter that all these students—in the great multitude of twenty-four thousand—did not come and become his students in a single day (or period), but rather over the course of many years. That is to say, as Rabbi Akiva began to teach and his name and Torah became known throughout the world, students began to flock to him, and over time their number grew until he had twenty-four thousand students.

Likewise, it is understood that the manner of conduct of all the students did not change all at once to a state of "not treating one another with respect," but rather from level to level, and so on.

It is therefore most puzzling: Why were they all punished in "one period"? It is extremely forced to say that in this short span of "one period" (between Pesach and Shavuos), the measure of all twenty-four thousand students was filled simultaneously to the point of being punished with death.

We must therefore say that during this specific period, something unique occurred because of which "they all died" in this "period."

2. It can be suggested regarding this—by first explaining the general matter of the students of Rabbi Akiva "not treating one another with respect"—that on the face of it, it is puzzling:

After all, the teacher of these students, Rabbi Akiva, is the Tanna who emphasized the immense greatness of Ahavas Yisroel: "'Love your neighbor as yourself'—Rabbi Akiva says, this is a great principle of the Torah". If so, it is very strange that it was precisely his students who "did not treat one another with respect"—the opposite of the matter of Ahavas Yisroel (a primary and fundamental instruction in the Torah of their teacher).

It has been explained elsewhere that, on the contrary—it was precisely the fact that they were students of Rabbi Akiva that caused them "not to treat one another with respect." The explanation is:

Chazal have said that the minds of people are not alike. Naturally, this was also the case regarding the twenty-four thousand students of Rabbi Akiva, as each one of them grasped the Torah of Rabbi Akiva according to his own path and understanding, and in his own judgment, his conclusion was that surely, as he had grasped it...


## בהר

## Sicha 1

### 154

A. The Mishnah at the end of Maseches Sheviis states: "All movable property is acquired through *meshichah* (pulling), and regarding anyone who fulfills his word, the Sages are pleased with him." This means that although according to the strict letter of the law the seller may retract, since the *meshichah* has not yet been performed, nevertheless, when he does not retract but rather fulfills his word, the Sages are pleased with him.

In the Yerushalmi there, a dispute is brought between Rabbi Yochanan and Rav. According to Rabbi Yochanan, "If one said he would give a gift to his fellow and then sought to retract, he may retract." Rav disagrees, saying, "When I tell the members of my household to give a gift to a person, I do not retract," as he fulfills his word to give the gift.

Further on in the *sugya*, the Yerushalmi raises a difficulty from an incident involving Rav: "A person deposited a *aravon* (earnest money) for salt (he made a transaction with someone for salt and gave him a pledge for the purchase), and the price rose (the salt became more expensive, and the seller wanted to retract). He came before Rav, who said to him: 'Either give him all of his *aravon* (give him the amount of salt equivalent to the value of his pledge), or (if he does not want to proceed with the sale) he must submit to *mi shepara* (since he received partial payment, he is at least obligated to accept the curse of *mi shepara*).' The Yerushalmi asks: "This contradicts Rav’s position! There, he said, 'When I tell the members of my household to give a gift to a person, I do not retract' (the recipient acquires it through the speech alone), and here he says this" (that even a seller who received a pledge for part of the purchase can retract).

The Yerushalmi resolves this: "There, you learned the *din* (law), and what Rav practiced was *midas chassidus* (a measure of piety)." (In this incident, it was according to the strict law, as he ruled according to the law; and what Rav practiced himself was according to *midas chassidus*, for even though according to the law the recipient had not acquired it, he acted *lifnim mishuras hadin*—beyond the line of the law—and practiced *midas chassidus*.)

The commentators raise a difficulty from another incident involving Rav (in Yerushalmi Maseches Bava Metzia): "Rav would instruct his servant: 'When I tell you to give a gift to a person, if he is poor, give it to him immediately; but if he is wealthy, consult with me a second time' (because I might retract)."

There is a contradiction here in Rav’s conduct: here (at the end of Maseches Sheviis) it is explicit that Rav acted according to *midas chassidus* and did not retract his words, whereas in Bava Metzia he commanded his servant not to rely on his statement and not to give it until he asked him a second time—the opposite of the conduct of not changing one's word.

## 156

...fulfills his word even though he was not obligated to do so by law. According to this reasoning, there is no difference whether the recipient is wealthy, or if it is a large gift (and the recipient’s mind was not fully settled), or if it was not in the presence of the recipient, etc.—for in any case, this is a change in his word, as he is not fulfilling what he said. And one cannot say, based on the greatness of Rav’s *midas chassidus* (for Rav specifically practiced the "ten measures of *chassidus*"), that he did not fulfill his word in its details.

Even though in the Bavli it is stated that Rav disagrees with Rabbi Yochanan and holds that "words" do not fall under the category of *mechusrei amanah* (those who lack reliability), that too refers to the law, not to Rav’s own specific conduct. This is unlike the Yerushalmi in Bava Metzia, which deals with the conduct of Rav himself, just as in the case in Sheviis.

C. It seems this can be explained by first noting the precision in the difference of language between the two Yerushalmi *sugyos*: at the end of Sheviis, Rav’s language is "When I tell the members of my household," and in Bava Metzia the language is "Rav would instruct his servant."

[One cannot say that because this conduct was *midas chassidus*, he wanted to show and educate his household in *midas chassidus*, unlike his servant, upon whom his education is not incumbent—for even when he "instructed his servant," it refers to conduct in his capacity as Rav’s agent.]

It seems to me that the difference is that when speaking to his household, Rav was dealing with the private money and property of Rav and his household. Conversely, what he said to his servant specifically (as it is not appropriate to say to his household) refers not to the private property of Rav and his household, but to property found in his possession in his capacity as the Rav of the city. Many types of property are found in his possession and under his responsibility (even if they are not his), such as: the communal fund, or money (of an individual or the public) entrusted to him to distribute or to be appointed to distribute for *tzedakah*, and the like.

It is understood that regarding this property in his possession (which is not his), Rav was accustomed to instructing his servant (the *shamesh* of the *beis din*), and the like, whereas regarding property (matters) in his home, Rav would instruct his household, including the servant and the *shamesh* in his home.

Based on this, the difference between the incident of Rav in Yerushalmi Sheviis and the incident in Yerushalmi Bava Metzia is understood:

Rav’s conduct regarding his private property (about which he instructed his household) was according to *midas chassidus*, not to change his word (even when it involved financial loss, such as with a gift, or a sale when the market price rose, and the like). But regarding property that is not his, but rather property of the community (or an individual) that is in his possession, there is no place to be stringent upon himself to act with...

## 158

...movable property is acquired through *meshichah* in the public domain or in a courtyard that does not belong to both, etc.; in the seller’s domain, he does not acquire it until he lifts it or until he pulls the entire thing from the owner’s domain, etc." [Seemingly, what is the connection between Rav’s words and the *baraita*? Rav said he would not retract his word (so as not to be a *mechusrei amanah*, or so that the Sages would be pleased with him, etc.), not regarding the acquisition of the object, whereas the *baraita* discusses the law of acquisition, "he does not acquire it until he lifts it, etc."? This proves] that the Yerushalmi interprets Rav’s words as meaning that he holds that there is an acquisition in this, and therefore he does not retract, as explained by the commentators. This is what it asks: "If regarding a seller and buyer they said that the buyer does not acquire it until he lifts it or until he removes it from the owner’s domain, all the more so regarding a gift recipient who does not acquire it through speech alone—why does Rav say 'I do not retract'?"

[And it is simple according to the resolution in the Yerushalmi there, "Here, where they stood with him; here, where they did not stand with him," as explained by the commentators, that "Rav applies this *baraita* to where the buyer did not stand with him, therefore he did not acquire it until he acquires it through the methods of acquisition; and this of Rav himself is where he stood with the recipient at the time he said he would give it to him and showed him what it was." Since "he stood with him," he acquired it and he does not retract.]

Now, something similar can be said regarding the conclusion of the Yerushalmi: "There, you learned the *din*, and what Rav practiced was *midas chassidus*." This means that the fact that Rav practiced *midas chassidus* himself, not retracting, is not in a different category from what he initially thought (in the Yerushalmi) regarding Rav’s opinion (that there is an acquisition in his speech). That is, Rav’s *midas chassidus* (not to retract his word) does not mean that he did not retract his word later, but rather that because of his *midas chassidus*, his word itself was with force, that he had finalized and transferred ownership.

Based on this, it is understood that all of this can be said only regarding his statement to his household, for regarding his private objects and property, his speech has the force of an acquisition in the gift he said he would give to his fellow. But when it concerns the things of another or of the community, which are only in his possession to instruct, etc., as in the case in Bava Metzia where he instructs his servant, then an acquisition (or even an obligation) does not take effect on the body of the gift [and consequently, Rav’s "midas chassidus" to stand by his word does not apply here *ab initio*, and if his word is not fulfilled later due to the owners of the property, there is no deficiency in his midas chassidus].

Therefore, "Rav would instruct his servant: 'When I tell you to give a gift to a person, if he is poor, give it to him immediately; but if he is wealthy, consult with me a second time.'" The intention is not that regarding a poor person a vow is created and therefore he cannot retract, but the opposite (as is the simple meaning of the continuation in the Yerushalmi there, which brings this of Rav in continuation to what was said before, that he can retract)—to emphasize the difference regarding the statement to his household: here, the acquisition (and the like) does not occur through the statement and the speech, where by his speech alone he finalized and transferred (or obligated) as in his statement to his household, but rather it depends on who the recipient is. Therefore, when the recipient is poor, one must give to him immediately, since he knows that the mind of the owner of the property (or the community) is also to give, and there is no room for further clarification, as above.

## 159

E. All matters of Torah are precise, and it is understood from this that the fact that the conclusion of Maseches Sheviis is (not only in matters of *Sheviis*, but) in the law regarding monetary matters and the like—"And regarding anyone who fulfills his word, the Sages are pleased with him"—and in this itself the Gemara (Yerushalmi) adds regarding Rav’s conduct in this matter in the manner of *midas chassidus*—that this conduct of "anyone who fulfills his word" (and in the manner of *midas chassidus*) belongs to the general content of the matter of "Sheviis."

This can be explained according to *drush* (in the *avodah* of man), by first noting the statement of our Sages on the verse "And the faith of your times shall be," that "faith" is the Order of *Zeraim* (Seeds), and "it is explained in the Yerushalmi that he believes in the Living One of the worlds and sows."

It has been explained elsewhere that although the matter of sowing is in the nature and conduct of the world, creation, and even a non-Jew who does not believe in the Living One of the worlds sows and relies on his sowing, nevertheless, a Jewish person who sows does not do so because that is the way of the world—like the conduct of his non-Jewish neighbor—and not even because he has practiced it himself many times, that he has already sown many times and each time the sowing brought growth, etc.—but solely because he "believes in the Living One of the worlds and (only therefore) sows" (now).

And more deeply: The novelty in this, that a Jewish person relies on Hashem (the "Living One of the worlds") when he sows, can be in two ways:
1) That he is certain that the sowing will lead to growth (as the nature of the world) because he relies on Hashem, who created the world and established the laws of nature in the manner of "seed and harvest... shall not cease," and only because of this is he certain that his sowing will succeed and grow. In this way, the ways of nature occupy a place for him, but their importance is not from themselves, but only because Hashem established these laws in creation.
2) "That he believes in the Living One of the worlds," that the ways of nature do not occupy a place for him at all, and he does not rely on them at all (because he feels that Hashem is the Creator of nature at every moment *me'ayin l'yesh* and the laws of nature have no existence at all)—but he sows solely because of his faith in Hashem, that since Hashem commanded him to sow, he is confident and certain that He, blessed be He, will make his sowing successful so that it will grow. This is the precision of the Sages' language, "that he believes in the Living One of the worlds," for if it were only because he relies on Hashem, the leader of nature, there would be no need now for faith (which is above intellect), since in the combination of his father, etc., this is more than a hundred times (and one); but since for him the ways of nature do not occupy a place at all, and he relies only on Hashem, that He, blessed be He, will make his sowing successful—for this, faith that is above intellect is needed.

F. Based on this, the greatness of the matter of the *mitzvah* of *Sheviis* will be understood and sweetened. Simply put, the root of this *mitzvah* is to instill and imprint in man the recognition that the growth in the land depends solely on Hashem, which is why Hashem commanded, "And the land shall keep a Sabbath to Hashem," in a way that man relies solely on Hashem. Therefore, even though "we do not sow and we do not gather our produce," "I will command My blessing for you, etc."

It can be said that the intention of this *mitzvah* is not only for one who has not yet reached the level of "believing in the Living One of the worlds and sowing" and therefore needs the *mitzvah* of *Sheviis* to instill this recognition in him, but even one who sowed his field during the six years in the manner of "believing in the Living One of the worlds and sowing" (as above)—nevertheless, for him too, the *mitzvah* of *Sheviis* effects a fundamental innovation.

The explanation for this:
During the six years, although man knows that the laws of nature and the act of his sowing are not the true cause of the growth of the grain and the fruits, nevertheless, since in practice there is the action of man, and Hashem’s blessing comes to him through his action—"And Hashem your G-d will bless you in all that you do"—it is not clearly evident to the eyes of flesh his faith in the Living One of the worlds.
And specifically in the seventh year, when he does not sow, etc., and there is no action or deed of man, and nevertheless he is occupied with the study of Torah in rest without any worry, this indicates his faith in the Living One of the worlds.

G. And according to all the above, the connection of *Sheviis* to the conduct of "fulfilling one's word" (up to the ultimate perfection in this in the manner of *midas chassidus*, and up to the conduct of Rav) is understood:
The ways of acquisition exist also among the nations of the world, that when a person makes an acquisition (according to the ways of acquisition in every country, practiced in every place) the thing passes into his ownership. But for a Jewish person, the force in the act of his acquisitions is not because it is accepted in the ways of the world, but because Hashem commanded so in His Torah, and gave us the laws of acquisitions, as it is written, "When you sell, etc., or buy from the hand of your fellow," and the like.

The Mishnah at the end of *Sheviis* adds that there is conduct even more elevated, that "anyone who fulfills his word," even though there was no act of acquisition here. And if so, not only according to the ways of the world is the object not acquired, but also according to the Torah according to the strict letter of the law (which gave force to the ways of acquisition) seemingly there is no acquisition here—nevertheless, because of the greatness of his *midas chassidus* (which comes from the general command of the Torah to stand by one's word), since he obligated himself by his word, it is considered for him that the thing is already acquired, and he fulfills his word.

May it be His will that through the study of Maseches *Sheviis* and up to its completion and conclusion, we will merit soon to fulfill this *mitzvah* in practice, that the prophecy will be fulfilled, "You have desired, Hashem, Your land ('perform the *Shemitos*'), the Sabbath of the rest of Jacob," with the coming of *Mashiach Tzidkeinu*, who will lead us upright to our land in this year—the year of "you shall release your hand"—literally.

(From the *sichos* of 6 Tishrei and *Shabbos Parshas Haazinu* 5747)

## Sicha 2

1. At the end of our Parsha, the laws of a Hebrew servant sold to a fellow Jew are discussed—"If your brother becomes impoverished and is sold to you"—which contains various warnings: "You shall not work him with the labor of a slave; he shall be with you as a hired laborer, as a resident; he shall go out from you... and return to his family". Subsequently, the laws of a Hebrew servant sold to a non-Jew are presented—"If the hand of a stranger... attains [wealth] and your brother becomes impoverished... and is sold to a resident stranger with you or to the root of a stranger’s family" (which refers to being "sold to idol worship itself, to be its servant... to chop wood, etc."), which includes various specific laws regarding his redemption : "After he is sold, there shall be a redemption for him... and if he is not redeemed by these, he shall go out in the Yovel year."

In both cases, the same reason is stated in the verse: "For they are My servants, whom I brought out of the land of Egypt", and "For the Children of Israel are servants to Me; they are My servants, whom I brought out of the land of Egypt". This means that this concept ("They are My servants") serves as the rationale both for the laws of a Hebrew servant sold to a Jew and for the laws of a Jew sold to a non-Jew.

Now, we find that Rashi repeats the explanation of this reason; for both verses, he explains: "They are My servants—My bill of sale takes precedence." Seemingly, according to the well-known rule that Rashi relies upon what he has already written in his commentary—especially since the two verses are in close proximity to one another—it requires clarification: why did Rashi repeat his explanation?

[Furthermore: In the Toras Kohanim (the source for Rashi, seemingly), it only explains the first verse, "For they are My servants"—"My bill of sale takes precedence (over them first)" ; whereas for the second verse, "For the Children of Israel are servants to Me," it does not repeat "My bill of sale takes precedence". Now, in the Toras Kohanim, which is a system of study according to Halacha and Midrash, we do find that various expositions and teachings are repeated ; however, Rashi, who "came only to explain the literal meaning of Scripture," as has been discussed many times, relies on what he has explained previously.]

Therefore, we must say that the reason of "My bill of sale takes precedence" regarding the laws of a Jew sold to a non-Jew is a different matter (and a novelty) compared to the reason for the laws of a Jew sold to a Jew. Consequently, Rashi found it necessary to repeat and inform us that the fact that "My bill of sale takes precedence" is (also) the reason for the laws of a Jew sold to a non-Jew.

2. On the face of it, this can be explained simply:
Regarding one who sells himself to a non-Jew, where the "cause" for this is that "he became attached to him (the non-Jew) because he learned from his deeds", to the point that he sold himself to idol worship—


## בחוקותי

## Sicha 1

### 168

A. "Then I will remember My covenant with Yaakov, and also My covenant with Yitzchak, and also My covenant with Avraham I will remember...". Rashi comments: "Why are [the Patriarchs] listed in reverse order? This is to say: Yaakov the youngest is worthy of this; if he is not worthy, then Yitzchak is with him; and if he is not worthy, then Avraham is with him, for he is worthy".

Seemingly, the source for this is the Toras Kohanim on this verse, which states: "Why are the Patriarchs mentioned in reverse order? It is because if the deeds of Avraham are not sufficient, then the deeds of Yitzchak [are], and if the deeds of Yitzchak are not sufficient, then the deeds of Yaakov [are]. It is sufficient for each one that the world should be sustained on his account."

However, Rashi’s commentary differs from the teaching of the Toras Kohanim in several details. Furthermore, his interpretation is the opposite of the Toras Kohanim’s: The Toras Kohanim begins with Avraham and continues, "If the deeds of Avraham are not sufficient, then the deeds of Yitzchak," concluding with Yaakov. Rashi, by contrast, writes in the reverse order: "Yaakov is worthy... and if he is not worthy, then Yitzchak, etc., then Avraham."

The reason for this change is easily understood: Rashi is interpreting the plain meaning of the text (*peshuto shel mikra*). The verse places "My covenant with Yaakov" before "My covenant with Yitzchak... My covenant with Avraham." From this, it is understood that this is also the order of the remembrance of the covenant of the Patriarchs —"Yaakov is worthy... and if he is not worthy, then Yitzchak is with him ('and also My covenant with Yitzchak'), etc., Avraham is with him ('and also My covenant with Avraham')."

But we must explain the reason for this change—that according to the Toras Kohanim, the merit of Yaakov is greater than the merit of Yitzchak, and that of Yitzchak is greater than that of Avraham, which is why "if the deeds of Avraham are not sufficient, then the deeds of Yitzchak (who is greater), and if the deeds of Yitzchak are not sufficient, then

## 169

the deeds of Yaakov" (as above). Conversely, according to Rashi, who begins with Yaakov and ends with Avraham, it is understood that his view is the opposite: that the merit of Avraham is greater than that of Yitzchak, and that of Yitzchak is greater than that of Yaakov.

There is another change: The Toras Kohanim concludes, "It is sufficient for each one that [the world] should be sustained... on his account," meaning that each of the Patriarchs individually is sufficient [though when the situation is at its worst, and a greater merit is needed and the "deeds of Avraham" are insufficient, one relies on the merit of the "deeds of Yitzchak," or more than that, the "deeds of Yaakov"]. Rashi, by contrast, is precise in his wording: "If he is not worthy, then Yitzchak is with him... Avraham is with him" —meaning that the merit of each one individually is not sufficient, but rather they are joined together, until they are all together.

[Simply put, the reason Rashi changed this detail from the Toras Kohanim is that the verse states, "And also My covenant with Yitzchak, and also My covenant with Avraham I will remember," which explicitly means in the plain sense of the text that the intention is not to remember each of the Patriarchs individually, but to join them together. Therefore, Rashi emphasizes that he comes to explain the plain meaning of the text: "Yitzchak is with him... Avraham is with him."

However, we must explain the reason for these two approaches—that according to the Toras Kohanim, the merit of each of the Patriarchs individually is sufficient, whereas according to the plain meaning of the text, the remembrance of the covenant of the Patriarchs of Yitzchak is specifically in the manner of "with him," in combination with Yaakov, and likewise regarding Avraham.]

B. Some commentators suggest that these two changes are interdependent—that even for Rashi, Yaakov is the "choicest of the Patriarchs" and his merit is greater than that of Avraham and Yitzchak. Therefore, Rashi writes "Yitzchak is with him... Avraham is with him," because it is not his view (in the plain meaning of the text) that "if (Yaakov) is not worthy," the merit of Yitzchak alone is sufficient (on the contrary—the merit of Yaakov is greater than that of Yitzchak). Rather, one then joins the merit

## 170

of Yitzchak with Yaakov ("Yitzchak is with him"), and likewise regarding Avraham.

However, [in addition to the fact that the logic of these positions still requires study—that according to the Toras Kohanim, the text emphasizes the merit of each of the Patriarchs individually ("It is sufficient for each one that the world should be sustained on his account"), while according to Rashi, their combination is emphasized ("Yitzchak is with him... Avraham is with him")—] Rashi explicitly writes, "Yaakov the youngest is worthy of this." Simply put, he means that although Yaakov is "youngest" in comparison to the other Patriarchs, nevertheless, "Yaakov the youngest is worthy of this" (though "if he is not worthy, then Yitzchak, etc., and if he is not worthy, then Avraham is with him, for he is worthy"). This means that Rashi does not hold (like the Toras Kohanim) that the merit of Yaakov is greater than that of Yitzchak and Avraham; rather, Yaakov is "younger" than them, and Avraham is the greatest of all.

[Simply put, one can say that Rashi’s words here, that Yaakov is "younger" in comparison to Avraham and Yitzchak, follow and are based upon his commentary above (in Parshas Tisa) on the verse "Remember Avraham, Yitzchak, and Yisrael" (after the sin of the Golden Calf): "If they are to be burned, remember Avraham who surrendered himself to be burned for You in Ur Kasdim; if they are to be killed, remember Yitzchak who bared his neck for the Akeidah; if they are to be exiled, remember Yaakov who went into exile to Charan." From this it is understood that the merit of Avraham is greater than that of Yitzchak (and Yaakov), since he "surrendered himself to be burned for You," and below that is the merit of Yitzchak "who bared his neck for the Akeidah", and below them is the merit of Yaakov, in whom we do not find an actual surrender of life, but only the matter of exile.]

From all the above, it is understood that there are two ways of viewing the relationship of the Patriarchs to one another: According to one way, whoever precedes the other is greater than him; the merit of Avraham is greater than the merit of Yitzchak, and the merit of Yitzchak is greater than Yaakov’s. According to the second way, whoever is later is greater; Yaakov is the choicest of all the Patriarchs, and Yitzchak is more select than Avraham. The difference between the Toras Kohanim and Rashi lies in the interpretation of these verses: what is the deciding factor in the remembrance of the covenant of the Patriarchs—a matter in which Yaakov is the "youngest," or a matter in which Yaakov is the essence (the choicest of the Patriarchs).

On this also depends the second change—for in the matter where Yaakov is greater than Yitzchak and Avraham, there is merit in each and every one of the Patriarchs individually (regarding the remembrance of the covenant), "It is sufficient for each one that the world should be sustained on his account." But in the matter where Avraham is greater than Yitzchak and Yitzchak is greater than Yaakov, there is a need to join them together (for this remembrance), to combine the virtue of Avraham and Yitzchak with the virtue of Yaakov (even though Yaakov is the "youngest" among them).

C. One can explain this based on another change in Rashi’s commentary compared to the Toras Kohanim: In the Toras Kohanim, the wording is "If the deeds of Avraham are not sufficient, the deeds of Yitzchak, etc., the deeds of Yaakov." Rashi, however, writes "Yaakov is worthy, etc., Yitzchak is with him, etc., Avraham... who is worthy," omitting the word "deeds."

This means that for the Toras Kohanim, the (primary) emphasis here (in the remembrance of the covenant of the Patriarchs) is the merit within the "deeds" of the Patriarchs (and in this, the virtue of Yaakov is greater than the other Patriarchs). For Rashi, however, the (primary) emphasis in this remembrance is the merit of Avraham, Yitzchak, and Yaakov from their own perspective—that is, the very fact that they are "Patriarchs" (and in this, the primary virtue is in Avraham, though for this merit to protect his children after him, it (perhaps) needs to be joined with Yitzchak and also with Yaakov, even though he is "Yaakov the youngest").

This will be understood by first noting a general difference between the Toras Kohanim and Rashi regarding these verses :

These verses (at the end of the rebuke) explain the order of the end of the exile and the order of the redemption: "And they shall confess their iniquity... then their uncircumcised heart will be humbled, and then they will accept their iniquity, and I will remember My covenant, etc." Now, the Toras Kohanim explains "And they shall confess their iniquity"—"these words refer to the side of teshuvah," and "then their uncircumcised heart will be humbled"—"these words refer to the side of teshuvah." This means that according to the Toras Kohanim, there is an act of teshuvah here. But in Rashi, the matter of teshuvah is not mentioned here. On the contrary, regarding the words "and then they will accept their iniquity," Rashi explains—"they will atone for their iniquity through their sufferings", for the acceptance and atonement for the iniquities come (not because they did teshuvah, but) through the sufferings.

This is a fundamental difference between the interpretation of the Toras Kohanim and Rashi regarding the reason the redemption will come (which the verse speaks of): According to the Toras Kohanim, it will come because of the merits of Yisrael, for since they did teshuvah for their iniquities and their iniquity was accepted, they are therefore worthy and have merited the redemption. Conversely, according to Rashi, the atonement is not because of the teshuvah and merits of Yisrael, but rather the Holy One, Blessed be He, brings sufferings upon them, and through this their iniquity is atoned for. In the words of the verse—which Rashi brings here —"And that which comes into your mind... surely with a mighty hand, etc.", the Holy One, Blessed be He, brings Yisrael back "under My wings" in a manner of a "mighty hand" (and not of their own volition).

Just as they differ on the cause of the acceptance of iniquities that leads to redemption (whether it is because of teshuvah or because of sufferings), so too do they differ on the essence of the remembrance of the covenant of the Patriarchs and its effect regarding the redemption.

For the fact that there is a need for the remembrance of the covenant of the Patriarchs so that Bnei Yisrael may return to their land, even though their iniquity has already been accepted, is understood as the Ohr HaChaim explains: "The confession is effective to lighten the evil decrees upon them, but to return them to their place (to Eretz Yisrael), the merit of the Patriarchs is needed." But the Toras Kohanim and Rashi differ on the essence of this remembrance and its effect:

According to the Toras Kohanim, since the acceptance of iniquities that brings the redemption comes through the teshuvah of Yisrael—meaning that Yisrael themselves are worthy of redemption—it can be said that the need for the remembrance of the covenant of the Patriarchs is because teshuvah (only) cancels the evil (their iniquity). But in order for them to be worthy of redemption (which is through "And I will turn to you," "And I will set My tabernacle among you... and I will walk among you"), they also need merits [and it can be said that this is similar to what we find regarding the redemption from Egypt, for since "they had no mitzvos to occupy themselves with in order to be redeemed... He gave them two mitzvos, the blood of the Pesach and the blood of the milah"].

This is the matter of the remembrance of the covenant of the Patriarchs: that through and because of the making of this covenant, the merit of the good deeds of the Patriarchs stands for Bnei Yisrael until the end of all generations [for this is the content of the covenant made between the Holy One, Blessed be He, and the Patriarchs, that it is not a covenant (only) for them, but because they are the Patriarchs of Bnei Yisrael, the covenant with the Patriarchs is inherently a covenant with all Bnei Yisrael, their seed after them, until the end of all generations]. This is considered as if they have good deeds and are worthy of redemption.

Conversely, according to Rashi, since the acceptance of iniquity that brings the redemption comes through the Holy One, Blessed be He, "bringing them back under My wings... with a mighty hand" (by bringing upon them sufferings that atone for their iniquities), it can be said that just as the acceptance of iniquity does not depend on the deeds of Yisrael, so too the merit of the redemption does not depend (so much) on the virtue in the deeds. Rather, it (also) is a result of the act of the Holy One, Blessed be He, with the Patriarchs. Since the Holy One, Blessed be He, made a covenant with the Patriarchs that He would redeem their children, therefore, it makes no difference (so much) whether they are worthy of redemption; ultimately, He redeems them from slavery to freedom [though as long as their iniquity is not accepted, this is a contradiction to the covenant, and it is impossible for them to be redeemed, but after their iniquity is accepted through sufferings, they are then redeemed through the remembrance of the covenant of the Patriarchs].

Therefore, the merit of the deeds of the Patriarchs is not relevant here, but rather the covenant that the Holy One, Blessed be He, made with Avraham, Yitzchak, and Yaakov as Patriarchs. Since He made a covenant with the Patriarchs, the state of the children makes no difference, and the Holy One, Blessed be He, fulfills His promise in any situation.

D. Based on all the above, the other differences (mentioned above) between the Toras Kohanim and Rashi on the verse "And I will remember, etc." will also be explained [whether Yaakov is the choicest of the Patriarchs or whether Yaakov is the "youngest," and also whether they need to be joined together until they are all together]:

The fact that the Holy One, Blessed be He, established that Avraham, Yitzchak, and Yaakov are the Patriarchs of the nation of Yisrael, whose children after them are the chosen nation to whom the Torah was given, etc.—this is because of their surrender of life and their attachment to Hashem. In this, the virtue of Avraham is greater than the other Patriarchs, for even though he was born to Terach and grew up among idolaters, etc., he recognized his Creator through his own service, to the point that he surrendered himself to be burned for the sanctification of His Name, blessed be He, through which he became the father of the nation.

On the other hand, there is a virtue in Yaakov, that precisely because he was the son of Yitzchak and the grandson of Avraham, therefore, even though he did not need the surrender of life of Avraham (and Yitzchak), nevertheless, because he was born and raised by Avraham and Yitzchak, his deeds and his service were in the ultimate holiness. Simply put, from his youth he was a "dweller of tents," "the tent of Shem and the tent of Ever", to the point that there was no blemish in his seed (even though from Avraham came Yishmael, and from Yitzchak came Esav). Therefore, he is called the "choicest of the Patriarchs."

This is the content of the difference between the Toras Kohanim and Rashi:

In the Toras Kohanim, where the emphasis is on the deeds of the Patriarchs, there is a virtue in the merit of the deeds of Yaakov over the deeds of Yitzchak and the deeds of Avraham, because the deeds of Yaakov were in the ultimate holiness, as above. Conversely, in Rashi, where the emphasis is on the Patriarchs themselves, as Patriarchs, Yaakov is the "youngest," and therefore Rashi begins with Yaakov and ends with Avraham, "who is worthy."

But on the other hand, precisely because of this, there is a need to join the merit of Avraham and Yitzchak to Yaakov ("Yitzchak is with him... Avraham is with him")—for the fact that Avraham is the "father" of his children after him (and likewise Yitzchak) could lead to influence also upon (the children of) Yishmael and (the children of) Esav, for they too came from (the seed of) Avraham and Yitzchak.

This is why Rashi is precise: "If he is not worthy, then Yitzchak is with him... Avraham is with him," for in order for the merit of the Patriarchs to stand specifically for Bnei Yisrael, this is done by joining Avraham and Yitzchak to Yaakov, at which point the remembrance of the covenant of Avraham and Yitzchak pertains solely and exclusively to Bnei Yisrael.

(From a Sicha of Shabbos Parshas Bechukosai, 5728)

## Sicha 2

### 174

### Sicha 2

A. The Rambam writes at the end of Hilchos Temurah—which is the conclusion of Sefer Korbanos —as follows:
"Even though all the statutes of the Torah are decrees, as we explained at the end of Hilchos Me'ilah [where he writes: 'The *mishpatim* are the mitzvos whose reason is revealed and the benefit of their performance in this world is known... The *chukim* are the mitzvos whose reason is not known... *Chukim chakati lach*, etc.'], it is fitting to contemplate them. And whatever reason you are able to provide for them, provide a reason. Indeed, the early Sages have said that King Solomon understood most of the reasons for all the statutes of the Torah" (and he subsequently proceeds to explain a "reason" for the laws of Temurah, as will be discussed in section D).

In other words: In addition to what is implied by the Rambam’s words in Hilchos Me'ilah—that in essence there are reasons even for *chukim*, only that "their reason is not known" 
[This is in accordance with his position in his book, Moreh Nevuchim, where he discusses at length the reasons for the mitzvos, stating that "every command and prohibition... is a product of wisdom, and its goal is a specific purpose (and not merely 'a product of absolute will,' 'a simple will without seeking a purpose')... All the mitzvos... have a reason, meaning that the command or prohibition has a beneficial purpose". The Ramban writes similarly in his commentary on the Torah]—
the Rambam here introduces an additional point: that there is an obligation upon a person to "contemplate" the statutes of the Torah, and "whatever reason you are able to provide for them, provide a reason".

B. This requires clarification:
It is explicitly stated in the Rambam’s Shemonah Perakim that the statement of our Sages, "A person should not say, 'I do not want (to commit a sin)... rather, I want to, but what can I do? My Father in Heaven has decreed upon me,'" applies only to *chukim*, "for were it not for the Torah, they would not be evils

### 175

at all." Therefore, abstaining from them is only because of the Torah’s command: "My Father in Heaven has decreed upon me." This is not the case with *mishpatim*, which are "matters that are well-known to all people as evils." Regarding these, one must say, "I do not want," because "a refined soul will not desire any of these evils." It is explicitly stated here that there is a distinction between *chukim* and *mishpatim* even in the manner of their fulfillment: regarding *mishpatim*, one is obligated to say, "I do not want," meaning that one is obligated to fulfill them because of their rational reason. Conversely, regarding *chukim*, one is obligated to say, "I want to," meaning that their fulfillment should be in a way that lacks a rational reason, existing only due to the acceptance of the yoke of His Kingship, "My Father in Heaven has decreed upon me".

The Ramban writes similarly in his commentary on the Torah (as he holds like the Rambam that all the mitzvos of the Torah have a "proper reason and complete benefit"—as mentioned in section A). He compares the matter of *chukim* to "a king’s decree which he enacts in his kingdom without revealing their benefit to the people" (even though the king himself "knows the necessity and benefit of that mitzvah... and will not tell it to the people, except to his wise advisors"). Consequently, "the people do not derive benefit from them... and they accept them (only) out of fear of the monarchy." From this, it is understood regarding the *chukim* of the Torah as well, that their fulfillment must be "out of fear of the monarchy."

Based on this, one must wonder about the Rambam’s statement here that it is "fitting to contemplate" the statutes of the Torah, and "whatever reason you are able to provide for them, provide a reason"—meaning that a person must strive to understand the benefit of the *chukim* (and that the things prohibited by the *chukim* of the Torah are evil things, etc.) [On the other hand, if a person is able to provide a reason, then his saying "I want to" is the opposite of the desired path, since "a refined soul will not desire any of these evils"]?

C. The explanation for this is that there is a difference between the reasons for *mishpatim* and the reasons for *chukim*:
The reasons for *mishpatim* are within human intellect, because these matters are necessitated by human intellect itself, as the Sages put it: "Were they not written, it would be logical that they be written." This is not the case with the reasons for *chukim*; their foundation is (only) in the intellect of the Torah (His wisdom, which is above the intellect of created beings). They are not reasons necessitated by the simple intellect of man (rather, after the reason and benefit are explained, the human intellect "agrees" and accepts that there is a benefit here, etc.).

With this, we can explain the precision of the Rambam’s language later in the halacha. After explaining the reason for the law of Temurah (and likewise the reason for adding a fifth in the redemption of *hekdesh*)—as discussed in section D—he concludes: "And most of the laws of the Torah are nothing but advice from afar from the Great Counselor to refine dispositions and straighten all deeds." It can be said that the reason the Rambam chose this phrase, "advice from afar from the Great Counselor," is not merely because he adopted the language of the verse 

### 176

for the beauty of the expression, but because this is the definition of the "reasons" in this halacha. The "reasons" that you can provide for *chukim* are "advice" that comes "from afar," "from the Great Counselor." That is to say: the reasons for *chukim* are not the "advice" (intellect) of man, which human intellect (by itself) advises a person to follow (since they are not matters necessitated by the intellect itself), but rather this is "advice" from Hashem, "from the Great Counselor" (who is "far" from man).

Therefore, one must be careful: if "one does not find a reason for it and does not know a cause—because it has not yet reached him from afar—it should not be light in his eyes, and he should not break through to ascend to Hashem, etc.".

This is why the Sages taught that a person must say (regarding *chukim*), "I want to, but what can I do? My Father in Heaven has decreed upon me"—even though "you are able to provide a reason"—because human intellect by itself (without the advice from afar) does not see the evil of the prohibited things. The fulfillment of the *chukim* is through the acceptance of the decree of the Omnipresent ("My Father in Heaven has decreed upon me"), yet the possibility was given to contemplate them and "provide a reason."

D. Based on this, it is also well-understood why the Rambam wrote this matter regarding the *chukim* of Temurah (and not regarding other *chukim* that preceded it) —because in Temurah, it is emphasized that the reason you are able to provide is only advice "from afar from the Great Counselor."

The Rambam’s words (regarding the reason for the law of Temurah) are: "It appears to me that what the verse says, 'Then it and its substitute shall be holy,' is like the matter that is said, 'And if the one who sanctifies it redeems his house, he shall add a fifth of the money of your valuation to it.' The Torah descended to the depths of human thought and a bit of his evil inclination, for it is the nature of man to increase his possessions and to spare his money. Even though he vowed and sanctified it, it is possible that he will regret it and redeem it for less than its value. The Torah said: If he redeems it for himself, he shall add a fifth. Likewise, if he sanctified an animal with *kedushas haguf*, perhaps he will regret it, and since he cannot redeem it, he will exchange it for one inferior to it. And if you give him permission to exchange the bad for the good, he will exchange the good for the bad and say, 'It is good.'"

Seemingly, this reason is not fully understood: since "one does not redeem *hekdesh* except in the presence of three experts", and furthermore, "they announce it before all who come to redeem: one says, 'They are mine for ten *selaim*,' and another says, 'For twenty,' etc., 'for fifty'" —how could it enter his mind that he would be able to redeem it "for less than its value"? After all, "all who come to redeem" will offer a higher sum, and all the more so, the "three experts" would not allow him to redeem it for less than its value ?

Likewise, it is not understood—the concern that "if you give him permission to exchange the bad for the good, he will exchange the good for the bad and say, 'It is good'"—for after seeing that he sanctified his money (thereby overcoming his inclination and nature to increase his possessions), we are nevertheless concerned not only that he might regret it, but that he will turn from one extreme to the other, to the point that he will lie and "exchange the good for the bad and say, 'It is good'"!

The explanation for this is that these reasons are "advice from afar from the Great Counselor." From the perspective of the simple intellect of man, this is not something acceptable, but "the Torah descended to the depths of human thought, etc." That is to say, Hashem, who created man and the traits of his soul, knows the nature of man to its "depths." He knows that this nature "to increase his possessions and to spare his money" has the power to sway a person until he strives to redeem it for less than its value, and until it is possible that he will turn from one extreme to the other. Instead of his previous desire to sanctify his money to Hashem, he will act in an opposite manner, until he "exchanges the good for the bad and says, 'It is good.'"

Only Hashem, the Creator of man, can provide such "advice".

E. But the matter is still not fully settled:
All of the above only answers that even after providing a reason for the *chukim*, their fulfillment depends on "fear of the monarchy" and the acceptance of the yoke of His Kingship (because the reasons that "you are able to provide" for *chukim* are not reasons necessitated by human intellect, but "advice from afar from the Great Counselor"). However, it still requires explanation: ultimately, contemplating the reasons for the *chukim* is the opposite of the concept of accepting the yoke, as is understood simply: when a person strives and yearns to find a reason and benefit in a mitzvah, this is a contradiction to the yearning to accept the mitzvah only because Hashem decreed so.

Also, it is not well-answered by the aforementioned statement of our Sages that in *chukim* a person must say "I want to"—for seemingly, since it is ultimately within the capacity of human intellect to understand that there is a benefit in the matter of the *chukim* (as the Rambam explained in his book, Moreh Nevuchim, the benefit in many *chukim*)—even though the intellect by itself does not necessitate these matters—then "a refined soul" should (at least after contemplating the reasons for the *chukim*) say "I do not want"?

F. This will be understood by prefacing the words of the Rambam in Moreh Nevuchim, where, after explaining that "all the mitzvos... have a reason... a beneficial purpose," he continues that all this is only regarding the *generality* of the mitzvah, that "the *generality* of the mitzvos have a reason absolutely, and it is for a specific benefit that He commanded them. But their details are those of which it is said that they are for the sake of the command... it is impossible to provide a reason at all" for the details of the mitzvos. That is, the details of the mitzvos are in the category of a "statute," meaning a decree without a reason.

It turns out that according to the Rambam, there is in all the mitzvos—even *mishpatim*—an aspect of a *chukah* (statute) in the literal sense, for which "it is impossible to provide a reason at all." From this, it is understood *a fortiori* regarding *chukim*, that even though the Rambam wrote that it is possible to provide them with a reason (in addition to the fact that the reason is "advice from afar from the Great Counselor"), they contain details for which "it is impossible to provide (them) a reason at all".

### 179

The distinction between *mishpatim* and *chukim* is: in *mishpatim*, the generality of the mitzvah is a matter that human intellect necessitates, and this aspect of the mitzvah overcomes and outweighs the details of the mitzvah that have no reason. This is not the case with *chukim*, where the part of the mitzvah that has no reason (whether because the generality of the mitzvah has no known reason, or because it has many details that have no reason) overcomes the part of the mitzvah for which one can provide a reason (especially since even the reason one can provide is only "advice from afar from the Great Counselor," as mentioned above).

Based on this, it is understood that in *chukim* there are two matters: the acceptance of the yoke of His Kingship and the contemplation of the reasons, and they do not contradict each other—because they are in different parts of the mitzvah: in *chukim*, there is that which "it is impossible to provide a reason at all" (and in *chukim*, this part is the one that overcomes and decides)—and the fulfillment of the mitzvah is through fear of the monarchy, the acceptance of the yoke of His Kingship [and regarding them, a person must say, "I want to, but what can I do? My Father in Heaven has decreed upon me"]; and regarding the part of the mitzvah for which "you are able to provide a reason"—the contemplation of the reason for the mitzvah (even though its main part has no reason).

G. Now, according to all the above, it is found that according to the Rambam, there is an "inclusion" in the mitzvos: in *mishpatim*, there is an aspect of a "statute" (for which it is impossible to provide a reason at all), namely the details of the mitzvah. Conversely, in *chukim*, there is a reason and benefit (and this is so in all *chukim*, as per the precision of the Rambam’s language regarding King Solomon, that he "understood most of the reasons for all the statutes of the Torah").

As for the reason for this—it can be said:
It is the nature of man, which Hashem implanted in him, that a command which has a reason (*mishpatim*)—its fulfillment is with greater vitality and *taanug*, and a command which has no reason (*chukim*)—its fulfillment is with greater acceptance of the yoke, like a statute and decree of the king.
The desired goal is that both virtues should be in all the mitzvos: that even the fulfillment of *chukim* should be with vitality and *taanug* (like *mishpatim*), and therefore there is an aspect of "reason" even in *chukim*. Conversely, even the mitzvos of *mishpatim* must be fulfilled (not only because of the rational reason, but) because of the will of Hashem, "Who has sanctified us with His mitzvos and commanded us," like *chukim*—and for this, there is no reason for the details of the mitzvos (of *mishpatim*), so that the fulfillment of *mishpatim* is also connected with the acceptance of the yoke of His Kingship and the fulfillment of the decree of Hashem.

### 180

And more deeply:
By the fact that a reason is provided even in *chukim*, this causes a person to fulfill these mitzvos with vitality and *taanug*, not only because of that part of the mitzvah for which "you are able to provide a reason," but also because of the generality of the mitzvah. That is, his acceptance of the yoke is from within vitality and *taanug*. The fulfillment of the statute is not only through his instruments of action, which he forces to do the will of Hashem, but all the powers of his soul "participate" in the performance of the mitzvah; "All my bones shall say," they fulfill His will.

Likewise, conversely, in *mishpatim*—the fact that the details of the mitzvah have no reason (and are nothing but a statute and a command of Hashem, without a reason) also acts upon the rational part of the mitzvah, so that the person recognizes that even this part of the mitzvah which he understands and grasps, "this is not... the ultimate reason and its boundary", because in truth they are (like *chukim*) "advice from afar from the Great Counselor". The true wisdom in the mitzvos of Hashem is His wisdom, which is above the intellect of created beings, as the Rambam writes that "there is no power in man to grasp and find the mind of the Creator."
